Asian Buyers Acquire London's 'Cheesegrater' Tower

By Art Patnaude 
442 words
2 March 2017
The Wall Street Journal (Asia Edition)
AWSJ
B1
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

CC Land Holdings Ltd., backed by a Hong Kong property tycoon, has agreed to pay GBP 1.15 billion ($1.42 billion) for a London skyscraper in one of the city's biggest real-estate deals.

British Land PLC and Oxford Properties Group, the real-estate arm of Canadian pension fund OMERS, said Wednesday that they sold the Leadenhall Building, known locally as the Cheesegrater for its triangular shape.

Investment in London property from China and Hong Kong has surged in recent months. A weak pound versus the yuan, in addition to economic and political pressures at home, have been driving the acquisition spree.

CC Land Holdings, controlled by Cheung Chung Kiu, bought a different London office building for GBP 292 million earlier this year.

At 736 feet, or 224 meters, the Cheesegrater is the tallest tower in London's main financial district. It was valued at GBP 915 million at the end of September.

The deal is the biggest transaction of a single London building since Qatar's sovereign-wealth fund bought the London headquarters of HSBC Holdings PLC for more than GBP 1.1 billion in 2014.

Investors from China and Hong Kong bought more than GBP 3 billion of central London property last year, more than those from the U.S. or Europe, according to real-estate broker JLL.

The influx stands out because U.K. property transaction volumes have fallen sharply in the past year. Britain's decision to leave the European Union, as well as high prices following a multiyear property boom, have made some overseas investors wary of current values.

But for Chinese investors, London property looks much different.

Currency has been a big factor. While the yuan has lost value against the dollar, it is up 12% versus sterling since the Brexit vote in June, making assets in the U.K. a bargain.

Yields on London property are also attractive relative to those in Hong Kong, a boon for investors hunting for returns in an environment of ultralow interest rates at global central banks.

The average capitalization rate -- a measure of yield -- on offices in the City of London was 4.6% at the end of last year, according to Real Capital Analytics. In Hong Kong, the yield on offices was just 2.6%.

Worries that the Chinese property market is overheated, and the chance that Beijing could impose more-stringent curbs on outbound capital flows from China is also speeding efforts to invest overseas, further driving demand for London property.

---

Olga Cotaga contributed to this article.

Intelligent virtual helpers whittle away at human jobs

Jessica Twentyman 
938 words
1 March 2017
05:12
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

When Royal Bank of Scotland introduced an online chatbot to the public in 2016, executives were keen to present the technology as an aid for hard-pressed call-centre agents. But it was clear the chatbot s powers would grow and perhaps, in time, could come to rival those of its human colleagues.

"https://www.ft.com/artificial-intelligence-robotics", RBS said, lets the chatbot   first named Luvo but now called Assist   handle routine web chat requests from clients, such as queries about address changes or asking for a bank card to be authorised. This would free human advisers to help customers with more complex questions.

The "http://www-03.ibm.com/press/uk/en/pressrelease/50729.wss" that began in December has been limited to 10 per cent of RBS customers in Scotland who use web chat to communicate with the bank. Queries that Assist cannot understand or process are passed to human advisers.

But Assist s  cognitive  capabilities, provided by IBM s cloud-based Watson platform, mean it will learn to handle a greater range of more complex tasks. IBM executives suggest this could even reach the point where it might be able to understand how a customer is feeling   dissatisfied, frustrated, happy   and change its tone and actions accordingly.

The rise of AI in handling customer inquiries across a range of industries   from retail to energy companies   strongly suggests that human jobs in call centres may eventually be whittled away, but that those who remain in employment are likely to be handling more complex inquiries, says Shamus Rae, head of innovation and investment at management consultancy KPMG.

 AI is already making its presence felt by its ability to handle web chats, and over the next 18 months we expect to see the same automation of simple transactions happen with voice calls too,  he says.  That, to my mind, can only lead to the replacement of some call-centre staff, but there will still be a need for human-to-human conversations to work through more complicated issues with customers. 

Whether that adds to staff stress levels, by leaving them to focus only on difficult customers with serious problems, or makes the job more interesting will probably depend on the attitude and aptitude of call-centre workers. In job satisfaction terms, AI could be a good thing, suggests Graeme Gabriel, a former call-centre agent and manager who now works as a consultant at call-centre software company Verint.

 As a call-centre agent, it s those more complex inquiries that are the more rewarding part of the job,  he says.  After several hours straight on the phone, taking calls about simple things like a change of address, you actually look forward to someone calling up with an issue, even if they re having a bit of a moan, because that brings into play your customer service skills, the stuff you ve been trained to handle. 

Like many in the call-centre industry, he sees a role for AI not just in automating the mundane and routine, but also in assisting human workers to solve complex and challenging problems.

Much of the training that call-centre agents receive, Mr Gabriel, says, focuses on managing customer  hold times    the amount of time someone has to wait on the other end of the phone listening to music or company messages   while staff search for information or type in data. Lengthy hold times can make customers impatient.

Instead, AI could be working in the background, acting as a smart assistant to call-centre agents, feeding them accurate, up-to-date information, prompting them to ask the right questions and guiding their responses to queries.

Sheryl Kingstone, analyst at emerging technology specialist 451 Research, thinks this kind of AI  intelligence layer  is overdue.  Typically, staff use a trial-and-error script to question the customer and gain a better understanding of the request or problem, but this is time consuming and inaccurate, increasing fatigue and frustration both for the employee and the customer. 

By guiding interactions, AI could help humans focus more on meeting customers  needs, building relationships with them and increasing revenues through selling additional products and services, according to Stian Westlake, executive director of policy and research at innovation agency Nesta.

 AI also makes it easier to connect customers to call-centre workers with particular skills, such as problem resolution or sales, making call-centre jobs more differentiated, since it makes it easier to deploy talented call-centre staff to calls where they can really add value,  he adds.

This shift is already under way, says Chetan Dube, chief executive of IPSoft, the company behind AI virtual agent Amelia. Amelia is assisting call-centre staff rather than interacting directly with customers in about 10 of the 30 IPSoft clients who have deployed it. These include a Swiss bank that is using Amelia to guide call-centre agents through the process of helping customers with their retirement planning.

Mr Dube says:  There are two aspects to the use of AI in call centres. One is through the displacement of mundane chores that represent a waste of the brains and talents of call-centre agents. The second is as assisted intelligence, which leads to an augmented experience for agent and customer. I d argue this makes the job of call-centre agent more fulfilling. It elevates the worker. 

Joe Gagnon, chief customer strategy officer at technology company Aspect Software, says the ability of AI to take on routine matters will benefit staff.  As a result they will be happier, of greater value to the company, and develop a more positive perspective on their role.

Losses at Man Group overshadow rise in funds under management

Lindsay Fortado 
592 words
1 March 2017
10:47
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Man Group is managing more money than ever, but losses and redemptions in many of its funds during a difficult year led to a pre-tax loss for 2016 and a writedown of the value of GLG, its discretionary trading unit.

The world s largest publicly traded hedge fund group managed $80.9bn at the end of 2016, up 3 per cent from the end of 2015.

The company was buoyed by a 20 per cent increase in investments in its quant alternative strategies, which use computer algorithms to make investing decisions, positive performance in its long-only and credit funds, and the purchase of Aalto Invest, a $1.7bn US property manager.

But GLG was hit by redemptions in alternative and long-only strategies, driven by poor returns and a move among investors towards cheaper, passive products.

The company reported a pre-tax loss of $272m, down from a profit of $184m in 2015, largely driven by the impairment of GLG s carrying value, currency swings and investment losses. Its overall investment movement, however, was positive, netting $3.2bn.

Both management and performance fees at Man also declined, but one of the biggest hits was from foreign exchange translation and other moves, which cost it $2.9bn, just slightly less than in 2015.

Shares in the group slumped nearly 10 per cent after the news, and by 3pm were trading down 1.8 per cent at 143.8p.

 The market environment was very unforgiving for the first three quarters and Man found it difficult to generate performance for clients and performance fees,  Luke Ellis, chief executive of Man, said in the results.  This led to disappointing profits. 

The company has started 2017 with  a good pipeline of interest from clients and encouraging performance across most of our strategies,  he added.

Mr Ellis said Man had already started to benefit from  an improved environment for active management  under Donald Trump.

 The jury is out as to whether Donald Trump s presidency will be good for the overall economy, but what is beyond doubt is his way of thinking, which has had a significant impact on the market environment for active investment managers,  Mr Ellis said.

 In a Trump world, there are  winners and losers  and everyone is not equal, which means the divergence between favourable and unfavourable asset classes and companies becomes increasingly significant; it s the differing outcomes we see which can create the opportunities for alpha. 

Peter Lenardos, an analyst with RBC Capital Markets, called the results  mixed , but said the pipeline for new investment was likely to be positive, based on the company s indication.

Despite the writedown of GLG by $281m, Mr Ellis said the unit  remains a crucial part  of the business. Several initiatives, such as increasingly using quantitative analysis and machine learning to inform the discretionary traders,  will improve the consistency of performance,  he added.

The company also said it was undergoing a restructuring, which it began last year after Mr Ellis took over as chief executive following Manny Roman s departure to Pimco, the US bond giant.

The restructuring will cost the company about $21m, with $17m of that accounting for compensation charges in 2016. The savings should amount to about $20m per year, starting this year, the company said.

Following the acquisition of Aalto, which completed at the start of the year, Man has $325m of surplus capital. Mr Ellis has said that money could be used for acquisitions, investment teams or hiring individuals.

Algorithms Human agents are still needed for complicated questions, writes Jessica Twentyman

When Royal Bank of Scotland introduced an online chatbot to the public in 2016, executives were keen to present the technology as an aid for hard-pressed call-centre agents. But it was clear the chatbot's powers would grow and perhaps, in time, could come to rival those of its human colleagues.

IBM executives suggest this could even reach the point where it might be able to understand how a customer is feeling - dissatisfied, frustrated, happy - and change its tone and actions accordingly.

"That, to my mind, can only lead to the replacement of some call-centre staff, but there will still be a need for human-to-human conversations to work through more complicated issues with customers."

"As a call-centre agent, it's those more complex inquiries that are the more rewarding part of the job," he says. Lengthy hold times can make customers impatient.

"Typically, staff use a trial-and-error script to question the customer and gain a better understanding of the request or problem, but this is time consuming and inaccurate, increasing fatigue and frustration both for the employee and the customer."

Amelia is assisting call-centre staff rather than interacting directly with customers in about 10 of the 30 IPSoft clients who have deployed it. These include a Swiss bank that is using Amelia to guide call-centre agents through the process of helping customers with their retirement planning.

Mr Dube says: "There are two aspects to the use of AI in call centres. One is through the displacement of mundane chores that represent a waste of the brains and talents of call-centre agents. The second is as assisted intelligence, which leads to an augmented experience for agent and customer. I'd argue this makes the job of call-centre agent more fulfilling. It elevates the worker."

Joe Gagnon, chief customer strategy officer at technology company Aspect Software, says the ability of AI to take on routine matters will benefit staff. "As a result they will be happier, of greater value to the company, and develop a more positive perspective on their role."

CC Land Holdings Ltd, backed by a Hong Kong property tycoon, has agreed to pay 1.15 billion ($1.42 billion) for a London skyscraper in one of the city's biggest ever real estate deals.

British Land PLC and Oxford Properties Group, the real-estate arm of Canadian pension fund OMERS, said Wednesday that they sold the Leadenhall Building, known locally as the Cheesegrater for its triangular shape.

Investment in London property from China and Hong Kong has surged in recent months. A weak pound versus the yuan, alongside "https://www.wsj.com/articles/london-property-regains-its-currency-appeal-1488295333", have been driving the acquisition spree.

CC Land Holdings, controlled by Cheung Chung Kiu, bought a different London office building for 292 million earlier this year.

At 736 feet, the Cheesegrater is the tallest tower in London's main financial district. It was valued at 915 million at the end of September.

The influx stands out because U.K. property transaction "https://www.wsj.com/articles/european-property-investors-look-beyond-london-1477274461" in the last year. "https://www.wsj.com/articles/boe-still-sees-brexit-damaging-economy-in-long-run-1487677118", as well as high prices following a multi-year property boom, have made some overseas investors wary of current values.

But for Chinese investors, London property looks much different.

Worries that "https://www.wsj.com/articles/time-to-start-worrying-again-about-chinese-property-1484023452", and the possibility that Beijing could impose more "https://www.wsj.com/articles/china-issuing-strict-controls-on-overseas-investment-1480071529"from China is also speeding efforts to invest overseas, further driving demand for London property.

Analysts for months have expected British Land to sell its 50% stake in the Cheesegrater. British Land said Wednesday it will use the money from the sale to pay down its debt.

The Cheesegrater's 46 floors of office space are fully let. Completed in the summer of 2014, the building's angled design stems from local planning requirements that preserve views of St. Paul's Cathedral from certain vantage points.

"This sale shows continued investor appetite for best in class, well located property in London," said Tim Roberts, head of offices and residential at British Land, in a statement.

Losses at Man Group overshadow rise in funds under management

Pro Private Markets
Cancer Test Makers Raise Nearly $1 Billion; Grail, Freenome seek early cancer detection through blood testing

By Brian Gormley 
446 words
1 March 2017
15:04
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Life sciences startups Grail Inc. and Freenome Inc. have raised a total of about $1 billion to develop blood tests to detect cancers in their earliest stages, well before symptoms appear.

Through early detection, Grail, Freenome and other companies hope to catch tumors when the prospects for a cure are highest. The promise of making cancer screening easier and more effective has lured numerous investors and scientific collaborators to these and other blood-testing startups.

Grail, which secured more than $900 million in the first closing of a Series B round, said Johnson & Johnson Innovation is the largest investor in the financing. Other investors include Amazon.com Inc., ARCH Venture Partners, Bristol-Myers Squibb Co., Celgene Corp., Merck & Co., McKesson Ventures, Tencent Holdings Ltd. and Varian Medical Systems Inc.

Menlo Park, Calif.-based Grail said it expects to close on the rest of the round this month but didn't specify the amount.

Meanwhile, Andreessen Horowitz is leading a $65 million Series A investment in Freenome, which collected $5.5 million last year. Allen & Co., AME Cloud Ventures, Asset Management Ventures, Charles River Ventures, Data Collective, Founders Fund, GV (formerly Google Ventures), Innovation Endeavors, Polaris Partners, Spectrum 28 and Third Kind Ventures also participated.

Dozens of blood-testing or "liquid biopsy" "https://www.wsj.com/articles/liquid-biopsy-startups-face-long-road-to-payoff-1464003003" have emerged to detect cancer signals in the blood. Most have sought to predict the best treatment for people already known to have cancer. Startups like Grail and Freenome are pushing upstream to target cancer detection.

Grail's approach involves analyzing fragments of tumor DNA shed into the blood. The fraction of circulating-tumor DNA in the bloodstream compared to non-tumor DNA is small, but Grail says it can detect these faint signals. The new cash infusion will finance studies involving hundreds of thousands of participants, including cancer patients and people who haven't been diagnosed with the disease.

Freenome's technology employs machine learning to search for genetic signatures of cancer. The South San Francisco, Calif., company doesn't focus only on circulating-tumor DNA. Certain metabolic and immunological changes that occur in the body in response to cancer may be a more robust means of detecting the disease than tumor DNA, according to co-founder Riley Ennis.

Freenome also is conducting further validation research, working with 25 research partners on clinical trials, including Massachusetts General Hospital, University of California, San Diego and University of California, San Francisco. The company is initially testing for four cancers: prostate, breast, lung and colorectal.

Write to Brian Gormley at "mailto:brian.gormley@wsj.com"

SEC Targets Bank Loans to States, Localities | GOP Finds Inconsistencies in FSOC's Work | Pension Deficits Spell Trouble in Germany | Tougher Money-Laundering Rules in Europe | Tracy's Take: Omitted from Trump Speech to Congress: Financial Regulation; The Wall Street Journal's financial regulation newsletter for Wednesday, March 1, 2017. 

5,449 words
1 March 2017
13:04
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Tracy's Take: Omitted from Trump Speech to Congress: Financial Regulation

SEC Targets Bank Loans to State, Local Governments

House Committee Finds Inconsistencies in Work of Financial Stability Panel

Ballooning Pension Deficits May Take Air Out of Corporate Germany's Future

BIS Chief Warns of Risk of Political Backlash Against Financial Globalization

Omitted from Trump Speech to Congress: Financial Regulation

President Donald Trump's first address to Congress tackled taxes, trade, immigration, health care, energy policy, foreign policy, drugs, education and more. One omission: financial regulation.

The speech was just one more piece of evidence that financial deregulation  "https://www.wsj.com/articles/competing-priorities-bog-down-efforts-to-quickly-roll-back-dodd-frank-1488136408" he wants tackled in the early stretch of his presidency. Not once did Mr. Trump mention the dismantling of the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial overhaul, claims of a dearth of bank lending, or any of the other financial matters the Trump administration has previously touted as among the president's priorities.

Instead it echoed the campaign trail, where Mr. Trump's regular stump speech only occasionally touched on financial regulatory policy. Some in the financial sector are surely pleased Mr. Trump didn't repeat his campaign promise of a 21st century version of the Depression-era Glass-Steagall Act, which separated traditional and investment banking. His Treasury secretary, Steven Mnuchin, endorsed the pledge at a January Senate hearing without offering specifics.

On some issues, Mr. Trump's silence may be helpful. Cowen & Co. analyst Jaret Seiberg said in a note before the speech that if Mr. Trump didn't mention housing finance legislation, "that is actually a positive for getting housing finance reform as we believe the probability for action is better if this is a process that the Senate drives forward."

Mr. Trump didn't bring it up.

On the other hand, banking groups may be disappointed that there was no mention of the idea that loosening financial regulations would help the economy, despite the fact that Mr. Trump talked about what he views as a laggard economy.

A push by Mr. Trump could have elevated changing Dodd-Frank on the legislative agenda, where there already are indications the timeline for changing financial regulations may be slipping.

Mr. Trump may want to push deregulation without talking about it, but Democrats appeared happy to bring it up as a cudgel highlighting the president's Wall Street ties. In their Tuesday response, former Kentucky Gov. Steve Beshear accused the president early in his administration of "rolling back rules that provide oversight of the financial industry and safeguard us against another national economic meltdown." Mr. Beshear was referencing executive actions Mr. Trump has signed laying out a framework for altering financial regulations put in place during the Obama administration.

Of course, there is much deregulation that Mr. Trump's team can do without legislation, once his financial regulatory nominees are in place. Mr. Trump's advisers say filling the key job of Federal Reserve vice chair for bank oversight is a top priority, but so far Mr. Trump hasn't done so. At least nine other major regulatory jobs are empty, along with senior posts in the Treasury Department. His one financial regulatory nominee lawyer Jay Clayton, tapped to be chairman of the Securities and Exchange Commission has yet to get a hearing before the Senate Banking Committee.

Meanwhile, Republicans are "https://www.wsj.com/articles/republicans-warn-fed-against-adopting-new-rules-before-senate-confirms-trump-nominee-1487908416" who dares propose a rule change before Mr. Trump's team is in place.

After Tuesday's speech, those appointments look even more important, and the game of financial deregulation looks even more like it will be played to a great extent outside Congress

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

WSJ Pro: SEC Targets Bank Loans to State, Local Governments

By Andrew Ackerman

WASHINGTON The Securities and Exchange Commission is taking its first step toward shedding light on states and localities that increasingly rely on loans from banks to finance capital projects, rather than issuing debt in public markets.

The SEC on Wednesday is expected to propose new requirements that state and local governments disclose so-called direct bank loans, helping to illuminate a corner of the nearly $4 trillion municipal-bond market where there is currently no consistent reporting.

States and localities looking to fund projects such as roads, schools and bridges have increasingly turned to bank loans for cheaper financing in recent years. Such loans total roughly $40 billion to $50 billion in annual issuance, according to consulting firm Municipal Market Analytics. Bank loans are cheaper than issuing debt in the public markets in part because they don't require a rating, which can cost a municipality tens of thousands of dollars, and typically don't carry the same disclosure requirements.

Wednesday's proposal is uncontroversial and is one of a handful of measures the SEC is able to advance despite its depleted ranks. The agency is operating with just two commissioners, three fewer than its full complement.

Jay Clayton, President Donald Trump's pick to head the agency on a full-time basis, is awaiting Senate confirmation. The SEC's acting chief is Republican Commissioner Michael Piwowar. The other current commissioner is Kara Stein, a Democrat.

Unlike publicly traded corporations, borrowers in the municipal-bond market are exempt from requirements to file documents with the SEC before they sell bonds and file updates on a regular basis. As a result, the SEC regulates municipal-debt disclosures only indirectly through banks, prohibiting them from underwriting the bonds unless the issuer enters into private disclosure agreements with investors.

Wednesday's move is expected to expand an existing list of "material events" municipal borrowers agree to disclose on a continuing basis after the issuance of their debt.

If the proposal is adopted by the SEC, the list would expand to include a requirement to disclose the terms of any loans the borrowers may have entered into with a bank outside the public markets, according to people familiar with the matter. It also may include a requirement that municipalities disclose the details of any swaps contracts they enter into with banks.

Currently the list encompasses more than a dozen events ranging from payment delinquencies to ratings changes.

The SEC will seek public comment on Wednesday's proposal. After the comment period, the agency would have to vote on the measure before it could go into effect.

WSJ Pro: House Committee Finds Inconsistencies in Work of Financial Stability Panel

By Ryan Tracy

WASHINGTON U.S. officials used a flawed and inconsistent process for investigating risks at large financial firms during the Obama administration, according to a report published Tuesday by Republicans on the House Financial Services Committee.

The report looks at private records of the Financial Stability Oversight Council, or FSOC, a group of senior regulators created after the financial crisis to look out for financial-system risks. The council has the authority to designate nonbank financial firms "systemically important," a label that comes with stricter regulation.

The FSOC is led by the Treasury secretary and includes representatives from other regulators.

Republicans on the committee reviewed the FSOC's analysis of nine companies and found it to be "inconsistent and arbitrary."

The findings suggest the council changed its approach depending on whether the officials involved wanted to designate a company "systemically important" or not, as opposed to evaluating each company using a consistent process. "The inconsistencies in the FSOC's analysis are not random; to a large degree the designated companies were evaluated in one fashion, and the non-designated companies were evaluated a different way," the report said.

A spokeswoman for Rep. Maxine Waters of California, the top Democrat on the House committee, didn't immediately respond to a request for comment.

A Treasury Department spokesman didn't immediately respond to a request for comment. The Wall Street Journal reached out to several former government officials who worked on the FSOC's evaluations during the Obama administration, and received no immediate response.

The report's findings could carry weight with Treasury Secretary Steven Mnuchin, the FSOC's new chairman, as well as with lawmakers looking to alter or repeal the council's powers. Mr. Mnuchin is set to convene his first meeting of the council on Thursday behind closed doors.

The FSOC designated four nonbank companies as "systemically important." A federal court overturned its designation of MetLife Inc. The council rescinded its designation of General Electric Co.'s financing arm after the company shrank substantially. Prudential Financial Inc. and American International Group Inc. still bear the "systemically important" tag.

Ballooning Pension Deficits May Take Air Out of Corporate Germany's Future

By Natascha Divac and Christopher Alessi

FRANKFURT Financially conservative Germans are Europe's stereotype of fiscal prudence. Just not when it comes to their corporate pensions.

Many German companies still operate huge traditional pension plans, which pay workers set sums after they retire. Yet thanks to permissive German pension regulation, the companies don't have to sequester cash to meet those future costs.

The results are ballooning pension deficits, thanks to persistently low interest rates, that critics call a time bomb for corporate Germany. At the end of October 2016, German companies listed on the blue-chip DAX index had pension obligations of  ¬416 billion, yet had set aside just  ¬245 billion, according to consulting firm Mercer. That ratio is far lower than in other major countries.

The German approach is "an unserious practice [and] a risky bet on the future," says Dieter Truxius, dean at the University of Mannheim Business School.

Standard & Poor's, the ratings firm, in September revised its outlook for Deutsche Lufthansa AG to negative from stable, calling the enormous amount the airline owes current and future pensioners a "material credit weakness."

"It's a very important issue for Lufthansa," said an airline spokesman.

Lufthansa's pension obligations rose roughly 50% in the third quarter from the year earlier, to  ¬10.5 billion, the spokesman said. Lufthansa is working to close its defined-benefit plans to new staff.

Germany's pension problem has been compounded by its rapidly aging population, meaning many workers on defined-benefit plans are approaching retirement.

"If low interest rates persist for a longer time, things could get tough when demographic shifts intensify," said Dieter Bruninger, a pensions specialist at Deutsche Bank.

Low rates are now deeply ensconced in Europe, unlike the U.S., where a gathering economic recovery and the prospect of fresh government spending by the new administration of President Donald Trump have boosted long-term bond yields. A 10-year U.S. government bond yields 2.3%; a 10-year German bond, just 0.2%.

In the U.S. and U.K., companies must squirrel away assets in special funds to cover pension liabilities. In France and many other countries, pensions are mainly government-managed.

In Germany, where private-sector employees get corporate pensions on top of state pensions, companies decide for themselves how much money to set aside and in what way. Many companies dole out pensions from their bank accounts, similar to paying electric bills.

Pension obligations are recorded as a liability on German-company balance sheets, but money isn't always set aside in a separate fund German companies take provisions for the part of their obligations that aren't funded, but those are simply accounting entries that can be undone.

Experts say this method flatters German companies in comparison with U.S. and U.K. peers. "Germany allows a financial flexibility to delay setting aside cash which can be fully reinvested to pursue strategic targets," said Kazim Razvi of Fitch Ratings.

Germany's system works well in good times, when retiree numbers are low, and for growing companies. "But if a company must shrink because its plan didn't work out, it gets tough," said Mr. Truxius.

A growing number of public companies especially large ones do set money aside in segregated investment vehicles. The blue chips listed on the DAX index have funding to cover 58% of their pension obligations, but that's around 20 percentage points below the international average, according to consultants Willis Towers Watson.

Many Germans say the pension gap isn't concerning. Stephan Wildner, pensions specialist at Willis Towers Watson in Frankfurt, sees "no reason to panic."

"German companies have put enough money aside and are doing well economically," he said.

Heiko Massa-Wirth, a pensions expert at German steel producer Salzgitter AG, said the larger concern is mortality that former employees outlive expectations. That is the "real risk," he says, not low interest rates.

But persistent low rates make the edifice shaky. When interest rates are low, pension provisions grow because projected financial returns drop. Even if just an accounting matter, those growing liabilities eat into shareholder equity.

Thyssenkrupp AG's mounting pension provisions have sapped shareholder equity by  ¬2 billion over three years, said Chief Financial Officer Guido Kerkhoff. "Our book equity is to a large degree burdened by that."

Profitable firms can withstand that crunch, but higher provisions can batter smaller or chronically unprofitable firms.

Hardest hit by low rates are German midsize companies, who use different accounting rules that treat pension provisions more harshly.

EDC GmbH, a producer of CDs and DVDs, was forced to file for bankruptcy last year after falling behind new-technology rivals. It also faced the burden of higher pension obligations, said Uwe Rittentropp, the company's top staff representative.

"It was the straw that broke the camel's back," he said.

WSJ Pro: BIS Chief Warns of Risk of Political Backlash Against Financial Globalization

By Brian Blackstone

ZURICH The head of the Bank for International Settlements warned Tuesday of "a clear and present risk" of a political backlash against global finance, though at this point any retreat from financial globalization appears restricted to Europe.

"It would be premature at the very least to declare that global financial integration has gone into reverse," said Jaime Caruana, general manager of the BIS, a consortium of central banks based in Switzerland. Still, he said, in prepared remarks to a conference in Geneva, that "we need answers for those who question the benefits of global financial integration."

 It would be premature at the very least to declare that global financial integration has gone into reverse'

 Bank for International Settlements General Manager Jaime Caruana

His remarks come as some political leaders and the public question the benefits of globalization in terms of international trade and finance. U.S. President Donald Trump has shelved a trans-Pacific trade agreement and wants to renegotiate an existing trade agreement the U.S. has with Mexico and Canada. Meanwhile, euroskeptic parties are on the rise in France, Italy and Germany.

Much of Mr. Caruana's comments addressed what he called "peak finance," the idea that "the world has seen the peak of global finance and that financial deglobalisation has begun."

Citing BIS data, he said that "the appearance of peak finance is more a feature of European banking than of global banking" with foreign assets held by banks based in the eurozone, U.K. and Switzerland declining but largely stable in other advanced economies relative to the size of their gross domestic product.

"The shrinkage of European banks' foreign claims in relation to activity reflects a needed deleveraging after unhealthy financial booms," Mr. Caruana said. Some of the slowdown in global finance was a consequence of the aftermath of the global financial crisis, when banks took steps to boost capital and in reduce assets.

Still, "even if the data show that financial deglobalisation is not happening, it is the case that pressures are building to push back financial globalization," he said. "We need to make the positive case for financial globalization though not for increasing global leverage."

European Lawmakers Vote for Tougher Anti-Money-Laundering Rules

By Julia-Ambra Verlaine

BRUSSELS European Union lawmakers voted Tuesday for tougher anti-money-laundering rules that would cover virtual currencies and prepaid cards as part of the bloc's fight against terror financing and a broader clamp down on tax avoidance.

The vote advances a proposal put forward in July by the European Commission, the EU's executive arm. It would see the upper limit of nonreloadable prepaid cards lowered to  ¬150 ($159) from  ¬250 and virtual-currency platforms such as bitcoin brought under anti-money-laundering rules.

Terrorism across Europe brought heightened attention to prepaid cards, which the perpetrators of the November 2015 Paris attacks used.

"The behavior of criminals has not changed," said Kriajnis KariFa, a Latvian member of the EU Parliament co-leading the anti-money-laundering review. "They use anonymity to launder their illicit proceeds or finance terrorism."

The rules also focus on fighting tax evasion, after the Panama Papers disclosures led to allegations that clients of Panama City-based law firm Mossack Fonseca & Co. were able to dodge sanctions and avoid taxes. EU member states would be required to create centralized registers of information about bank and payment-account holders, which national authorities could access in case of suspicious activities.

Moreover, tax authorities would gain access to national anti-money-laundering information, including the true owners of companies and trusts to prevent tax evasion by hiding funds offshore.

"Tax evasion and avoidance cause countries inside and outside Europe to miss out on billions of euros every year," said Sven Giegold, who speaks for the EU's Green Party on financial and economic issues. "Through a public register for companies and trusts, the European Parliament wants to shed light on these structures in order to combat them."

The rules agreed by the parliament now require approval from national governments, one of the final steps in the EU legislative process.

Earlier in February, EU finance ministers agreed to measures to stop international corporations from cutting their tax bills another part of Europe's tax avoidance battle.

U.S. Banking Industry Annual Profit Hit Record in 2016

By Ryan Tracy

WASHINGTON The U.S. banking industry's total annual profit hit a record in 2016, and banks earned the highest return on equity they have seen in three years, according to government figures released Tuesday.

The Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. pegged the industry's 2016 net income at $171.3 billion, 4.9% more than the previous year. Profits were $43.7 billion in the fourth quarter of last year, up 7.7% from a year earlier. Only 4.2% of the nation's roughly 5,900 banks lost money in 2016, the lowest percentage in any year since 1995.

Banks' total profits are high in part because the size of their balance sheets and loan books are also at record levels. In percentage terms, the picture of the industry's health is more mixed.

Earnings as a share of banks' assets or equity haven't returned to levels reached before the financial crisis. Banks' return on assets was 1.04% in 2016, the same as the previous year, the FDIC said. Return on equity, a key measure of banks' profitability, rose to 9.32% industrywide last year, the highest since 2013, but less than the 10% figure many investors desire.

Loan growth also slowed in 2016, with balances up 5.3% during the year. Loans grew at a rate of 6.8% in 2015.

Banks' balance sheets are looking healthier, with noncurrent loans at their lowest average rate since 2007, the FDIC said.

The Newest Bank Blockchain: Will This Be the Breakthrough?

By Paul Vigna

A new consortium of banks and tech giants was set to announce its formation Tuesday, the latest stab at expanding open-ledger "blockchain" technology that could shave billions off the cost of basic Wall Street functions.

The latest project, which was to be unveiled in Brooklyn, is called the Enterprise Ethereum Alliance. The nonprofit foundation, which includes J.P. Morgan Chase & Co., Microsoft Corp., Bank of New York Mellon Corp., Intel Corp. and Banco Santander SA, shows the fight is continuing to develop the killer back-office app in finance, despite increased focus since last year's election on revenue growth.

So far, there has been more hype than tangible progress on blockchain at banks. The group announcing itself this week is building a platform based on an open-source software project J.P. Morgan started last year called Quorum, which aims to eliminate the need for a costly infrastructure of middlemen and third parties overseeing transactions.

Quorum uses the core concepts behind blockchain, the open-ledger technology that underpins the digital currency bitcoin. But it is based on Ethereum, an alternative version of bitcoin.

Blockchains have been appealing to banks because they are networks of connected computers sharing the same database and record-keeping. That eliminates costs, but raises questions about how banks would handle proprietary information on such a transparent ledger.

The Ethereum Enterprise Alliance expects to launch a working version of its protocol, called EntEth 1.0, this year, and will be inviting developers to help build it out and build products on it. The goal is to create an open, flexible platform that would at the same time preserve some privacy for banks.

The alliance is a sign that the race to build out this technology on a large scale is heating up again. There have been a number of attempts over the past two years, but none have yet produced a working version. Many of the banks involved have more than one iron in the fire, in fact. J.P. Morgan, for example, is also a shareholder in startups Digital Asset Holdings LLC and Axoni, and also has some of its top coders working on Quorum.

Whichever group gets there first, a working blockchain-based network would be a big advantage for the financial-services industry. In January, the consulting firm Accenture, which also is part of the alliance, estimated that eight of the world's largest investment banks could realize an average of $10 billion in annual cost savings, by 2025, assuming blockchain technology reaches widespread use.

 Telis Demos contributed this article.

Corrections & Amplifications

Consulting firm Accenture estimated eight of the world's largest investment banks could realize an average of $10 billion in annual cost savings, by 2025, assuming blockchain technology reaches widespread use. An earlier version of this article incorrectly stated that the financial services industry could realize an average of $10 billion in cost savings. (Feb. 28, 2017)

Market Talk: Daily Analysis Roundup

Labor's Fiduciary Rule Still up in the Air

A little more than a month before the Labor Dept's fiduciary rule is supposed to kick in, its fate is still up in the air. The Office of Management and Budget has finished its review of the delay the DOL is considering, determining that the delay the department is eyeing is "economically significant." That designation may make it harder for the DOL to initiate a six-month delay, which is what many in the industry have anticipated. Erin Sweeney, attorney at Miller & Chevalier Chartered who specializes in fiduciary obligations, says the OMB decision means the DOL has two choices: conduct a more robust analysis of a delay's implications, or opt for a shorter delay that the OMB might not deem economically significant. (lisa.beilfuss@wsj.com)

What Could Banks Do With Loosening Regulations?

If the bank industry has more opportunity to grow with loosened regulations than there are a range of opportunities available, says JPMorgan (JPM) Chief James Dimon at the bank's annual Investor Day presentation. He says banks in general, not necessarily just JPM, may open in more countries overseas, hire private bankers in Asia--and then grow Asia investment banking--or grow in cities that don't have retail branches. Dimon adds community banks may not be as driven by local real estate or AML risks around small business lending. (emily.glazer@wsj.com; @EmilyGlazerWSJ)

JPM Chief Dimon Talks Possible Regulatory Change

"Reforms are a very good thing to do," said JPMorgan (JPM) Chief James Dimon at the bank's annual Investor Day. "It is high time we look at it," he added, reiterating CFO Marianne Lake's earlier comments. "We're not fanatics about it. We know there was a crisis," and that capital, liquidity and other controls are necessary. But he highlighted the fact that there aren't safe harbors, such as with mortgages, and that has crimped lending perhaps as much as $1T over the years. Dimon also sounded off on CCAR again, reiterating that the bank believes in stress testing but that CCAR has uncertainties that makes it hard to manage capital. "We want a collaborative regime to talk about these things," he said. (emily.glazer@wsj.com; @EmilyGlazerWSJ)

Tarullo to Attend Final Fed Meeting

Federal Reserve governor Daniel Tarullo plans to participate in his final board meeting March 14-15, according to a Fed spokesperson. Tarullo has previously said he is leaving the central bank's board of governors around April 5. Fed officials who are stepping down sometimes skip their last policy meeting as a way to avoid participating in confidential discussions close to their exit date. Tarullo's decision to attend the meeting could boost the voice of the central bank's so-called "doves" who argue for patience in raising interest rates. The last Fed official to skip his final meeting was Narayana Kocherlakota, who did not participate in the December 2015 meeting and retired from his position as Minneapolis Fed president at the end of that year. (david.harrison@wsj.com; @d_harrison)

Financial Stability Board to Replace Tarullo

Global financial standards setter, the Financial Stability Board, appoints Norman Chan -- the head of the Hong Kong Monetary Authority -- to replace Federal Reserve governor Daniel Tarullo as chair of its standing committee on supervisory and regulatory cooperation. The post is for a two-year term beginning April 1. The new appointment comes as Tarullo, the lead architect of the US post-crisis financial rulebook, plans to resign from the Federal Reserve this spring. Tarullo was a proponent for imposing safeguards on big banks to avert a global financial meltdown (julia.verlaine@wsj.com; @JAVerlaine)

JPM's New Loan Platform Could Cut Closing Time by 50%

JPMorgan (JPM) is rolling out a new loan origination platform that could cut its time-to-close certain commercial banking lending processes by 50%. Known as CREOS, the system is expected to also boost transparency, generate incremental operating leverage, improve client experience and enhance risk management, according to commercial banking chief Doug Petno at the bank's annual Investor Day. The platform is an example of other tech investments JPM has been making to better use data. (emily.glazer@wsj.com; @EmilyGlazerWSJ)

Medisafe Sets Sights on European Expansion; The consumer app maker raises $14 million for push into commercial partnerships, big data 

By Cat Zakrzewski 
382 words
1 March 2017
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Medisafe Inc. on Wednesday will announce plans for expansion to London following an investment led by European-based Octopus Ventures.

The London firm led a $14.5 million Series B investment in the medication tracking app. M Ventures, the venture arm of the German pharmaceutical giant Merck KGaA, also participated in the round with existing investors Pitango Venture Capital, 7wire ventures, lool Ventures, TriVentures and Qualcomm Ventures.

Medisafe Chief Executive Omri Shor said he launched the company about five years ago in Israel after his father accidentally took two doses of his medication one day, including two rounds of his insulin shots. The app helps patients and caregivers track which medications they've taken and set reminder alarms to take pills.

With the Series B funding, the firm plans to open an office in London and expand its sales and marketing team.

The app has had 3.5 million users to date, who have recorded more than 500 million medication doses, the company said. Mr. Shor said the company will use the funding to hire more data scientists who can help pull insights from the data the company is collecting about medication use. He compared the app to the "biggest clinical trial."

"That data aspect is something that will create a lot of value for the industry," he said.

After Medisafe started as a consumer app, Mr. Shor said the company has begun seeing increased demand from commercial partners. Through partnerships, Medisafe helps health-care providers deliver content like medication videos to patients.

Medisafe is currently available on iOS and Android smartphones, tablets and some smartwatches, like the Apple Watch. Mr. Shor said some of the new funding would be used to help the company expand to new platforms, potentially even to devices like the Amazon Inc. Alexa.

Medisafe is compliant with HIPPA requirements and uses a level of encryption typically deployed by financial service providers to keep patient information secure.

"That's one of our biggest differentiators," Mr. Shor said. "Creating an app and putting it out there in a store is not that difficult."

Pro Private Markets
Lux Capital Raises $400 Million, Adds Partner Renata Quintini; Investor has backed driverless cars, space and satellites, drones and artificial intelligence 

By Tomio Geron 
326 words
28 February 2017
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Lux Capital, a venture firm focusing on hard science and future technologies, has raised $400 million for its fifth fund.

The Menlo Park, Calif. and New York-based firm, which now has $1.1 billion under management, has also added a new partner, Renata Quintini, formerly of Felicis Ventures. Ms. Quintini has invested in or advised startups including Cruise Automation, Dollar Shave Club, Bonobos, MetroMile, Planet, Swift Navigation, Survios, and Rigetti.

Lux has invested in startups in driverless vehicles such as Zoox; in space, such as through Planet and Orbital Insight; in drone plays such as AirMap and Saildrone; and in artificial intelligence startups like Clarifai and Recursion.

The firm, which raised $350 million for its previous fund, aims to invest in new emerging technologies before they become well known among other investors, said Josh Wolfe, a Lux co-founder and managing partner.

"When there's an abundance of capital, there's a scarcity of returns and when there's a scarcity of capital there's an abundance of returns," Mr. Wolfe said. "We like to look in areas where others aren't looking."

He cited the venture industry's focus on clean energy several years ago, and Lux's decision to instead invest in a company solving nuclear waste, Kurion, which was acquired in 2016 by Veolia.

Ms. Quintini, who has a law degree, will continue to invest in "frontier technology" while seeking to examine the ethical questions raised by cutting-edge innovation.

"What's special and unique about Lux is that the DNA of the firm, the culture and everything is built to invest in the future and invent the future," Ms. Quintini said. "Some VCs dip their toe in, but Lux is built to do this."

Technology
SoftBank Angles For AI Advantage

By Stu Woo 
367 words
28 February 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

BARCELONA -- Within 30 years, artificial intelligence will be smarter than the human brain.

That is according to Masayoshi Son, chief executive of SoftBank Group Corp., who says that supersmart robots will outnumber humans and more than a trillion objects will be connected to the internet within three decades.

These beliefs underpin the wave of large deals the Japanese internet and telecommunications company has pulled off in the past year, he said Monday. These include starting a $100 billion technology-investment fund with a Saudi sovereign-wealth fund, buying British microprocessor designer ARM Holdings PLC for $32 billion and acquiring U.S. asset manager Fortress Investment Group PLC for $3.3 billion.

This 30-year forecast created urgency, Mr. Son said in a speech at the telecom industry's biggest trade show, Mobile World Congress. "That is why I'm in a hurry to aggregate cash to invest."

In a brief interview after his speech, Mr. Son said his $100 billion project with the Saudis, dubbed the SoftBank Vision Fund, was bigger than the $65 billion in combined investments from the venture-capital world. He said the SoftBank Vision Fund would be focused.

"Artificial intelligence, Internet of Things, smart robots: Those are the three main things I'm interested in," he said.

The Internet of Things is the technology world's term for connecting everyday objects, such as refrigerators and sneakers, to the web.

Mr. Son said he didn't expect oil company Saudi Aramco's planned initial public offering of stock to affect the size of Vision Fund. "They are a great partner," he said of the Saudis. "They're already rich. They have lots of money, even before the IPO."

Mr. Son said this month's acquisition of Fortress Investment would complement the Vision Fund. "SoftBank Vision Fund is more for the technology," he said. "Fortress is for everything else -- traditional private equity and so on."

Fortress has about $70 billion in assets under management.

Combined with the Vision Fund, he said, "pretty soon we will have $200 billion together. So we would help each other to identify investment opportunities."

Technology
Behind SoftBank's Wave of Deals --- CEO angles to position Japanese telecom firm for the coming arrival of supersmart robots

By Stu Woo 
604 words
28 February 2017
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
B4
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

BARCELONA -- Within 30 years, artificial intelligence will be smarter than the human brain.

That is according to Masayoshi Son, chief executive of SoftBank Group Corp., who says that supersmart robots will outnumber humans and more than a trillion objects will be connected to the internet within three decades.

These beliefs underpin the wave of large deals the Japanese internet and telecommunications company has pulled off in the past year, he said Monday. These include starting a $100 billion technology-investment fund with a Saudi sovereign-wealth fund, buying British microprocessor designer ARM Holdings PLC for $32 billion and acquiring U.S. asset manager Fortress Investment Group PLC for $3.3 billion.

This 30-year forecast created urgency, Mr. Son said in a speech at the telecom industry's biggest trade show, Mobile World Congress. "That is why I'm in a hurry to aggregate cash to invest."

In a brief interview after his speech, Mr. Son said his $100 billion project with the Saudis, dubbed the SoftBank Vision Fund, was bigger than the $65 billion in combined investments from the venture-capital world. He said the SoftBank Vision Fund would be focused. "Artificial intelligence, Internet of Things, smart robots: Those are the three main things I'm interested in," he said.

The Internet of Things is the technology world's term for connecting everyday objects, such as refrigerators and sneakers, to the web.

Mr. Son said he didn't expect oil company Saudi Aramco's planned initial public offering of stock to affect the size of Vision Fund. "They are a great partner," he said of the Saudis. "They're already rich. They have lots of money, even before the IPO."

Mr. Son said this month's unexpected acquisition of Fortress Investment would complement the Vision Fund. "SoftBank Vision Fund is more for the technology," he said. "Fortress is for everything else -- traditional private equity and so on. Money is money, you know?"

Fortress has about $70 billion in assets under management. Combined with the Vision Fund, he said, "pretty soon we will have $200 billion together. So we would help each other to identify investment opportunities and manage the portfolio companies."

Mr. Son said he would also greatly increase the research-and-development funding for ARM, the British chip designer SoftBank bought in 2016. ARM doesn't manufacture chips, but it designs the basic architecture for the chips in more than 95% of the world's smartphones. Mr. Son said he believed that within 30 years, more than one trillion ARM-designed Internet of Things chips would be in use.

In his speech, Mr. Son said that while average humans had an IQ of roughly 100 and that geniuses such as Albert Einstein were believed to score about 200, superintelligent computers would have IQs of 10,000. He said computer chips possessing superintelligence would be put into robots big and small that can fly and swim. These robots would number in the billions and would be greater than the human population within 30 years, he said.

The chips would also be in everyday objects. "One of the chips in our shoes will be smarter than our brain," he said. "We will be less than our shoes, and we will be stepping on them."

During the brief interview after the speech, Mr. Son said he remained committed to stepping down from his job once he turned 70. He is 59 years old.

(The following is a roundup of selected companies that have revealed securities sales in recent Form D regulatory filings. These companies are required to file a Form D notification within 15 days after the first sale of securities in an offering, which could indicate fundraising, acquisitions or other securities activity. The filings don't list the recipients of the securities being offered. However, they do list board members, so in cases where new investors have surfaced, we'll report them below.)

Halfpenny Technologies Inc., Blue Bell, Pa., which enables hospitals, health plans, and laboratories to exchange key electronic medical information across devices and record-keeping systems, has issued $500,000 in debt. Osage Partners and Rittenhouse Ventures list the company in their portfolios.

Sanergy Inc., Boston, provides hygienic sanitation services throughout Africa, has sold $2.5 million of a $7 million equity round. Acumen and Novastar Ventures count the company in their portfolios.

SynGen Inc., Sacramento, Calif., a regenerative-medicine device company focused on the development of next-generation stem-cell harvesting systems, has issued another $500,000 in equity, debt and rights. The company previously issued a total $6.1 million through similar offerings, according to several earlier filings going back to last February. Bay City Capital is a backer.

Trufa Inc., San Mateo, Calif., a data analytics software provider, has issued $400,000 of a $1 million debt offering. In 2013, the company raised a $4.5 million Series A round led by Accel Partners.

Wireless Registry Inc., Washington, a global registry for wireless names and identifiers, has issued all but $100,000 of a $1 million offering of debt and rights. The company is included in the portfolios of American Family Ventures and New Atlantic Ventures.

A European bad bank is needed to clean up European banks  mess

Martin Sandbu 
1,063 words
27 February 2017
11:51
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

A few weeks ago Andrea Enria, the head of the European Banking Authority, proposed an EU-wide publicly funded  bad bank . The idea was that it would buy out the overhang of bad loans that still debilitates too many European banks (the Italian banking system is the most worrying casualty). The idea was "http://www.reuters.com/article/us-eu-banks-regulations-germany-idUSKBN15F21G" by German  government sources . But a "http://www.centralbanking.com/central-banking-journal/opinion/2481794/why-the-eu-needs-an-asset-management-company" by Enria s EBA colleagues, Piers Haben and Mario Quagliariello, shows that the proponents of the idea are not giving up without a fight. That is a good thing.

It is worth restating the simple policy reason to sequester bad loans and other toxic assets from banks  balance sheets. The value of such assets is uncertain, but plausibly much less than the accounting value. If their quantity is large enough, that means the solidity of the bank that holds them is in doubt. The possibility of lossmaking time-bombs lurking in its portfolio can both inhibit the bank itself from undertaking new risks, and may lead its own creditors to charge more for financing it. Both create pressure to retrench rather than lend more.

This needn t have wider importance if it s a single bank but it can hold back economic growth if it applies widely enough through an economy s banking system. A lending drought is the last thing an economy needs after the sort of crisis that typically causes widespread bad loans in the first place. Conversely, freeing up the banking sector to lend can power a recovery. By  warehousing  the loans in an entity that is not itself engaged in lending   often called a  bad bank    that separate institution can take its time to liquidate the assets or let them run off to maturity. Crucially, those decisions will not be linked to decisions determining that availability of fresh credit in the economy.

Hence the policy imperative to clean out the rot from banks  balance sheets swiftly after a financial crisis. The US did this with more determination than Europe, which is part of why its recovery was stronger. On a smaller-scale comparison, Iceland s more radical approach to its banks led to results that compare favourably to Ireland s (and is a better explanation of the two countries  economic performance since 2008 than their different exchange rate regimes).

Why does it typically prove so difficult to do the right thing in this regard? Because removing bad assets from a bank means the losses have to be allocated. Either they are allocated with the bank, which is forced to write down the relevant assets to their fair value, or they are more or less explicitly taken on by the buyer of the assets who pays more than that fair value, leaving the selling bank with smaller losses. In the first case, the banks  solvency may be at risk, and it may have to be restructured or bailed out   neither a palatable choice for most politicians. In the latter case, taxpayer money will more or less surreptitiously be put at risk, given that the institution taking on the bad loans after a systemic crisis will most probably be a government-sponsored one.

Note, therefore, that a lot hinges on the price at which the bad assets are transferred out of the banking institution and into the  warehouse  in which it will be kept. This will always involve guesswork. But notice two things. If the price is close enough to fair value, it need not cost taxpayers (or whoever puts up the funds for the  bad bank ) anything. And there is not just one but a range of prices where this is possible, because the bad assets  economic value for the bad bank should be higher than for the bank being weighed down by them.

That s because as a lender, the selling bank has stricter capital requirements than a pure asset management company so it faces a greater ongoing cost in holding on to them. It is also because an asset management company, having no other objectives and priorities, can time its asset disposals more profitably than a bank. And finally, in systemic crises, moving bad assets out of the banking system itself should improve credit and growth in the economy   which in turn should somewhat boost the value of the assets.

That leaves the question: why should the  bad bank  be pan-European? Haben and Quagliariello give some good arguments relating to economies of scale and expertise, as well as the incontestable fact that the bad loan problem in some member states has affected growth in the region as a whole (and monetary policy effectiveness for the eurozone as a whole). It is politically difficult for them to emphasise the most important reason, which is this: a supranational bad bank is desirable because national governments have not been up to the job. That in itself is understandable   the national politics of forcing largely nationally focused banks to take losses is awful   if not forgivable. But the supranational regulatory structure that is emerging as part of Europe s banking union now has the power to push banks into selling off the assets that weight them down at an unsubsidised price. All that is needed, given the absence of a liquid market, is a buyer. If the EU heeds the EBA s advice, it will earn the gratitude of Europe s unemployed and underpaid.

Other readables

Researchers are examining whether "http://nber.org/papers/w23180" in deciding whether a defendant should await trial in jail or out on bail. They suggest huge improvement in policy could be had through machine learning.  Crime can be reduced by up to 24.8 per cent with no change in jailing rates, or jail populations can be reduced by 42.0 per cent with no increase in crime rates. Moreover, we see reductions in all categories of crime, including violent ones. Importantly, such gains can be had while also significantly reducing the percentage of African-Americans and Hispanics in jail. 

Machine bail decisions are not just science fiction, moreover: "https://motherboard.vice.com/en_us/article/an-algorithm-is-replacing-bail-hearings-in-new-jersey" for or against bail in New Jersey and elsewhere.

Numbers news

* French economic sentiment is buoyant.

Markets
Prosper Inks $5 Billion Loan-Buying Deal With Investors Including Soros, Jefferies; Investors in online lender's loans also include Third Point, Fortress affiliate

By Peter Rudegeair 
658 words
27 February 2017
21:40
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Prosper Marketplace Inc. struck a deal on Monday to sell up to $5 billion worth of consumer loans to a group of investment firms over the next two years.

Members of the group arranged by the online lender include New Residential Investment Corp, a real-estate investment trust managed by an affiliate of asset manager Fortress Investment Group LLC; hedge-fund firms Soros Fund Management LLC and Third Point LLC; and the investment bank Jefferies LLC. The Wall Street Journal reported in August that the four firms in the consortium being announced Monday were in advanced talks with Prosper on a loan-buying deal.

The arrangement clears one of the biggest issues hanging over the San Francisco-based company, which was valued at $1.9 billion in an April 2015 fundraising round. A subsequent drying up of investor appetite for Prosper's credits caused loan volume to fall by around one-third in the first nine months of 2016, prompting the company to lay off staff and slash spending to conserve cash.

"The space was maybe hotter [among money managers] than the mechanics or fundamentals would have supported at the time," said Prosper CEO David Kimball in an interview Monday. "Now I think we're at a stage where there's just a little more of a thoughtful approach."

Mr. Kimball, who took over the top job at Prosper late last year, said his focus was on getting the company to profitability. The bulk of Prosper's revenue comes from fees it charges borrowers to take out loans, so having a large commitment from money managers to fund loans helps ensure that revenue will be more sustainable.

That certainty won't come cheap, though. As part of the deal, the investor group will receive warrants to buy Prosper shares that are tied to the volume of loans that they buy at face value. If the group buys the full $5 billion, they would have the right to purchase shares representing 35% of the company, according to people familiar with the matter.

Prosper said that the consortium of investors has also lined up financing for up to $1 billion of loan purchases from Credit Suisse Group AG, Deutsche Bank AG, Goldman Sachs Group Inc. and Morgan Stanley. Fintech analytics company dv01 Inc. will serve as the consortium's loan data agent.

Throughout the deal talks, Prosper was advised by investment bank Financial Technology Partners LP.

Founded in 2005, Prosper was originally structured as a peer-to-peer lender, but following a 2013 recapitalization, it began to look to Wall Street firms to fund more of the loans it made. Until last fall, one of its biggest sources of funding was Citigroup Inc., which packaged Prosper loans into bonds. That arrangement ended in April after Prosper executives indicated dissatisfaction about how the bonds were priced.

Mr. Kimball said that he expected Prosper to launch its own securitization program, Prosper Marketplace Issuance Trust, in the second quarter. Loans that will underlie those bonds will likely come from the new investor group as well as other Prosper investors that want to fund their purchases in alternative ways.

Part of what delayed the deal's closing was a legal dispute between Prosper and another money manager, Colchis Capital Management, over the terms of an earlier loan-purchase agreement, Mr. Kimball said. In November, Prosper said it would pay Colchis $9 million in cash and warrants to purchase shares worth 7% of the company as part of a settlement.

Pro Private Markets
The Roundup

5,809 words
27 February 2017
20:55
The Wall Street Journal Online
