English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

U.S. & Canada

Buyouts

Avista Capital Partners, New York

Avista Capital Partners rounded up at least $200.3 million so far for its fourth fund, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. According to the filings, the total offering amount for Avista Capital Partners IV LP and Avista Capital Partners (Offshore) IV LP is $1 billion. The firm was seeking $2 billion for the vehicle's predecessor, according to SEC filings, although it isn't clear how much that fund ultimately collected. Avista targets control and minority investments of $50 million to $300 million in the health-care, communications, industrials and energy sectors.

Bertram Capital Management, San Mateo, Calif.

Bertram Capital Management rounded up at least $261 million so far for Bertram Growth Capital III LP and a related pool, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The tally raised so far for the fund puts it more than halfway to a $500 million total offering amount indicated in the filings. Bertram closed its second fund in 2010 with $500 million in limited partner commitments. The firm targets control investments in companies with annual revenue of $30 million to $250 million and earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization of $5 million to $30 million. Typical platform investments range from $25 million to $100 million.

Carlyle Group LP, Washington

Carlyle Group LP plans to start raising its third global financial-services buyout vehicle in the next few months, said a person with knowledge of the matter. The person declined to specify a target size for the new fund. The firm's previous vehicle under this strategy, Carlyle Global Financial Services Partners II LP, closed on $1 billion in 2014 and was 80% invested as of Dec. 31, according to a news release detailing Carlyle's most recent quarterly earnings. Carlyle's financial-services group partially or fully exited six investments in 2016 totaling more than $500 million, said Co-Chief Executive Bill Conway during a conference call to discuss the firm's financial results. Investors in Fund II include the Employees Retirement System of Texas and the Kentucky Teachers' Retirement System. Meanwhile, Carlyle also has hit the $2.5 billion hard cap for its fourth distressed investing fund, Carlyle Strategic Partners IV LP, according to a news release. The new distressed vehicle is more than triple size of its predecessor vehicle, which collected $703 million and closed in 2013, Private Equity Analyst previously reported. Minnesota State Board of Investment committed to the latest fund, according to a pension fund document. The Strategic Partners funds invest from New York and London in the debt and equity of financially distressed businesses and are managed by Carlyle's Special Situations and Corporate Opportunities group, which was launched in 2004.

Dyal Capital Partners, New York

Dyal Capital Partners, a division of Neuberger Berman Group, closed the largest fund ever raised to buy direct stakes in private-equity fund managers. Dyal Capital Partners III LP wrapped up at $5.3 billion, a sum that exceeded the vehicle's original fundraising target by more than $2 billion. Its predecessor closed with $2.1 billion in 2015, according to a Neuberger Berman annual report. The new fund so far has committed more than half its capital in six transactions, buying stakes in Vista Equity Partners, EnCap Investments, Silver Lake, H.I.G. Capital, Starwood Capital Group and KPS Capital Partners. It plans to invest in 10 to 12 private-equity firms in total. Investors in the third fund include the New York State Common Retirement Fund, Alaska Permanent Fund Corp., New Jersey State Investment Council and the Board of Regents of the University of Michigan, according to pension documents. Dyal takes stakes of about 20% in established private-equity shops, offering capital for expansion in exchange for a portion of management fees and carried interest.

EIV Capital, Houston

EIV Capital held a final closing for its third fund at the vehicle's $450 million hard cap. The energy-focused firm exceeded its $350 million target for EIV Capital Fund III LP and affiliates. Like the firm's previous funds, its latest vehicle will invest primarily within the midstream energy sector. It will focus on infrastructure projects including those involved in gathering, processing, distributing, storing and marketing oil, natural gas and refined products. Limited partners include the Texas County & District Retirement System, according to the pension system's website. EIV's second fund held a final closing on $267 million in 2015. The firm invests $20 million to $80 million of equity in businesses with earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization of $5 million to $40 million and enterprise values of $20 million to $200 million.

Great Hill Partners, Boston

Great Hill Partners held a first and final closing of its sixth private-equity fund, Great Hill Equity Partners VI LP, at its $1.5 billion hard cap. The vehicle, which had a $1.25 billion target, also collected more than its $1.1 billion predecessor, which closed in 2015. Similar to Great Hill's previous funds, the latest capital pool will invest in midmarket companies with enterprise values of $25 million to $500 million. Alameda County Employees' Retirement Association committed up to $50 million to the fund, according to a pension document. Green Hill targets investments of $25 million to $150 million in companies in the business and consumer services, health-care, media, communications and software industries.

Huron Capital Partners, Detroit

Huron Capital Partners held a final closing for Huron Fund V LP at $550 million. The vehicle closed at its hard cap and exceeded a $500 million target. The fund was oversubscribed with more than $1 billion of demand, according to placement agent Sixpoint Partners. The firm's previous pool closed above its goal at $500 million in 2013. Fund V will seek to invest $20 million to $70 million per transaction in lower midmarket companies with valuations of less than $200 million. Huron targets U.S. and Canadian companies in the business-services, consumer products and services and specialty manufacturing sectors.

Incline Equity Partners, Pittsburgh

Incline Equity Partners wrapped up its fourth fund at $601.1 million. Incline Equity Partners IV LP exceeded its $450 million target and closed at its hard cap. The new fund is almost double the size of its predecessor, which closed at $301 million in 2013. Limited partners for the latest fundraising effort include the Pennsylvania Public School Employees' Retirement System, the Texas Municipal Retirement System and the Los Angeles City Employees' Retirement System. Incline, which spun out of PNC Financial Services Group Inc. in 2011, invests in U.S. and Canadian distribution, light-manufacturing and business- and industrial-services companies valued at $50 million to $200 million.

Luminate Capital Partners, San Francisco

Luminate Capital Partners gathered at least $84.1 million so far for its debut fund, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. Luminate, which targets controlling stakes in software and software-based services companies, is seeking $200 million for Luminate Capital Partners LP, according to the filing. The firm was founded in 2014 by Hollie Moore Hynes, who had been with Silver Lake for more than a decade.

Northleaf Capital Partners, Toronto

Northleaf Capital Partners raised 950 million Canadian dollars ($714.6 million) for its second infrastructure fund focused primarily on investments in North America, Western Europe and Australia. The firm raised more capital for Northleaf Infrastructure Capital Partners II than its debut infrastructure fund, which closed with C$520 million in 2014. Northleaf's infrastructure funds target equity investments in small to midsize assets. The firm has completed two deals through its latest fund, including bulk liquid storage assets in the U.K. and wind and solar assets in the U.S. It also has committed to acquire a stake in Northwest Parkway, a Denver-area highway. Late last year, the firm formed a global private-credit platform with more than $1.4 billion in capital raised from a core group of Canadian investors.

One Equity Partners, New York

One Equity Partners, which spun out from J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. in 2015, collected $1.65 billion for its first fund raised with capital from third-party investors. The firm completed fundraising for One Equity Partners VI LP above its $1.5 billion goal and attracted commitments from a range of institutional and high-net-worth investors, including Lexington Partners and AlpInvest Partners. One Equity has invested or committed about $600 million of the fund to eight platform deals and eight add-on acquisitions. One person with knowledge of the firm said it has a ninth deal in the pipeline. The firm targets investments of about $100 million to $150 million, including co-investment capital, in midmarket companies in the health-care, industrial services and technology sectors.

Perella Weinberg Partners, New York

Perella Weinberg Partners, through its PWP Growth Equity unit, is pitching its second growth-equity fund with a $750 million offering amount, according to Securities and Exchange Commission filings. The amount the firm is seeking for PWP Growth Equity Fund II LP exceeds the $600 million the firm closed on for its debut growth-equity fund in 2014. Perella Weinberg's growth-equity arm targets investments in lower midmarket companies in the consumer, services and industrial sectors.

Resource Capital Funds, Denver

Resource Capital Funds, a private-equity firm that focuses on the mining sector, has launched a new fund seeking $2 billion. The firm didn't record any capital commitments for Resource Capital Fund VII LP in a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. If the new fund hits its fundraising goal, it would roughly match the size of its predecessor, which closed on $2.04 billion in 2013. Resource Capital Funds invests globally in the metal, mineral and mining sectors, aiming to deploy $10 million to $300 million per investment. It backs projects in all stages of development and invests across the capital structure.

Sageview Capital, Greenwich, Conn.

Sageview Capital collected at least $196.2 million for its second fund, according to Securities and Exchange Commission filings. Sageview Capital Partners II (Onshore) LP and an affiliated offshore vehicle have a cumulative total offering amount of $600 million, according to the filings. The amount Sageview is seeking for the latest fundraising effort is sharply down from the firm's $1.35 billion debut fund, which closed in 2006. The firm primarily seeks minority investments of $20 million to $75 million in companies in the technology, financial-services and business-services sectors.

Vector Capital, San Francisco

Technology-focused Vector Capital collected $1.4 billion for its fifth fund. The final closing of Vector Capital V LP exceeded a $1.2 billion target. The vehicle netted more capital than its fourth fund, which held a final closing on $1.2 billion in 2007. District of Columbia Retirement Board approved a $30 million commitment to the latest fund in October, according to the pension system's board meeting minutes. Vector targets midmarket public and private technology companies with enterprise values of $100 million to $2 billion in North America, Europe and Israel. The firm focuses on such industries as software, internet and digital media, communications and industrial technology.

Veritas Capital Fund Management, New York

Veritas Capital Fund Management completed fundraising for its sixth fund at $3.55 billion, nearly doubling the capital it raised for its $1.88 billion predecessor in 2014. Veritas Capital Fund VI LP reached its hard cap and surpassed its $3 billion target. The firm kicked off fundraising at the end of September and finished the process in mid-January, said Chief Executive and Managing Partner Ramzi Musallam. Mr. Musallam said the firm brought on new investors for its latest fundraising effort, including sovereign-wealth funds, European pension plans and family investment offices. The firm hasn't yet deployed capital from its newest vehicle, he said, adding its fifth fund is itself likely to house one more platform investment. Veritas invests in technology-focused midmarket companies within government and commercial spaces, including the aerospace and defense, health-care, technology, national security, communications, energy and education sectors. It generally targets companies with enterprise values of $200 million to $3 billion, Mr. Musallam said.

Distressed/Turnaround

Angeles Equity Partners, Los Angeles

Angeles Equity Partners wrapped up its first fund with $360 million, above a $300 million target. The fund, comprised of Angeles Equity Partners I LP and parallel vehicles, initially had a $350 million hard cap, said a spokesman for the firm. Limited partners in the fund include investors from North America, Europe and the Middle East. Angeles Equity buys industrial companies with enterprise values of up to $200 million that are stressed, underperforming or in need of financial and operational restructuring in the U.S. and Canada. The firm also seeks family- or founder-owned businesses and noncore assets of companies that require carve-outs or turnarounds.

Funds of Funds

Aberdeen Asset Management, Aberdeen, Scotland

Aberdeen Asset Management is seeking $300 million for its newest U.S. pool. The firm in Securities and Exchange Commission filings didn't record any capital commitments for Aberdeen U.S. Private Equity VII LP or a related vehicle. Aberdeen in 2015 expanded its U.S. footprint with the purchase of FLAG Capital Management. Later that year, it held a final closing for FLAG Private Equity VI LP at $295 million to target investments in a portfolio of lower midmarket U.S. buyout funds. It isn't clear from the filing whether the latest fund is a predecessor to the FLAG vehicle.

The Investment Fund for Foundations, Radnor, Pa.

The Investment Fund for Foundations, which manages money for nonprofits, collected $110 million so far for a new private-equity fund of funds, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The filing for TIFF Private Equity Partners 2017 LP doesn't indicate an offering amount or if the vehicle has closed. TIFF was formed in 1991 by a group of foundations to help nonprofits invest. It managed a total of about $9.5 billion as of Sept. 30, including $2.4 billion in private investments, according to its website.

Crown Capital Partners, Calgary, Alberta

Crown, through its Crown Capital Funding Corp. subsidiary, increased its capital commitment to the vehicle by C$11.25 million to C$61.25 million. After this closing, Crown holds a 35% interest in the fund, which targets special situations transactions. Crown, through its special situations strategy, provides senior and subordinated loans. To date, the fund has invested a total of C$110 million in six transactions.

Orion Energy Partners has secured $480 million so far for a new direct-lending fund targeting midmarket energy infrastructure deals. Orion Energy Credit Opportunities Fund II LP held a second closing in late January and is expected to wrap up fundraising this year, said people familiar with the matter. It is targeting $750 million and doesn't have a hard cap, the people said. He was portfolio manager for the $805 million Energy Capital Partners Mezzanine Opportunities Fund LP, which closed in 2013. Mr. Massouh left Energy Capital to form Orion in 2015. Over time, it will also consider opportunities to form companies, said Jon Behr, managing director of the fund. It so far has invested in Bigfoot Biomedical Inc., a developer of a new artificial-pancreas technology. The fund also has made another investment that JDRF hasn't yet revealed.

ManchesterStory Group, a new Midwest firm that aims to invest in financial-technology and health-care companies with a focus on the insurance industry, intends to raise $125 million for ManchesterStory Venture Fund LP, a goal that could be increased to $200 million, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The firm, which now has about $200 million under management, plans to continue investing in early-stage companies. With the new fund, Merus said it would make six to eight Series A investments and 12 to 15 seed investments. The firm typically invests about $5 million in Series A rounds and $500,000 in seed-stage companies. Merus frequently invests in the Series A rounds of companies it has backed at the seed stage. SoFi increases its firepower with $500m funding round

Ben McLannahan in New York 
601 words
25 February 2017
00:12
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

SoFi, the biggest and the brashest of a new breed of online lenders in the US, has sealed a $500m "https://www.sofi.com/press/sofi-announces-500-million-strategic-growth-investment-led-silver-lake/", giving it firepower to push into new products and markets including Canada and Australia.

The private San Francisco-based company last received a big shot of funds   its fifth   in September 2015, when SoftBank of Japan led a $1bn round, the biggest in the history of  fintech . The latest infusion was led by Silver Lake, the private equity firm, supported by SoftBank and GPI Capital, an asset manager, among others.

The valuation was undisclosed.

 SoFi s platform   equal parts technology, community, and exceptional customer experience   is rapidly transforming personal finance,  said Mike Bingle, New York-based managing partner at Silver Lake, who is taking a seat on the board.

The six-year-old company, founded by Mike Cagney and a couple of his classmates from Stanford Graduate School of Business, began with refinancing student debt, recognising that there was a big gap between the interest rates good borrowers were paying on their loans from the government and the rates at which private investors were willing to lend to them.

Mr Cagney   a software engineer turned trader turned entrepreneur   has since branched out into mortgages, personal loans, insurance and wealth management. Last month he took another step in his quest to build a retail banking empire by buying Zenbanx, a provider of bank accounts, money transfers and credit cards.

Along the way, he has tried to foster a club-like mentality, hosting dinners and happy-hour mixers for his customers, whom he calls  members .

A person familiar with the latest fundraising said it should support SoFi s drive outside its home market, while allowing it to spend more on its basic mission of  building a bank in the cloud .

Many online lenders have had a tough time of it over the past year, as choppy credit markets, rising delinquencies and a governance scandal at Lending Club prompted many buyers of the platforms  loans to put programmes on hold. That, in turn, has caused several groups to cut back on generating assets, squeezing their bottom lines as revenues falter.

But in recent months institutional investors have begun to return, apparently convinced that the platforms have smartened up their acts. At Lending Club, for example, banks accounted for 31 per cent of loan volume in the fourth quarter, double their share of the third quarter, even as the company cut out special discounts.

Overall, "http://www.prnewswire.com/news-releases/promontory-interfinancial-network-clears-a-path-for-community-banks-to-purchase-sofi-loans-300403945.html" on consumer-loan platforms rose 10 per cent in the fourth quarter from the third, according to Orchard, a data and technology firm, breaking a three-quarter run of falls.

Last year SoFi originated $8bn of loans, it said on Friday, up from $5bn in 2015.

The company continues to home in on people in their 20s and 30s with good salaries and good prospects. Mr Cagney has called them  Henrys    short for  high-earning, not rich yet. 

A $600m package of loans sold last month via the securitisation market was a good illustration of the target demographic: borrowers who owed an average of $75,000 had an average age of 34, a credit score of 764   well into super-prime territory   and annual income of $170,000. The dominant schools were New York University, Michigan and Penn State.

SoFi s focus on trying to service this high-achieving cohort by creating a strong sense of community may make it  the next American Express , said Ram Ahluwalia, chief executive of PeerIQ, an analytics firm.

FT WEEKEND SUPPLEMENT - MONEY
The big data  tech revolution set to shake up insurance

By Oliver Ralph 
2,362 words
25 February 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
08
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Filling in forms to arrange cover for a car or health policy may become a thing of the past, as insurers - traditionally resistant to change - find new ways to assess risk, writes Oliver Ralph

Automatic razors, jet packs and traffic jams on the skyways. The Jetsons made all sorts of predictions about life in the distant future. But the HannaBarbera cartoon, made in the 1960s and 1980s, skipped over the question of insurance.

That is hardly surprising. Risk management is rarely a popular topic in science fiction. And in any case, the insurance industry has not always been quick to pick up on new technology. "The entire industry is focused on how life was lived five or 10 years ago," says Andrew Brem, chief digital officer at Aviva.

He is not the only one who feels that way. In a recent survey of insurers by broker Willis Towers Watson, 74 per cent of respondents felt the industry had failed to show leadership in digital innovation.

But change is coming as the industry wakes up to the idea that the cover we might buy 20 years from now will look very different from the cover we need today.

Big insurance companies are pouring millions into innovation and research. Mr Brem's company has set up what it calls a "digital garage" in Hoxton Square in east London to work on new ideas. Axa is putting more than  ¬3bn into IT and digital developments. And others, from Allianz and Munich Re in Europe to Manulife in Canada and XL Catlin in Bermuda, are also investing heavily.

Meanwhile, billions more are going into so-called "insurtech" start-ups that are aiming to challenge the big players. The likes of So-sure, Friendsurance, Lemonade, Guevara, Brolly and a host of others are aiming to disrupt insurance in the same way that Uber, Airbnb, Netflix and Spotify have caused upheaval in other industries. According to CB Insights, $1.7bn went into insurance start-ups last year, across 173 deals.

"Insurance will be enormously different in the future," says Scott Walchek who runs Trov, an insurtech start-up that operates in Australia and the UK. "We're at the beginning of technology being applied to risk management."

How we buy

The first thing that could change over the next two decades is the way we buy insurance. Even with the development of price comparison sites and mobile apps, many people still find the experience time consuming and frustrating.

"Buying insurance is ridiculously retrograde, with endless questions resulting in a quote," says Mr Brem. "We'll be moving to a world where those questions are basically unnecessary. With the use of big data, we are discovering interesting and accurate predictors of risk that do not involve asking people questions."

One example already in use is the way that Aviva prices its car insurance. Traditionally, this involved a lot of questions about the type of car, the location and the driving history. But the company has found a statistical link between the purchase of life insurance policies and safer driving. So life insurance policyholders get lower quotes.

This idea is likely to grow over the next couple of decades. "Not far into the future, the power of the data will mean that people who look the same [to an insurer] now will no longer look the same," says Mr Brem.

Insurers are already experimenting with this. Last year, Admiral wanted to use the language used in Facebook posts to gauge how dangerously a person would drive. Facebook got cold feet over the experiment, but industry experts say that other insurers are likely to try to use data in similarly innovative ways to assess risk.

The result, say insurtech enthusiasts, will be a system that accurately prices risk without burdening the buyer with cumbersome, time-consuming questionnaires. In the Willis Towers Watson survey, 94 per cent of respondents said that distribution would be the area where technology would make the biggest difference over the next five years.

What we buy

What we choose to cover, and how we choose to cover it, is also likely to change radically. Today, we buy car insurance, home insurance and travel insurance that is much the same as it was a couple of decades ago, except that we can now buy online as well as via a broker.

But Mr Walchek believes the policies of the future will bear little resemblance to those of today.

"We still own things that we want to protect and we take risks with them," he says. "What has changed is that life and its risk is being ever more precisely measured. In the past, for example, you might use a postcode to give you an indication of risk. Now it is being measured in feet and inches."

Imagine that mobile phone signals or other sensors detect that a person is about to walk down a road where several people have recently fallen on ice, he says. The insurer will react by either sending a message warning the person to walk more carefully or else automatically increase the premium and cover while the policyholder is walking down that road.

In the shorter term, more precision is likely to mean that insurance will be broken down into easier to digest chunks. The days of the annual policy may be numbered.

Trov is already offering coverage for specific items for limited periods of time. Customers can buy insurance covering a camera for a day, for example.

The same principle is being applied to car insurance, where a common complaint is that, thanks to annual policies, people are paying to insure cars that spend most of their lives sitting in car parks or driveways. Being able to switch policies on and off, the argument goes, will make them much more attractive to occasional users. Cuvva in the UK and Metromile in the US are among those offering pared-down policies.

But not everyone thinks this model will have universal appeal. "The consumer has to make the decision," says Murray Raisbeck, an insurance technology specialist at KPMG. "But consumers don't really want to think about their insurance."

Andrew Rear, chief executive of Munich Re Digital Partners, agrees. "I can't see people wanting to turn their insurance on and off. But one of the things that's interesting about atomisation is that the customer doesn't have to turn the insurance on and off. You can make a decision that you want insurance when you are doing X, and the machine decides when you are doing X [and so switches the cover on]."

Another possibility is that we will be insuring things that, currently, we do not insure at all.

"Over the long term what we'll see is a shift in insurance premiums," says Mr Rear. "You see risks going down in areas where sensor technology can make a difference and help to reduce risk and reduce premiums, but you also get new risks and new sectors that need insuring."

Digital data are one example. "There are virtual assets such as images, or collections of items in games," says Mr Brem. "The digital world is creating a lot of new types of assets."

At the moment, cyber insurance tends to be a corporate policy, covering companies for the consequences of a data breach, for example. But, given the amount of personal data that is now held digitally, some insurers are starting to look at the question of whether there would be a market for personal cyber cover.

On the road

It is not all a question of buying more insurance. In some areas we may find that we need less. Car insurance is one of them.

The question of how to insure driverless cars is the subject of much debate in the industry. If a car has an accident in driverless mode, who is responsible? The latest government proposals suggest that car owners will buy a single policy much as they do now, and the insurer will be able to claim from the manufacturer if it turns out that the car was at fault.

Over the long term, however, the nature of driving could change dramatically. At a recent conference on insuring autonomous vehicles, Lukas Neckermann, a consultant, predicted that: "Over the next five to 10 years, we will move to a point where we don't need to own or drive a car and we'll have mobility on demand. The classes and types of vehicles are going to change significantly to classes that the insurers are not familiar with yet."

That, in turn, will change the nature of car insurance. "Personal motor insurance will decline but fleet insurance will increase," says Mr Raisbeck. "The manufacturers of vehicles may be the providers of insurance."

Prevention not cure

One of the big benefits of autonomous cars is expected to be a reduction in accidents. David Williams, technical director at Axa, says that 90 per cent of accidents are caused by driver error. Fewer accidents should mean fewer insurance claims and hence cheaper insurance. Companies using in-car telematics, such as Insure the Box and Carrot, already try to encourage their policyholders to drive more safely.

Many in the industry hope that a similar principle will start to apply elsewhere. They hope that by using technology, insurers can advise policyholders how to avoid incidents and hence cut the number of claims. The insurer, they hope, will change from being purely a source of claims payments to a trusted adviser.

Alerting the person about to walk down an icy road would be one example of this. But others are already available.

"This has been talked about for a while, but we're at a tipping point, for example with connected homes," says Mr Brem. "I have something called a leakbot at home which tells me if it thinks there may be a leak."

Giving insurers access to vast swaths of personal data is not for everyone, and some companies may offer policies that require less information. But that could carry a financial cost - the less insurers know about the risks they have to cover, the more they will charge for providing that cover.

The progress of telematics policies in car insurance offers an example. Many younger drivers, who can save by allowing insurers to see their driving data, are happy to sign up to telematics policies. However, for older drivers the savings may be less significant because the insurance companies already know a lot about how likely they are to make a claim, and the market for telematics among older drivers has not really taken off.

Health is one area where the potential for monitoring technology is moving ahead fast. South African insurer Discovery, creator of the Vitality programme , uses devices such as Fitbits or other activity trackers to encourage its health insurance customers to exercise more. The more they exercise, the fitter they are and so the cheaper the insurance.

"There's an imbalance - healthcare is over-consumed, and wellness is under-consumed," says Adrian Gore, chief executive of Discovery. "We pioneered the idea of incentivised wellness a decade ago. Now it is part of health policy. There's been a massive change. It's about trying to understand risk factors better, trying to incentivise behavioural change and making the healthcare system more personalised."

In the future, he says, insurers may be able to use genetic data to help tailor health plans for each person, although he warns that the same data should not be used as a way of pricing policies.

The far future

In the dim and distant future, Mr Brem believes we may not even have to think about insurance for individual items. "Our industry has created a false separation between different types of insurance. That is not how I think about my life. I'd like to have a single relationship that I can tailor. I'd like the insurance company to say 'I'm going to evaluate you and here is our recommendation across the piece with a single premium you pay every month'."

Mr Walchek agrees: "There will be a rich brew of data that identifies risk in real time. That changes the way insurance works. Maybe there's a single insurance policy that goes with you at all times - call it a halo - and covers all kinds of risks. The distinction between risks diminishes."

How very Jetsons.

Another way

Peer-to-peer insurance harks back to the past

There is an alternative view of the future of insurance. Rather than using big data to price everybody's risk individually, there is a school of thought that insurance will return to its roots where small groups of people get together to pool their risks.

The modern name for this old concept is peer-to-peer insurance . One major benefit is expected to be a reduction of fraud - people are less likely to put in bogus claims if they think they are hurting their friends, or causes they care about.

US newcomer Lemonade is one of the highest-profile promoters of the idea. It provides home insurance for the rental market, but customers are grouped together by selecting a charity. The premiums of each group go into a pot at the start of the year. Whatever is left at the end goes to the charity. Lemonade launched last September and says that it now has more than 2,200 policies.

Lots of others are working on similar models, including Guevara, So-sure, Friendsurance, Inspeer and Tongjubao. Their models vary. For example, some companies use the money left at the end to reduce premiums for the following year.

Murray Raisbeck, an insurance technology specialist at KPMG, says that there are limits to the model. "If someone can create a platform that allows lots of peer-to-peer niches to emerge then it can work. But individual peer-to-peer pools break down when they get over a couple of hundred people. You need a deep trust between the people in the pool."

Insurance and the big data  technology revolution

Oliver Ralph 
2,346 words
24 February 2017
05:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Automatic razors, jet packs and traffic jams on the skyways. The Jetsons made all sorts of predictions about life in the distant future. But the Hanna-Barbera cartoon, made in the 1960s and 1980s, skipped over the question of insurance.

That is hardly surprising. Risk management is rarely a popular topic in science fiction. And in any case, the insurance industry has not always been quick to pick up on new technology.  The entire industry is focused on how life was lived five or 10 years ago,  says Andrew Brem, chief digital officer at Aviva.

He is not the only one who feels that way. In a recent survey of insurers by broker Willis Towers Watson, 74 per cent of respondents felt the industry had failed to show leadership in digital innovation.

But change is coming as the industry wakes up to the idea that the cover we might buy 20 years from now will look very different from the cover we need today.

Big insurance companies are pouring millions into innovation and research. Mr Brem s company has set up what it calls a  digital garage  in Hoxton Square in east London to work on new ideas. Axa is putting more than  ¬3bn into IT and digital developments. And others, from Allianz and Munich Re in Europe to Manulife in Canada and XL Catlin in Bermuda, are also investing heavily.

Meanwhile, billions more are going into so-called  insurtech  start-ups which are aiming to challenge the big players. The likes of So-sure, Friendsurance, Lemonade, Guevara, Brolly and a host of others are aiming to disrupt insurance in the same way that Uber, Airbnb, Netflix and Spotify have caused upheaval in other industries. According to CB Insights, $1.7bn went into insurance start-ups last year, across 173 deals.

 Insurance will be enormously different in the future,  says Scott Walchek who runs Trov, an insurtech start-up that operates in Australia and the UK.  We re at the beginning of technology being applied to risk management. 

How we buy

The first thing that could change over the next two decades is the way we buy insurance. Even with the development of price comparison sites and mobile apps, many people still find the experience time consuming and frustrating.

 Buying insurance is ridiculously retrograde, with endless questions resulting in a quote,  says Mr Brem.  We ll be moving to a world where those questions are basically unnecessary. With the use of big data, we are discovering interesting and accurate predictors of risk that do not involve asking people questions. 

One example already in use is the way that Aviva prices its car insurance. Traditionally, this involved a lot of questions about the type of car, the location and the driving history. But the company has found a statistical link between the purchase of life insurance policies and safer driving. So life insurance policyholders get lower quotes.

This idea is likely to grow over the next couple of decades.  Not far into the future, the power of the data will mean that people who look the same [to an insurer] now will no longer look the same,  says Mr Brem.

Insurers are already experimenting with this. Last year, Admiral wanted to use the language used in Facebook posts to gauge how dangerously a person would drive. Facebook got cold feet over the experiment, but industry experts say that other insurers are likely to try to use data in similarly innovative ways to assess risk.

The result, say insurtech enthusiasts, will be a system that accurately prices risk without burdening the buyer with cumbersome, time-consuming questionnaires. In the Willis Towers Watson survey, 94 per cent of respondents said that distribution would be the area where technology would make the biggest difference over the next five years.

What we buy

What we choose to cover, and how we choose to cover it, is also likely to change radically. Today, we buy car insurance, home insurance and travel insurance that is much the same as it was a couple of decades ago, except that we can now buy online as well as via a broker.

But Mr Walchek believes the policies of the future will bear little resemblance to those of today.

 We still own things that we want to protect and we take risks with them,  he says.  What has changed is that life and its risk is being ever more precisely measured. In the past, for example, you might use a postcode to give you an indication of risk. Now it is being measured in feet and inches. 

Imagine that mobile phone signals or other sensors detect that a person is about to walk down a road where several people have recently fallen on ice, he says. The insurer will react by either sending a message warning the person to walk more carefully or else automatically increase the premium and cover while the policyholder is walking down that road.

In the shorter term, more precision is likely to mean that insurance will be broken down into easier to digest chunks. The days of the annual policy may be numbered.

Trov is already offering coverage for specific items for limited periods of time. Customers can buy insurance covering a camera for a day, for example.

The same principle is being applied to car insurance, where a common complaint is that, thanks to annual policies, people are paying to insure cars that spend most of their lives sitting in car parks or driveways. Being able to switch policies on and off, the argument goes, will make them much more attractive to occasional users. Cuvva in the UK and Metromile in the US are among those offering pared-down policies.

But not everyone thinks this model will have universal appeal.  The consumer has to make the decision,  says Murray Raisbeck, an insurance technology specialist at KPMG.  But consumers don t really want to think about their insurance. 

Andrew Rear, chief executive of Munich Re Digital Partners, agrees.  I can t see people wanting to turn their insurance on and off. But one of the things that s interesting about atomisation is that the customer doesn t have to turn the insurance on and off. You can make a decision that you want insurance when you are doing X, and the machine decides when you are doing X [and so switches the cover on]. 

Another possibility is that we will be insuring things that, currently, we do not insure at all.

 Over the long term what we ll see is a shift in insurance premiums,  says Mr Rear.  You see risks going down in areas where sensor technology can make a difference and help to reduce risk and reduce premiums, but you also get new risks and new sectors that need insuring. 

Digital data are one example.  There are virtual assets such as images, or collections of items in games,  says Mr Brem.  The digital world is creating a lot of new types of assets. 

At the moment, cyber insurance tends to be a corporate policy, covering companies for the consequences of a data breach, for example. But, given the amount of personal data that is now held digitally, some insurers are starting to look at the question of whether there would be a market for personal cyber cover.

On the road

It is not all a question of buying more insurance. In some areas we may find that we need less. Car insurance is one of them. The question of how to insure driverless cars is the subject of much debate in the industry. If a car has an accident in driverless mode, who is responsible? The latest government proposals suggest that car owners will buy a single policy much as they do now, and the insurer will be able to claim from the manufacturer if it turns out that the car was at fault.

Over the long term, however, the nature of driving could change dramatically. At a recent conference on insuring autonomous vehicles, Lukas Neckermann, a consultant, predicted that:  Over the next five to 10 years, we will move to a point where we don t need to own or drive a car and we ll have mobility on demand. The classes and types of vehicles are going to change significantly to classes that the insurers are not familiar with yet. 

That, in turn, will change the nature of car insurance.  Personal motor insurance will decline but fleet insurance will increase,  says Mr Raisbeck.  The manufacturers of vehicles may be the providers of insurance. 

Prevention not cure

One of the big benefits of autonomous cars is expected to be a reduction in accidents. David Williams, technical director at Axa, says that 90 per cent of accidents are caused by driver error. Fewer accidents should mean fewer insurance claims and hence cheaper insurance. Companies using in-car telematics, such as Insure the Box and Carrot, already try to encourage their policyholders to drive more safely.

Many in the industry hope that a similar principle will start to apply elsewhere. They hope that by using technology, insurers can advise policyholders how to avoid incidents and hence cut the number of claims. The insurer, they hope, will change from being purely a source of claims payments to a trusted adviser.

Alerting the person about to walk down an icy road would be one example of this. But others are already available.

 This has been talked about for a while, but we re at a tipping point, for example with connected homes,  says Mr Brem.  I have something called a leakbot at home which tells me if it thinks there may be a leak. 

Giving insurers access to vast swaths of personal data is not for everyone, and some companies may offer policies that require less information. But that could carry a financial cost   the less insurers know about the risks they have to cover, the more they will charge for providing that cover.

The progress of telematics policies in car insurance offers an example. Many younger drivers, who can save by allowing insurers to see their driving data, are happy to sign up to telematics policies. However, for older drivers the savings may be less significant because the insurance companies already know a lot about how likely they are to make a claim, and the market for telematics among older drivers has not really taken off.

Health is one area where the potential for monitoring technology is moving ahead fast. South African insurer Discovery, creator of the Vitality programme, uses devices such as Fitbits or other activity trackers to encourage its health insurance customers to exercise more. The more they exercise, the fitter they are and so the cheaper the insurance.

 There s an imbalance   healthcare is over-consumed, and wellness is under-consumed,  says Adrian Gore, chief executive of Discovery.  We pioneered the idea of incentivised wellness a decade ago. Now it is part of health policy. There s been a massive change. It s about trying to understand risk factors better, trying to incentivise behavioural change and making the healthcare system more personalised. 

In the future, he says, insurers may be able to use genetic data to help tailor health plans for each person, although he warns that the same data should not be used as a way of pricing policies.

The far future

In the dim and distant future, Mr Brem believes we may not even have to think about insurance for individual items.  Our industry has created a false separation between different types of insurance. That is not how I think about my life. I d like to have a single relationship that I can tailor. I d like the insurance company to say  I m going to evaluate you and here is our recommendation across the piece with a single premium you pay every month . 

Mr Walchek agrees:  There will be a rich brew of data that identifies risk in real time. That changes the way insurance works. Maybe there s a single insurance policy that goes with you at all times   call it a halo   and covers all kinds of risks. The distinction between risks diminishes. 

How very Jetsons.

Phone a friend

There is an alternative view of the future of insurance. Rather than using big data to price everybody s risk individually, there is a school of thought that says insurance will return to its roots where small groups of people get together to pool their risks.

The modern name for this old concept is peer-to-peer insurance. One of the main benefits is expected to be a reduction of fraud   people are less likely to put in bogus claims if they think they are hurting their friends, or causes they care about.

US newcomer Lemonade is one of the highest profile promoters of the idea. It provides home insurance for the rental market, but customers are grouped together by selecting a charity. The premiums of each group go into a pot at the start of the year. Whatever is left at the end goes to the charity. Lemonade launched last September and says that it already has more than 2,200 policies.

Lots of others are working on similar models, including Guevara, So-sure, Friendsurance, Inspeer and Tongjubao. The models they are using vary. For example, the money left at the end does not always go to a charity. Some companies use it to reduce premiums for the following year.

One thing that unites most, however, is using the peer-to-peer concept to reduce the mistrust that has often existed between insurers and policyholders.

Mr Raisbeck says that there are limits to the model.  If someone can create a platform that allows lots of peer to peer niches to emerge then it can work. But individual peer to peer pools break down when they get over a couple of hundred people. You need a deep trust between the people in the pool.

Deng's Take: China's Open Markets Much Talk, Little Action

Trump Team Broadens Search for Fed Regulatory Post

WSJ Pro Interactive: Opening China's Financial Markets

Treasury's Mnuchin Raises Concerns Over Bank Lending, Liquidity

U.K. Banks Unlikely to Keep Full EU Access, Bundesbank Official Says

China's Open Markets Much Talk, Little Action

After agreeing to uphold the "One China" policy "https://www.wsj.com/articles/donald-trump-affirms-commitment-to-one-china-policy-in-call-with-xi-jinping-1486699771" with Chinese President Xi Jinping, U.S. President Donald Trump is moving closer to finally starting a discussion with Beijing over its trade and currency practices. As relations between the new administration and China take hold, however, there are several areas that Mr. Trump has yet to recognize publicly as sore points for the U.S. One of them: Wall Street firms' limited access to China's financial sector.

China joined the World Trade Organization in 2001. After a five-year grace period, the country was supposed to open its doors wider to foreign banks. While that happened on paper, in reality rules have made it difficult for foreign banks to penetrate the market. The share of foreign banking operations amounted to just 1.4% of China's total banking assets in 2015, according the China Banking Regulatory Commission. It was 2.4% in 2007.

In practice, foreign banks must create separate companies to operate in China and face difficulties opening branches.

"As a result, foreign banks have almost no retail banking business, and they can't utilize their more efficient banking services to compete head to head against China's own banks," said Scott Kennedy of the Center for Strategic and International Studies, a Washington think tank. "There are similar constraints still in place for the securities business, credit ratings, insurance and credit cards."

Discussions about a bilateral investment treaty started in 2008 and have "https://www.wsj.com/articles/china-vows-to-cut-red-tape-to-boost-foreign-investment-1475993079" without a deal. It isn't clear whether such talks will continue under Mr. Trump.

Meanwhile, repeated assurances by Chinese regulators to allow the world greater access to the country's finance industry are getting stale.

The National Development and Reform Commission"https://www.wsj.com/articles/china-to-ease-curbs-on-foreign-investment-in-finance-1483094113" in December. Then, the State Council said it would ease investment curbs, including in banking, securities, futures, fund management and insurance. Its statement was published Jan. 17, the same day President Xi Jinping"https://www.wsj.com/articles/xi-jinping-in-davos-making-the-most-of-a-waning-era-1484560806" at the World Economic Forum in Davos, Switzerland. Critics quickly pointed out the irony of an economic nationalist declaring China's openness to the world.

Beijing has kept its finance sector well protected because banks are its major conduit for implementing economic policy. By and large, funds flow to China's corporate sector through state-owned banks, not the securities market.

A foreign commercial bank can own up to 25% of a domestic commercial bank, while a foreign investment bank can own up to 49% of a securities joint venture. For example, HSBC Holdings PLC has a 19.9% stake in Bank of Communications Co., while UBS Group AG and Morgan Stanley have been "https://www.wsj.com/articles/morgan-stanley-and-ubs-to-increase-stakes-in-mainland-china-joint-ventures-1483883686" in their securities joint ventures to 49%, from about 25% and about 33%, respectively.

Other areas of finance are up for review. "https://www.wsj.com/articles/china-underwhelms-with-market-opening-plan-1481196590" of revised rules for opening up markets loosened restrictions on the credit-rating industry.

Currently, foreign firms can rate domestic securities only through joint ventures something the likes of Moody's Investors Service, Fitch Ratings and S&P Global Ratings have been reluctant to do.

Most China observers aren't hopeful about meaningful change to the finance sector, given that Mr. Trump has focused his complaints toward China on its trade and currency practices.

"I don't see China taking a pre-emptive action to further open up its banking market to foreign banks at this stage, given already heightened trade tensions, particularly with the U.S." said Matthew Phillips, a Hong Kong-based financial-services partner at PwC. "Trade is going to drive the dynamic between the two countries and until China sees the lay of the land, it might not want to show its hand and further open access to a critical area."

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

Trump Team Broadens Search for Fed Regulatory Post

By Ryan Tracy and Andrew Ackerman

WASHINGTON The Trump administration has broadened its search for a key regulatory job at the Federal Reserve, according to people familiar with the matter, meeting in recent weeks with at least two people about the post of Fed vice chair in charge of bank oversight.

President Donald Trump hasn't announced who he will nominate to the currently vacant post, and his decision won't be final until that happens. He also has at least two other seats on the Fed's seven-member board to fill. One of those is currently vacant, and the other will be open once Fed governor Daniel Tarullo, currently the de facto chief of bank oversight, resigns in early April.

The administration is still said to be considering David Nason, a former Treasury Department official in the administration of President George W. Bush and currently an executive in the financing arm of General Electric Co. Mr. Nason had emerged as the front-runner for the job weeks ago, people familiar with the matter said at the time.

President Trump's team also recently met with Richard Davis, the chief executive of U.S. Bancorp, and Hal Scott, a professor at Harvard Law School, according to people familiar with the matter.

A spokesman for Mr. Davis, responding to an inquiry on Thursday, said "Richard is not a candidate for the Fed position. He is focused on a successful CEO transition in April." U.S. Bank said in January Mr. Davis will be stepping down from the CEO role in April, while remaining on as executive chairman.

Mr. Scott declined to comment. The White House had no immediate comment. A GE spokeswoman had no comment.

The meetings provide more evidence the administration is looking at candidates who will take a less aggressive regulatory posture than the Fed did under the Obama administration, in line with the desire of Mr. Trump and his advisers to pare back regulations spurred by the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial law.

Mr. Scott has publicly criticized some of the Fed's regulatory moves, for instance by suggesting capital requirements are too strict and calling on the central bank to disclose more about its annual "stress tests" of banks' resilience and "living wills" that outline how they would fail without taxpayer support. He is director of the Committee on Capital Markets Regulation, a group of financial industry representatives and academics that comments on regulatory policy, often in ways critical of the Fed.

Mr. Davis has been chief executive of Minneapolis-based U.S. Bank since 2006. His bank, one of the country's largest, has dealt with many of the new rules and stricter enforcement under the Obama administration, though he hasn't railed against them in public as strongly as some other bankers.

Mr. Nason, for his part, appears to be getting a push from people with access to the highest levels of the administration.

On Thursday, Mr. Trump was meeting with manufacturing CEOs at the White House. As the group was waiting for the president within earshot of reporters, Jeffrey Immelt, chief executive of GE, greeted Treasury Secretary Steven Mnuchin and said, "Secretary, just wanted to tell you Dave Nason is a great guy."

 Nick Timiraos and Ted Mann contributed to this article.

WSJ Pro: Treasury's Mnuchin Raises Concerns Over Bank Lending, Liquidity

By Ryan Tracy

WASHINGTON Treasury Secretary Steven Mnuchin raised concerns about banks' ability to meet lending demands on Thursday, suggesting rules affecting bank holdings of cash and securities could be in the Trump administration's crosshairs.

"There's an extraordinary amount of liquidity trapped in the system at banks," Mr. Mnuchin said in an interview on Fox Business Network, echoing a comment he made on CNBC earlier in the day. "We're all for banks being highly regulated but we want to make sure that banks are lending. That is the No. 1 priority for us."

Mr. Mnuchin didn't specify which rules he was blaming for the problem he perceived. Regulators have adopted several new rules targeting banks' balance sheets in recent years, an effort to prevent them from taking the outsize risks that had destabilized large firms during the financial crisis. As Treasury secretary, Mr. Mnuchin doesn't have final say over regulatory policy, but he is helping to pick the officials who will.

One set of rules, including the so-called liquidity coverage ratio, forces large banks to hold a minimum amount of cash or securities that could be quickly sold, so they would be able to pay their bills in the event of a sudden credit crunch. The restriction means banks can't dedicate that cash to loans or other investments, a downside regulators have so far been willing to accept in the name of a safer financial system.

The Volcker rule, which bans banks from trading for their own account, has also been blamed for affecting market liquidity, or the ability of investors to buy and sell securities quickly at a desired price. Critics say the rule is a big reason why banks have reduced their trading activity, while defenders of the rule point to other factors, such as the rise of electronic trading by non-bank firms.

Mr. Mnuchin has criticized the Volcker rule in the past and did so again in the Fox interview, saying it has "hurt liquidity" and that policy makers need to clarify the definition of banned trading.

Meanwhile, an official at the Federal Reserve Bank of New York signaled in a speech Thursday an openness to discussing whether post-crisis rules have struck the right balance between safety and economic growth.

"In my view, it is entirely appropriate to continuously evaluate and rigorously assess the impact to ensure that the observed outcomes are consistent with stated objectives, such as greater resilience, efficient financial intermediation over time, and financial stability," said Kevin Stiroh, the New York Fed's head of supervision.

He pointed specifically to liquidity and capital rules, which he said can increase banks' safety while reducing their ability to provide financial services. "A firm holding a large buffer of low-return, highly liquid assets might be stronger from a liquidity perspective, but might have less lending capacity," he said.

Mr. Stiroh also defended post-crisis rules, saying the financial system is much more resilient than it used to be.

The New York Fed doesn't have final say over rules for banks, but it enforces regulations adopted in Washington and participates in the development of new rules.

WSJ Pro: Regulators Issue Guidance Aimed at Robo Investment Advisers

By Dave Michaels

U.S. market regulators on Thursday issued their first set of guidelines aimed at robo advisers, a burgeoning class of money managers that have sometimes come under fire for whether they're sufficiently clear about how their platforms work.

Robo advisers such as Betterment LLC and Wealthfront Inc. use algorithms to analyze a client's demographic profile and investment goals in order to build a portfolio for them. Humans aren't involved in picking assets and, as a result, the firms can undercut other brokers and money managers by selling their services at a lower cost.

The Securities and Exchange Commission, in guidance issued Thursday, said lack of human interaction means robo advisers should take special care to disclose how they operate, including whether they can cut off clients' ability to trade during bouts of market turmoil. Betterment faced criticism last year for suspending all trading for institutional customers during the mayhem that followed the U.K.'s decision to leave the European Union.

"As technology continues to improve and make profound changes to the financial services industry, it's important for regulators to assess its impact on U.S. markets and give thoughtful guidance to market participants," said SEC Acting Chairman Michael Piwowar.

Betterment, which manages $7.8 billion in assets, has said it halted trading to protect investors from paying higher transaction costs associated with buying and selling securities when volatility spikes. Some Betterment clients said they weren't informed about the halt. The firm has updated a required regulatory disclosure to provide greater detail around its trading discretion.

"We appreciate the SEC's thoughtful and rigorous analysis of the space," said Betterment spokesman Joe Ziemer. "We think there is valuable guidance for the companies providing these types of services and for investors considering them."

Investors who use robo advisers typically answer brief questionnaires that provide the firm with factors on which to base a diversified portfolio. But sometimes clients provide inconsistent answers, and not all firms try to resolve that, the SEC said. The SEC said robo advisers might want to use "alerts" to inform investors when their answers conflict.

Robo advisers may also need to implement policies that help detect and protect against cybersecurity threats, the SEC said..

WSJ Pro: Business Roundtable Urges White House to Roll Back Financial Rules

By Gabriel T. Rubin

WASHINGTON A trade group representing large U.S. companies urged the White House to repeal or modify a slew of regulations, including several that affect financial services firms.

The letter from the Business Roundtable, which was sent a day in advance of a meeting between President Donald Trump and manufacturing executives on Thursday, identifies four financial regulations the executives and their members would like to see rolled back. The letter was addressed to Gary Cohn, the former Goldman Sachs Group Inc. president who now heads Mr. Trump's National Economic Council.

The Business Roundtable called on the Trump administration to overhaul the shareholder proposal process to pare down the ability of "activist investors with insignificant stakes in public companies" to "pursue social or political agendas" and to reconsider a Dodd-Frank Act requirement for firms to disclose CEO pay as a ratio of average employee pay.

Both of those rules are under the jurisdiction of the Securities and Exchange Commission, as is a rule to require public companies to disclose whether their products contain "conflict minerals" originating in certain central African countries. The Business Roundtable asks that that rule be reconsidered as well.

They also requested a "transitional period" in order to comply with new margin requirements for over-the-counter derivatives.

The Trump administration has made deregulation a key part of its governing agenda, releasing an executive order shortly after Mr. Trump took office that called on federal agencies to eliminate two regulations for every new one created.

The Business Roundtable's requests followed the unveiling of Trump administration principles earlier in February on how to overhaul the financial regulatory system. Mr. Trump has directed the Treasury Department, under Secretary Steven Mnuchin, to undertake a broad review of financial rules as part of the administration's efforts to spur economic growth.

U.K. Banks Unlikely to Keep Full EU Access, Bundesbank Official Says

By Paul Hannon

U.K.-based bank operations are unlikely to retain access to the European Union's single market after Brexit and a transition period to allow for a measured relocation process would be desirable, a senior Bundesbank official said Friday.

The U.K. voted in June 2016 to leave the EU and the government is expected to trigger a two-year period of exit talks next month. In January, U.K. Prime Minister Theresa May said the U.K. intended to leave the EU's single market and would instead seek a new free-trade agreement to allow the country to continue selling goods and services easily to EU member states.

However, Andreas Dombret, a member of the German central bank's executive board who is responsible for financial regulation, said that would make it unlikely that U.K.-based banks would retain a "passport" that would allow them to sell services in the EU.

"It seems that the prospects for EU market access through the U.K. look rather dim," said Mr. Dombret, speaking at the opening of a London office by Zeb, a German consultancy focused on financial services.

The U.K. could seek a status known as equivalence, under which the EU would recognize that its regulatory regime was the same as its own and its regulated institutions would therefore gain access to the bloc. However, Mr. Dombret said this status was likely to be too uncertain for banks making long-term location decisions. He added that there was no precedent for financial services being "fully integrated" in a free-trade agreement between the EU and another country.

Mr. Dombret warned that a transition period to allow banks a longer period in which to move some operations out of London might prove politically difficult, even if it made economic sense.

He said it was even possible that the two years of negotiations could end without an agreement, leaving the U.K. to exit from the bloc without any special deal an outcome that would be economically damaging to both the U.K. and the EU.

"While economic policy will of course be an important topic during negotiations, we should not count on economic sanity being the main guiding principle," he said.

Bankers worry that such an outcome would interrupt access to complex financial instruments for businesses and investors around Europe, since the provision of those products is concentrated in London.

Mr. Dombrets acknowledged that a sudden halt in the availability of products that allow businesses and investors to manage currency and other risks could threaten financial stability across the continent. But he said he was confident financial institutions would prepare for every possible outcome.

"We are working under the assumption that the financial sector will adapt to whatever outcome the negotiations will yield," he said. "It is not a pressing financial stability risk."

Mr. Dombret also warned against the U.K. easing regulation to retain banks, or other parts of Europe doing so to attract institutions that are on the move.

"We must avoid a regulatory race to the bottom at all costs," he said.

U.S. Renews Real-Estate Data Targeting Order

The U.S. Treasury Department extended for another six months a data-collection program to aid a crackdown on money laundering in real estate.

The program, which began in March 2016 and was expanded in August, requires title insurance companies to identify the true owners of limited liability companies that buy luxury real estate in all-cash transactions in some large cities, including New York City, Los Angeles, San Francisco and Miami. It was set to end Thursday; the renewal expires Aug. 22.

Treasury's Financial Crimes Enforcement Network, or FinCEN, which issued the directives, known as geographic targeting orders, said in a statement that the data collected thus far corroborate the agency's concerns about the use of shell companies to buy high-end property.

Acting FinCEN director Jamal El-Hindi said in the statement that the geographic-targeting orders are "producing valuable data" that's assisting law enforcement and are "serving to inform our future efforts to address money laundering in the real-estate sector."

Stephen Hudak, a FinCEN spokesman, declined to comment on any future plans to address the issue. Risk & Compliance Journal reported last year on the orders representing the first step toward broader anti-money laundering compliance requirements for the real-estate industry, which had previously fended off such efforts from the federal government after the Sept. 11, 2001, terrorist attacks.

The goal, Treasury says, is to make it more difficult for buyers, particularly foreigners, to launder money through U.S. property purchases. About one-third of the transactions covered by the orders involve a person who is already the subject of a suspicious-activity report, FinCEN said.

Treasury says the orders impose the requirements on title-insurance companies because such insurance is a common feature in the vast majority of real-estate transactions, including those that don't involve banks, which would perform checks for money-laundering risks when considering whether to fund a mortgage. As such, Treasury says, the title insurance companies "can provide FinCEN with valuable information" about transactions of concern.

Michelle Korsmo, chief executive of the American Land Title Association, a trade group representing the companies covered by the orders, said the efforts of their member for the past year "appear to be beneficial" to the federal government's work identifying money-laundering schemes in real-estate purchases.

"We continue to work closely with our members and FinCEN to collect the needed information as efficiently as possible," said Ms. Korsmo.

FinCEN collects and analyzes suspicious-activity reports filed by financial institutions, and Mr. Hudak said banks have also been filing more reports related to potential money laundering involving real estate as a result of the attention generated by the geographic targeting orders.

Mr. Hudak said the orders flagged, among others, beneficial owners suspected of being involved in public corruption in Asia and South America, and a beneficial owner who engaged in $160 million of suspicious financial activity and sought to disguise ownership of related financial accounts. He declined to provide further detail.

At some point, said Andrew Ittleman, a partner at Fuerst Ittleman David & Joseph PL, FinCEN will have to start the process of writing regulations to permanently formalize the process.

"Kicking the can down the road isn't the way this is supposed to be addressed. It's not the way federal agencies are supposed to create rules," he said.

Capital One Discloses Probe Into Anti-Money Laundering Program

By AnnaMaria Andriotis

Capital One Financial Corp. is under investigation for its anti-money-laundering program, according to the company's annual securities filing.

The bank disclosed Thursday that it is under investigation by the New York District Attorney's Office, the Justice Department and the Financial Crimes Enforcement Network of the Treasury Department over "certain former check casher clients" of its commercial banking business and over its anti-money-laundering program.

The bank's November filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission said it had received subpoenas and requests for testimony, but not that it was being investigated.

A Capital One spokeswoman said the bank used different words in the filing to describe the same situation.

As previously disclosed, Capital One has also been the subject of an open consent order with the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency since July 2015 over regulatory deficiencies in its anti-money-laundering program.

The OCC action has posed a hurdle for Capital One's planned acquisition of the Cabela's Inc. credit-card portfolio, raising the possibility another bank will snatch that deal away. That deal was announced last year when Bass Pro Shops agreed to purchase Cabela's. Capital One agreed to buy the credit-card business as part of the deal.

On Capital One's earnings call last month, Chief Executive Richard Fairbank said the bank didn't expect to receive regulatory approval for the purchase before Oct. 3. That is when Cabela's and Bass Pros Shops can walk away from the transaction. He added that the bank wouldn't be able to refile its application for the deal until after "we have completed our work under the AML consent order."

WSJ Pro: Market Talk: Daily Analysis Roundup

Brazil's Credit Market Likely to Recover in 2H

The amount of credit in Brazil's financial system, which started this year declining, is expected to recover some ground in 2H, says the economist team at Banco de Tokyo-Mitsubishi UFJ Brasil. "Banks will continue adopting a cautious approach on lending (mainly in the first half) due to the risk of higher delinquency rates by individuals and by companies. But, there is scope for some acceleration of credit supply from the second half of this year on, following higher economic growth rate," it says. Credit totaled BRL3.07T ($1T) in January, down 3.9% from the same month in 2016. (rogerio.jelmayer@wsj.com)

Regulators Grant Reprieve on New Margin Requirements

US and European regulators grant a six month grace period to smaller financial firms that don't yet have paperwork ready to meet new margin requirements set to kick-in on March 1 for certain swaps transactions. Banks have complained that some of their smaller clients -- including pension funds and insurance firms -- aren't yet ready to begin posting so-called variation margin. US bank regulators and European policy makers agreed to the grace period a week after the CFTC adopted a similar move. "The final rule incorporated a phase-in period for swap entities to begin exchanging variation margin with their swap counterparties," the US banking regulators said in a statement. "The phase-in period gave markets and firms time to adjust to the new requirements, which were adopted in October 2015." (andrew.ackerman@wsj.com; @amacker)

Bank Execs Await Loan-Loss Rule

Executives at US banks say they're getting ready for a new accounting rule that will revamp how they treat loan losses, but they expect it to cause increases in their loan impairments that will cut into their regulatory capital, a Deloitte & Touche survey says. The rule, which banks will start adopting as soon as 2019, will require them to book losses on soured loans much faster, and is expected to require some banks to set aside more in reserves for loan losses. Seventy percent of the 31 banks responding to the Deloitte survey say they've started preparations for the new approach, and majorities say they expect their impairments of mortgages, consumer loans and commercial loans to rise by at least 10%. As a result, majorities of those surveyed anticipate declines in their regulatory-capital ratios, and more than 80% expect their earnings to become more volatile under the new model. (michael.rapoport@wsj.com; @rapoportwsj)

New China Framework to Hit W-M Products

A new framework by Chinese financial regulators aims to clamp down on wealth-management products, a longtime goal of authorities. Banks have been hiding such products off their balance sheets, leaving room on their books to make more loans. NSBO Research notes wealth-management products generally banned by banking regulators from investing in commercial property. The products, though, have been able to do so "by investing in asset-management products sold by brokers or fund companies' subsidiaries--which are not prohibited from these kind of investments" by securities regulators. But the new framework, led by China's central bank and yet to be approved by top leaders, would ban asset-management products from investing in other ones. How effective implementation would be is key. (chao.deng@wsj.com; @chao_deng)

China's Coordinated Move on Asset Managers

Chinese financial regulators have come up with a draft framework on overseeing the country's near-$9 trillion asset-management industry. Among the new requirements, firms would have put aside reserves equal to 10% of product-management fees and be barred from promising guaranteed returns. Gavekal Research describes the work a "rare coordinated move by the People's Bank of China--together with the banking, securities and insurance regulators"--which "signals a more-joined-up approach to financial supervision" in China's efforts "to curb the rapid expansion of" shadow finance there. At the end of 2016, banks' exposure to off-balance-sheet wealth-management products totaled CNY26 trillion, 30% higher than a year earlier. "Under the draft rules, financial institutions--which have previously found ways to arbitrage China's fragmented regulatory environment--will be subject to coordinated supervision and tighter restrictions," says Gavekal. (chao.deng@wsj.com; @chao_deng)

 Tailored' Accounting Takes Companies Into Alternate Reality

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/tailored-accounting-takes-companies-into-alternate-reality-1487869300?tesla=y", some companies are pushing alternative accounting.

The Securities and Exchange Commission is increasingly calling out companies that offer a different flavor of "non-GAAP" accounting reporting their numbers as if they could calculate them using assumptions or practices not permitted under generally accepted accounting principles. This goes beyond the SEC's crusade against companies using more traditional tactics like stripping out costs from their customized measures, generally making their numbers look better than under GAAP.

The ECB's Stress Test Framework

In a "http://voxeu.org/article/macroprudential-stress-tests-new-analytical-tool", European Central Bank President Vitor Constncio lays out a new framework for designing the stress tests used to inform macroprudential policies at the eurozone regulator. The framework sets out five requirements for successful stress tests.

Tom Perez's Bank-Friendly Record a Bad Fit for DNC

The Obama administration's Justice Department focused on financial settlements rather than criminal prosecutions in these cases, a position that is out of step with the opinions of the Democratic Party's base, he says.

Deng's Take: China's Open Markets Much Talk, Little Action
