Tech
Instagram Seeks Engineering Staff From Wall Street; Facebook's photo-sharing app is looking for  recovering quants' from hedge funds or investment banks

By Deepa Seetharaman 
536 words
15 February 2017
16:24
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Attention Wall Street: Instagram is looking to hire.

Facebook Inc.'s photo-sharing app plans to double its engineering staff in New York this year to 150 people. In particular, it is looking for machine-learning experts, many of whom work for hedge funds or investment banks. These "recovering quants" as Instagram co-founder Mike Krieger calls them will allow Instagram to more personally tailor its feed and other features to its 600 million users.

The move signals a new stage of growth for Instagram. The expansion of its New York office which it expects will have half of the company's engineers by the end of next year means Instagram can "tackle a broader portfolio of things within the product," Mr. Krieger said. Instagram is adding more employees more rapidly than ever; it has already outgrown its brand-new offices in Menlo Park, Calif., which opened just last fall.

The amped-up presence in New York is unusual for Facebook, which likes to keep the bulk of its engineers close to the group's headquarters. But other factors, including tighter competition for engineers in Silicon Valley, prompted Instagram to look elsewhere. The company also has a big base in Seattle, Amazon.com Inc.'s hometown.

Most of Instagram's New York-based engineers will be experts in machine learning, a branch of artificial intelligence that enables computers to learn from enormous data sets with minimal human input.

Machine-learning and personalization are at the heart of some of Instagram's top projects, such as "http://blogs.wsj.com/digits/2016/03/15/instagram-testing-new-algorithm-driven-feed/" that replaced its reverse-chronological feed with a ranked feed, reordered with posts Instagram predicted users would find interesting at the top. Facebook has had a ranked feed since 2006.

Instagram also uses machine learning to assemble its "Top Live" feature, found on the Explore tab, which shows a montage of the best live-videos on Instagram world-wide. Instagram's safety team uses the technology to detect abusive comments.

These tools came in handy "https://www.wsj.com/articles/instagram-unveils-instagram-stories-1470146403", a feature that lets users post photos and videos that vanish after 24 hours. The order of the stories at the top of users' feeds is ranked based on Instagram's predictions of what a user will find most interesting.

Instagram is also exploring a way to create thematic Stories, centered around a current event such as last month's Women's March or a topic like cooking, using machine learning, said Blake Barnes, Instagram's product management director overseeing the New York expansion. That effort, which would allow it to personalize thematic stories for each user, would be powered by machine learning.

Snapchat, the disappearing-chat app owned by Snap Inc., has a similar feature through its own Stories tab, but it is hand-curated.

"The arc for us is to try to figure out how people can see what else is going on in the world that's relevant to them," Mr. Barnes said.

Michaels's Take: A Second Bite at the Cuban Apple

The Dodd-Frank Rule Banks Want to Keep

Trump Gives Impetus to Revamp of Sarbanes-Oxley Auditor Rule

Steven Mnuchin Confirmed as Treasury Secretary

Regional Bank CEOs Seek Review of Postcrisis Rules

A Second Bite at the Cuban Apple

As the Securities and Exchange Commission pursues its latest big insider-trading battle against hedge-fund manager Leon Cooperman, the shadow of Mark Cuban looms in plain sight.

Recall that the SEC in 2013 took Mr. Cuban to trial for alleged insider trading, and that "https://www.wsj.com/news/articles/SB10001424052702303680404579139774081561730" decisively. The "https://www.wsj.com/articles/SB122693827604333637" that Mr. Cuban traded on confidential information by selling his stake in an Internet-search company, Mamma.com , in 2004 after being told about the firm's planned offering of new shares, which was likely to trigger a decline in the stock's price.

The Cuban case partly turned on whether the billionaire investor had agreed not to sell his stock when he learned about the new offering. A Dallas jury ultimately decided the answer was no.

In September, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/sec-charges-leon-cooperman-with-insider-trading-1474470504", the founder of Omega Advisors Inc ., of similarly agreeing to refrain from trading on confidential information that he received. As in the Cuban case, the SEC alleges that by violating that agreement, Mr. Cooperman broke securities laws. He has denied wrongdoing.

At a federal court hearing last week, Mr. Cooperman's lawyers argued the SEC's case doesn't hold up if the agency can't show the agreement preceded the passing of information. (The SEC disputes this.) If the information was freely given by the company, and then Mr. Cooperman agreed not to trade but did, well then that's just a broken promise, his lawyers argue. But it's not insider trading.

Like Mr. Cuban's case, the Cooperman claims appear headed to trial. The outcome could turn on the credibility of the SEC's witnesses and Mr. Cooperman's explanation for his trades in Atlas Pipeline Partners LP . In the Cuban case, the SEC suffered because its key witness the executive who told Mr. Cuban about the offering declined to testify in person.

An SEC victory could erase some of the shame of losing to Mr. Cuban, who after winning went on the warpath against the agency. Mr. Cuban crashed an SEC legal conference in Washington and trolled the agency's chairman with a tweetstorm, saying she showed a "lack of self awareness."

A win also could buttress a theory of insider trading that wasn't resolved in the Cuban battle: namely, that traders commit fraud if they vow to not trade on material nonpublic information and then break that promise. The SEC says one of its rules plainly labels this as fraud, but the agency's view hasn't been addressed by a court decision, said Jill Fisch, a securities law expert at the University of Pennsylvania Law School. A court decision affirming the SEC's view would further expand what constitutes insider trading, which isn't defined in the law and has evolved over time through judicial decisions.

At "https://www.wsj.com/articles/judge-questions-coopermans-bid-to-get-insider-trading-suit-dismissed-1486498437", the two sides faced off over how that rule should apply. The hedge-fund manager's lawyers said it doesn't fit Mr. Cooperman's case, while the SEC said the defense was trying to create a "loophole" in the rule where none exists.

"I think the SEC would like to win on a case like this because they would like to establish the legal issues, and would like a judge to hold that it's valid," Ms. Fisch said. "Part of the reason the SEC fought so hard in Cuban is because it wanted to establish...that this was a legitimate theory of insider trading."

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

WSJ Pro First: The Dodd-Frank Rule Banks Want to Keep

By Ryan Tracy

Republican promises to dismantle the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial overhaul may prove good news for many financial firms. The bad news for Wall Street: A top target is a provision big banks would rather retain.

"Orderly liquidation authority," or OLA, was an answer to the 2008 bailouts of Wall Street firms during the financial crisis. It empowers the federal government to take over and wind down a failing financial firm. Conservatives have sought for years to repeal OLA. Now Trump administration officials are talking about doing so, providing new momentum.

OLA is a particularly ripe target with partisan tensions boiling on Capitol Hill. That is the result of quirks in congressional budget rules, which say that because OLA repeal has an impact on the federal budget, it could be passed with a simple majority vote and clear the Senate with only GOP votes.

In contrast, other Republican-favored changes to Dodd-Frank, such as stripping the government of powers to extend more stringent oversight to big nonbank firms, would require some Democratic support to clear a higher vote hurdle.

Killing OLA has also been scored as creating $15.2 billion in budget savings over 10 years, according to the Congressional Budget Office  about the price tag of other priorities, such as President Donald Trump's planned border wall with Mexico.

"We are going to probably at some point talk about whether OLA works and whether OLA should ever be triggered, because no one thinks that we really solved  too big to fail,'" White House National Economic Council Director Gary Cohn said in a Wall Street Journal interview, referring to the crisis-era conclusions banks had to be bailed out. He said the administration was contemplating an unspecified executive action related to the policy.

Steven Mnuchin, who was confirmed as Treasury secretary Monday, told lawmakers at a Jan. 19 hearing that "I think if we have proper regulation, a lot of the need for Title II also goes away," a reference to the portion of the law that lays out OLA.

Many in the financial industry disagree, especially big banks who fear an OLA replacement would be more draconian regulation, both in the U.S. and in other countries where officials view OLA as an important backstop.

The Clearing House Association of large banks says OLA is a low-cost "Plan B" now that banks are better prepared for bankruptcy than before the financial crisis, citing "living will" bankruptcy plans that banks such as J.P. Morgan Chase & Co . and Wells Fargo & Co . have been working on for years.

Tim Pawlenty, chief executive of the Financial Services Roundtable, said the trade group thinks "some reasonable adjustments would improve" Dodd-Frank, but added: "We don't think Title II is in need of change."

The debate over OLA boils down to a fight over how to handle financial crises. Should huge firms fail no matter the cost or should the government have authority to step in if it believes the economy is at risk?

Many financiers and policy makers regard OLA as an essential defense against economic catastrophe, a better option for taxpayers than the 2008 bailouts. Back then officials tried letting one firm, Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc ., go through bankruptcy court. When that sparked panic, they turned to another bad option: providing Wall Street firms billions of dollars in taxpayer money in exchange for shares.

Under Dodd-Frank, bankruptcy remains the first option. Huge banks now write detailed bankruptcy contingency plans. But if the collapse of a systemically important firm might create Lehman-like chaos, the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp . has the authority to take over the firm and wait for market calm before selling it off.

The FDIC may borrow money from the Treasury to keep the firms' lights on a backup plan to prevent panic that would ensue if a huge financial firm suddenly didn't open for business. These loans can be recouped by taxing the financial industry later.

This "orderly liquidation fund" has become the most controversial part of OLA. Supporters say the temporary loans aren't a bailout. The failing firm's shareholders would be wiped out, management would be fired, and lenders to the failing company would lose money, they say.

"It's the one tool to avoid a bailout," Jacob Lew, former Treasury secretary under President Barack Obama, said in a recent interview. "In a financial crisis, it would be extremely dangerous to be in a world where there was no mechanism that you had access to, to respond."

Conservatives say the temporary loans are a bailout. In their view, the taxpayer funds would go to some of the firm's creditors, creating the perception of a "safety net" that will encourage financiers to take excessive risks.

"It's just amazing eight years after the crash that taxpayers are still on the hook for bailing out big banks, but they are under Dodd-Frank," said House Financial Services Committee Chairman Jeb Hensarling (R., Tex.) in a recent radio interview.

Mr. Hensarling and others including Sen. Pat Toomey (R., Penn.) favor changes to the bankruptcy code that they say would avoid a Lehman-like scenario.

"I think we need to look at the bankruptcy code and what we can do as an alternative," Mr. Mnuchin said at the Jan. 19 hearing.

WSJ Pro First: Trump Gives Impetus to Revamp of Sarbanes-Oxley Auditor Rule

By Michael Rapoport

President Donald Trump wants to dismantle the Dodd-Frank regulatory overhaul put in place after the financial crisis. Many companies don't want him to stop there they are targeting a long-contested provision of the Sarbanes-Oxley Act, passed in the wake of the dot.com bust.

Business groups want to soften a Sarbanes-Oxley rule that requires companies to have auditors weigh in on their "internal controls" the policies and procedures intended to prevent errors or fraud on their financial statements.

Sarbanes-Oxley requires companies to evaluate whether these controls are effective and to have their auditor pass judgment on that assessment. Shareholder advocates say the rule, known as Section 404(b), helps ensure companies are giving accurate numbers to investors. Groups like the U.S. Chamber of Commerce say the rule is too costly and burdensome for small companies.

The fight over the rule has been simmering for years, but both sides say President Trump's antiregulatory push has given it renewed intensity. This "changed the mind-set," said John Berlau, a senior fellow at the Competitive Enterprise Institute, who is among those pushing to revamp the rule. "I think with the general sort of regulatory skepticism that goes along with the new administration, you're seeing more of a direct focus on 404."

The rule's supporters are girding to defend it. For companies, "it creates a greater sense of accountability that shouldn't go away," said Sandra Peters, head of financial reporting policy for the CFA Institute , which represents chartered financial analysts who work with individual investors.

And change may be coming fast: A coming version of the Financial Choice Act, Republicans' proposed replacement for Dodd-Frank, would apparently relax the Sarbanes-Oxley provision by exempting even more companies from it than had been planned under previous versions of this legislation.

The most recent version of the legislation called for raising the threshold under which companies are exempt from the rule to a market capitalization of $250 million. A coming version of the legislation will raise that to $500 million, according to a memo from House Financial Services Committee Chairman Jeb Hensarling (R., Texas) reviewed by The Wall Street Journal. As things now stand, the current threshold, imposed by Dodd-Frank, is a public float under $75 million.

"We feel that dialing back some of those provisions would help spur economic activity and create jobs," said Adam Ingles, head of regulatory risk solutions at MBAF, a Miami-based accounting and consulting firm.

Supporters of the rule say it is worth the trouble and should apply to companies of all sizes. A 2013 Government Accountability Office report found companies exempt from it restate their financial statements more often than those that must comply with it.

When larger companies began reviewing internal controls more than a decade ago, restatements initially soared, as companies found and fixed flaws in their controls, but subsequently, restatements declined. In 2015, 663 companies restated their financial statements, the fewest since 2002, according to consulting firm Audit Analytics.

"We don't need people investing in small companies that take off and then die," said Lynn Turner, a former Securities and Exchange Commission chief accountant.

But opponents of the internal-control rule argue that audits cost small companies money they just can't spare. They also maintain the compliance requirement deters companies from going public. "Our investors have told us time and again we would much rather you be spending money on research," said Charles Crain, director of tax and financial-services policy for the Biotechnology Innovation Organization, an industry group lobbying for changes in the rule.

Regulators and legislators have tried to address small companies' concerns in the past. Besides Dodd-Frank's exemption of the tiniest companies, regulators made their rules for conducting auditor reviews "scalable" for small companies in 2007. The 2012 JOBS Act further exempted new "emerging growth companies" from following the rule.

BIO supports an approach, also part of the proposed Choice Act, that would extend the exemption from auditor review for emerging-growth companies for an additional five years, to a total of 10 years, if they have annual revenue below $50 million. That would help small biotech companies with no revenue as they go through the long development period for new drugs, Mr. Crain said.

"Our companies need to go public, it is vital to their survival, and once they get there we want to make sure everything goes easily for them," he said.

 Rachel Witkowski contributed to this article.

Steven Mnuchin Confirmed as Treasury Secretary

By Nick Timiraos

WASHINGTON The Senate confirmed financier Steven Mnuchin as the next Treasury secretary in a 53-47 vote Monday evening, placing him in a leading position to advance President Donald Trump's plans to revamp financial regulation and the U.S. tax code.

Sen. Joe Manchin of West Virginia was the only Democrat to join with all of the Senate's Republicans to approve the nomination of Mr. Mnuchin, who was sworn in on Monday night.

Mr. Mnuchin, who served as Mr. Trump's campaign-finance chairman last year, spent 17 years at Goldman Sachs Group Inc . before leaving in 2002 to work in the hedge-fund industry. He co-founded Dune Capital Management LP and expanded into the entertainment business, financing Hollywood movies.

Mr. Mnuchin's role buying and rehabilitating the failed IndyMac Bank , later rebranded OneWest Bank , became a key focus of his confirmation battle. He and Republican backers said the financial-crisis era acquisition from the U.S. government demonstrated his business savvy.

Democrats opposed Mr. Mnuchin, 54 years old, citing the bank's involvement moving thousands of defaulted mortgages through foreclosure. They also questioned whether the bank had done enough new lending to under-served markets after the financial crisis.

"A president's cabinet provides insight into how they'll govern and what their priorities will be. The president has shown his hand by selecting the most anti-working-class cabinet that we have ever seen," said Senate Minority Leader Chuck Schumer (D., N.Y.) on the Senate floor Monday.

The delay between presidential administrations in filling the Treasury post was the longest in the nation's history, said Senate Finance Committee Chairman Orrin Hatch (R., Utah). "None of the allegations my colleagues have raised can stand even a modest amount of scrutiny," he said.

The lack of bipartisan support for a Treasury secretary is unusual. Previously, the closest vote for the job came in 2009 for President Barack Obama's first Treasury secretary, Timothy Geithner, who won confirmation on a 60-34 vote with 10 votes from Republicans. His nomination became controversial after disclosures that Mr. Geithner failed to pay some employment taxes in a timely manner.

One question now centers on how Mr. Mnuchin will be able to work with both parties to advance an overhaul of the tax code, an infrastructure-spending package and a rewrite of financial regulations passed by Congress in 2010.

Democrats have already pressed Mr. Mnuchin to explain how he will uphold a pledge delivered after his nomination was announced last fall to revamp the tax code in a way that provides "no absolute tax cut" for the upper class, a position that would appear very difficult to reconcile with the tax plan Mr. Trump unveiled last year.

Mr. Mnuchin faces several immediate tasks. The government's borrowing limit is suspended through March 15, after which it must be raised or suspended again. Independent analysts believe the department can use emergency measures for a few months to prevent the country from being unable to pay certain bills.

On the international front, the Treasury Department will be at the locus of decision-making on any changes to sanctions, including against North Korea following a ballistic-missile test last weekend. And officials will prepare this spring the semiannual report to Congress that would identify whether U.S. trading partners, such as China, are manipulating their currencies.

Mr. Mnuchin will also need to ramp up hiring at the agencies for dozens of appointed positions, many of which require Senate confirmation that could keep those slots vacant for weeks or months.

Mr. Mnuchin is expected to tap David Malpass, the former chief economist at Bear Stearns, for the Treasury's top international affairs post. Jim Donovan, a senior executive at Goldman Sachs, is being considered for a senior position overseeing domestic finance. Drew Maloney, a Washington lobbyist who is the top government affairs executive at energy supplier Hess Corp ., is being considered for the top legislative affairs post at the department, according to people familiar with the matter.

Mr. Mnuchin will bring on another campaign official, Eli Miller, as his chief of staff. He also has recruited several senior advisers, including former BlackRock executive Craig Phillips and Reed Rubinstein, a Washington lawyer who previously held a top post at the U.S. Chamber of Commerce . Shannon McGahn, a top aide to the Republican leadership of the House Financial Services Committee , will also join the department.

Yellen Testimony to Be Watched for Hints on Deregulation Concerns

By Harriet Torry

Federal Reserve Chairwoman Janet Yellen heads to Capitol Hill this week for two days of so-called Humphrey-Hawkins testimony, named for the 1978 law that required it. She will start at 10 a.m. EST Tuesday before the Senate Banking Committee, by delivering her monetary policy report and prepared remarks. She will present the same report and remarks to the House Financial Services Committee at 10 a.m. Wednesday. On both days, she will take lawmakers' questions, which are likely to focus on the economy, the path of interest rates, the labor market, financial regulation and the potential impact of the Trump administration's economic policies. Here is what to watch for.

A March Rate Increase?

All ears will be alert for signals about whether the Fed might raise its benchmark federal-funds rate at its next policy meeting March 14-15. Central bank officials lifted it in December to a range between 0.50% and 0.75%, and a March move isn't widely expected. Most economists surveyed recently by The Wall Street Journal expect the next rate increase in June. Investors on Monday saw a roughly 18% chance of a rate rise at the March gathering, according to CME Group . If Ms. Yellen wants to adjust expectations, her Capitol Hill appearances will be an opportunity to do so. Or she could wait until her next scheduled public speaking engagement on March 3.

Some senior Fed officials have raised the prospect of a move next month. Philadelphia Fed President Patrick Harker said last week that a March rate increase is "on the table," and San Francisco Fed President John Williams also said recently it might make sense for the central bank to raise interest rates as early as March.

Economic Uncertainty

Ms. Yellen likely will be asked to weigh in on the potential economic effects of President Donald Trump's fiscal plans, including talk of boosting infrastructure spending and slashing taxes. She was pressed on this during her congressional testimony in November, and said fiscal policies should be aimed at improving long-run economic growth and productivity. Other Fed officials have been dubious about the need for fiscal stimulus with the jobless rate so low, at 4.8% in January. A surge in government spending could push inflation too high and prompt Fed officials to raise interest rates faster than they currently expect. On monetary policy, Ms. Yellen likely will repeat the message of her remarks in Stanford, Calif., in late January: The Fed favors a gradual approach to raising short-term rates, and the fiscal outlook under Mr. Trump remains unclear.

Audit the Fed

The Fed faces political pressure from lawmakers mostly Republicans, but also some Democrats who hope a Trump White House will embrace their efforts to revamp the central bank. Legislative proposals include measures to subject the Fed to more congressional scrutiny, force it to adopt rules-based policy-making, and limit its discretionary powers. Expect Ms. Yellen to again make the case against these efforts, which she has said would erode the Fed's independence and subject it to political interference that could ultimately harm the economy.

Financial Regulation

Another area of political tension between the Fed and lawmakers is over financial regulation. Mr. Trump recently signed a memorandum ordering a review of the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial-overhaul law that has guided regulators such as the Fed. Lawmakers likely will question Ms. Yellen on her thoughts. In her November testimony, she warned against rolling back some measures, such as capital and liquidity rules, saying they have been critical in making the financial system safer.

Will She Stay or Will She Go?

Ms. Yellen hasn't said whether she would leave the Fed board of governors when her four-year term as chairwoman ends early next year, if Mr. Trump doesn't nominate her to a second term as Fed leader. He said during the campaign he probably wouldn't. But her 14-year term as a governor expires Jan. 31, 2024, which means she could stay on the board even if she isn't chairwoman. When asked in December whether she might stay, which would be an unusual but not unprecedented move, she said "that's a decision for another day."

WSJ Pro: GOP Lawmakers Urge Trump to Scrap More of Dodd-Frank

By Rachel Witkowski

WASHINGTON A group of Republicans on the House Financial Services Committee wrote President Donald Trump on Monday urging a further revamp of the financial regulatory laws enacted during the Obama administration.

The letter, signed by Rep. Dave Trott of Michigan and nine other members of the panel, thanked Mr. Trump for making a "critical first step" by issuing orders Feb. 3 that halted part of the 2010 Dodd-Frank Act and required the Treasury Department to review the law's impact. The Republican lawmakers asked for more of Dodd-Frank to be rolled back.

"We look forward to working with you to continue to shepherd through regulatory reform and promote policies that level the playing field for fair access to capital," said the letter, also signed by Rep. French Hill of Arkansas, reportedly a potential nominee to be deputy Treasury secretary; Andy Barr of Kentucky; Randy Hultgren of Illinois and Steve Stivers of Ohio.

Republicans on the Financial Services Committee have been gearing up to reintroduce a proposal from the panel's chairman, Rep. Jeb Hensarling (R., Texas), to revamp Dodd-Frank, the Financial Choice Act. The proposal, expected as soon as this week, is meant to relieve certain banks of heightened regulatory standards and take away most of the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau 's powers.

While Dodd-Frank's changes "were sold as making the American financial system safer, they instead transferred power from Main Street to Wall Street," the letter said.

Other Republicans who signed the letter were Warren Davidson of Ohio, Tom Emmer of Minnesota, Tom MacArthur of New Jersey, Roger Williams of Texas and Robert Pittenger of North Carolina.

WSJ Pro: Regional Bank CEOs Seek Review of Postcrisis Rules

By Donna Borak

WASHINGTON CEOs of the nation's top regional banks on Monday asked for a sweeping review of postcrisis regulations, saying they have limited financial firms' ability to lend to businesses and consumers.

In a letter to congressional leaders, the chiefs of 18 banks including Fifth Third Bancorp , U.S. Bank, Zions Bancorporation , BB&T Corp . and Huntington Bancshares Inc . said now was the "appropriate time" for the new Congress to conduct a thorough review of rules put into place by U.S. bank regulators such as the Federal Reserve and to identify regulations that have "unintended consequences or burdens."

The chief executive officers said the review should assess the effect regulations have had on the flow of credit.

"We support appropriately tailored regulations to ensure the safety and soundness of all banks, the stability of the financial system and strong consumer protections," they wrote in the letter.

President Donald Trump has vowed to eliminate regulations on U.S. businesses, particularly those that he says have suppressed lending. Earlier this month, Mr. Trump promised to undo a bevy of restrictions on financial firms put into place after the 2008 crisis.

"I have so many people, friends of mine, that had nice businesses," Mr. Trump said this month. "They can't borrow money. They just can't get any money because the banks just won't let them borrow it because of the rules and regulations in Dodd-Frank."

Bank lending has grown, even though some bankers believe they would be lending more without the new rules. Many data suggest it has been a lack of demand, not supply, that is tamping down borrowing by U.S. companies.

The CEOs also cited recent remarks by Treasury secretary nominee Steven Mnuchin, who endorsed the view of a regulatory framework based on "complexity and activity, not simply size."

Specifically, the CEOs asked Congress to undertake a review of existing regulations based on their risk profile and systemic footprint, eliminate the government's ability to set interchange fees on debit-card transactions, and require regulators to conduct an analysis of the costs of each new regulation.

The Fed has already taken steps to loosen the regulatory burden on midsize and regional banks. In January, it freed 20 U.S. banks with less than $250 billion in assets from the subjective portion of its annual stress tests to make the exams less onerous.

EU Online-Security Plan Is Criticized

By Todd Buell

FRANKFURT Business groups are slamming a European Union proposal that would require customers to enter extra security information for online purchases above  ¬10 ($10.64), saying the extra hassle could cut online sales by more than  ¬11 billion ($11.7 billion) a year.

The European Banking Authority , an EU rule-making body based in London, said it aims to combat fraud by requiring customers to enter extra information, such as a personal identification number, but "will seriously consider" making changes. It plans to announce revisions by the end of this month.

Credit-card companies and e-commerce associations worry that if online purchases become too cumbersome customers will abandon them.

Consumer advocates, on the other hand, say there is no trade-off between antifraud protections and promoting e-commerce.

"We disagree that stringent security measures will put people off," consumer-rights group BEUC said in a written statement. It said instances of online fraud in recent years warrant tougher rules.

"The situation needs to be improved quite quickly," said a spokesman for BEUC, Sebastien Pant.

Still, even BEUC said it doesn't "see the logic of the  ¬10 limit," arguing instead that when a buyer is known to a merchant the stricter standards aren't needed.

The conflict centers on technical standards to the Payment Services Directive, an EU law designed to ease cross-border payments, which will go into full effect next year. The EBA said it received a record number of responses to its draft standards after publication in August.

There was "extremely heated" debate at the EBA's public hearing in September, said Arun Srivastava, a partner at the law firm Baker McKenzie in London who attended the crowded meeting. "I was quite taken aback by the number of people," he said.

The retail sector objects to proposed exceptions from so-called Strong Customer Authentication, an industry standard designed to prevent online fraud. Under SCA a buyer would need to use a combination of two elements such as a PIN, authentication code or fingerprint. The EBA proposes exempting online purchases below  ¬10 from such rules.

Retailers and credit-card providers worry that if customers must take extra steps, then they will buy less. Purveyors prefer to set their own rules on how best to combat fraud.

Visa Europe , the European subsidiary of global payments-technology company Visa Inc ., estimates that the new EBA rules would put  ¬11.2 billion of online sales a year in Europe at risk. This equals about 2% of Europe's e-commerce market, which is expected to have exceeded  ¬510 billion last year. A survey of more than 5,000 adults across Europe, conducted on behalf of Visa, indicated that 61% of consumers would abandon purchases if more steps were added to the online payment process.

The industry would prefer to use a system known as Risk Based Authentication, which takes into consideration factors such as whether the retailer's website recognizes the buyer's internet address.

"There are existing methods of [customer] authentication, which are more efficient than this two-factor authentication," said David Stephenson, general secretary of the European Card Payment Association, a trade group.

The  ¬10 figure is "completely random and arbitrary," said David Birch, an industry consultant. If there is a black-and-white limit on when the stricter authentication is required, criminals also will know when it will be harder and easier to commit fraud, he said. The airline Deutsche Lufthansa AG and retailer Toys "R" Us Inc . could have different limits, for example, he said.

Lawmakers also question the  ¬10 limit. Markus Ferber, a German member of the European Parliament , said that if the EBA's proposals became law, "many fast payment possibilities, which are valued by customers, would no longer be possible."

He said customer-authentication policy should recognize the difference between a customer making a payment from a regular device or location versus the customer "suddenly making transactions from Nigeria."

Co-Op Bank Woes Mean Pain for Investors, But How Much?

By Margot Patrick

LONDON Britain's Co-operative Bank PLC could pose a fresh test to the regime for bank bailouts in Europe.

Co-op Bank on Monday put itself up for sale, saying it needs more capital and may buy back bonds at a discount. If a buyer doesn't emerge, the lender could be taken over by the Bank of England . Either way, investors in its shares and bonds face potential losses. The question is: how much?

Taxpayer bailouts in the financial crisis prompted European policy makers to come up with stricter rules to make shareholders and bondholders bear losses ahead of taxpayers. But the new regime hasn't led to standardized treatment of bondholders at banks nearing failure, and some big U.S. funds have endured losses from authorities' unexpected moves. Co-op Bank on Monday said it continues to meet minimum regulatory capital requirements. It didn't give a deadline for finding a buyer.

Under the current EU guidelines, bank shares and bonds must absorb losses a "bail in" before a government mounts a bailout. Yet authorities can still protect systemically important banks or investors, and some restructurings have taken longer than anticipated as initial rescue attempts proved insufficient.

A state-backed rescue at Italy's Banca Monte dei Paschi di Siena SpA is currently challenging the expectations of investors in troubled banks. There, retail investors may get a sweeter offer than international funds in the proposed government plan, which is awaiting European Commission approval. Or maybe not. Mom-and-pop bondholders were wiped out when a set of smaller Italian banks failed 14 months ago. The Italian government later said some of those investors can be compensated.

Before the financial crisis, government-backed bank bailouts had to comply with EU state aid rules, but bondholders were usually repaid as bank after bank neared collapse a situation that looked unfair to many taxpayers, who collectively had paid billions.

"The outcome in every case is very specific and the outcome for investors can vary dramatically, from 10 cents to par, depending on the political situation," said Philippe Bodereau, portfolio manager at Pacific Investment Management Co., or Pimco.

Last year, Pimco and other U.S. fund managers sued Portugal's central bank for removing EUR2 billion ($2.13 billion) in bonds for repayment at Novo Banco SA , a still-troubled lender carved out of the former Banco Esprito Santo . The central bank left retail bonds intact. It said the decision protected Novo Banco's depositors and was in the public interest.

In December, as a private recapitalization at Italy's Banca Monte dei Paschi di Siena SpA was failing, teams of lawyers and regulatory experts mapped out scenarios for U.S.-based hedge funds and other clients sensing an opportunity to buy the bank's junior bonds at low prices and make a profit if the government engineered a favorable deal. They parsed statements from Italian officials, studied Germany's body language over the brewing problem and brushed up on Italian law, according to people doing the work.

"There's a type of investor that is intrigued to find a smart way around these situations and make money from them, and others that don't have the appetite for the detail," said Barney Reynolds, a partner at Shearman & Sterling .

Italy's solution a "precautionary recapitalization" allowed under the EU Bank Recovery and Resolution Directive was seen as an acceptable result to many institutional investors despite the preferential treatment of retail bondholders. The proposal needs state-aid clearance from the EU executive arm but is expected to be approved.

In Britain, Co-op Bank narrowly avoided a taxpayer bailout in 2013 when hedge funds including Silver Point Capital and Perry Capital converted some bonds to equity and injected new capital. But plans to turn the bank around were stymied by low interest rates and rising capital charges. Now its investors must calculate how they might fare in a private sale or recapitalization, or in a seizure of the lender by the central bank.

Some of the mystery around bail-ins may lift once European banks have new bonds in place. Part of the new rules mandate that banks have a certain percentage of loss-absorbing bonds in their funding, and that their rank is clear in relation to other debt and deposits.

Despite the precarious state of some European economies, few banks have actually failed or come close in the last few years. A new EU body to handle failing banks, the Single Resolution Board , is untested.

The new bonds present their own issues. There are three ways for banks to give the bonds lower priority in a bailout to other debt and deposits through structure, contract or national law. The scope of future bail-ins could hinge on the nuances.

"There are a lot more factors involved then there were a few years ago. It makes the job of the investor to understand the bond they are buying and how it will operate more challenging." said Tom Grant, a partner at Allen & Overy .

To Show Credit Decline Since Crisis, Measure Differently

The traditional way of measuring mortgage denials suggests that it's easier to get a mortgage now than it was before the housing crisis, despite ample data to the contrary, "http://www.urban.org/urban-wire/borrowers-less-perfect-credit-are-giving-trying-get-mortgage". Their method of measuring the mortgage denial rate "offers a more accurate barometer of credit accessibility by eliminating borrowers with perfect credit from the denial calculation, because these borrowers will never be denied credit," they write. By using this method, they are able to clearly show that mortgage credit availability has declined since the crisis.

The CFPB Is a Target Because It Is Too Effective

Republicans are targeting the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau because it has been too effective and aggressive dealing with wrongdoing by financial institutions, "https://www.nytimes.com/2017/02/10/business/consumer-financial-protection-bureau-gretchen-morgenson.html?ref=dealbook&_r=0". Before the financial crisis, there was no federal regulator dedicated to dealing with consumer complaints, she writes. Since its establishment, the CFPB has made enemies of banks, consumer credit reporting companies, credit card issuers and student loan collectors, as well as their Republican allies in Congress, Ms. Morgenson says.

"http://blogs.wsj.com/moneybeat/2017/02/13/hain-celestial-spoils-investor-appetite-again/"announced Friday afternoon that it will be unable to file a required report on time with the Securities and Exchange Commission . This is the third such disclosure from the embattled natural foods company since it discovered revenue irregularities last summer.

"http://blogs.wsj.com/moneybeat/2017/02/13/are-you-fed-up-with-the-fed-so-is-this-veteran-of-the-bank/" could use a few more goat farmers and few less theoretical models. That is the gist of Fed veteran Danielle DiMartino Booth's new book, "Fed Up."

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/time-to-log-out-of-bank-of-the-internet-1487066580", a rapidly growing bank with nontraditional borrowers, appears to be winning its battle with whistleblowers and short sellers. The question for investors is: Can the bank stay ahead?

Pro Private Markets
Thoma Bravo-Backed SailPoint Lays the Groundwork for Future IPO; Cybersecurity company provides software products for identity management

By Laura Cooper 
577 words
14 February 2017
12:45
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Although 2017 has started off slowly for private companies seeking initial public offerings, SailPoint Technologies Inc. is betting its continuing revenue growth and a growing need for cybersecurity products will pave the way for a successful public market debut.

SailPoint, which is backed by private-equity firm Thoma Bravo, is laying the groundwork for a possible IPO filing later this year, according to people familiar with the situation.

The Austin, Texas-based cybersecurity company offers identity management software that helps companies monitor security breaches, including those from inside companies themselves, along with other security-related tasks such as password management, compliance control and data access, according to its website. Its customers include Weight Watchers International Inc., Molina Healthcare Inc. and Sallie Mae.

SailPoint has made two new hires to help groom it for an IPO filing later this year, if markets are favorable, these people said. In November, the company announced it had appointed Kevin Hansel as chief information officer to support its continued business expansion. More recently, it hired a general counsel, who will join SailPoint in about two weeks, one of the people said, although this person declined to name the general counsel.

"There have been a lot of opportunities that were snatched out of the hands of the public market investors," this person said, noting Cisco Systems Inc.'s $3.7 billion recent announced acquisition of AppDynamics Inc. in January, the week of the startup's slated IPO. "[SailPoint] is profitable and has the flexibility [to wait] until the market is attractive."

If SailPoint moves forward with its IPO ambitions, it would be one of only a few technology companies braving the public markets so far this year. The highly anticipated IPO of disappearing-message app Snapchat's parent company, Snap Inc., has attracted attention from investors because of its high private-market valuation and visibility in the social media market.

Other companies, such as big data analytics company Zeta Global Inc. and enterprise communication software provider Asana Inc., are considering possible public offerings in 2017, or have made strategic hires that may indicate a future public listing, according to previous reports in The Wall Street Journal.

Founded in 2005, SailPoint received an investment from Thoma Bravo "https://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFLBO0120140813ea8dqxnz3". Since then, the company's revenue has grown steadily. According to a February 2016 news release, the company's 2015 revenue surpassed $100 million. The firm announced this month that 2016 revenue had grown to more than $130 million, according to a release recapping last year's financial results.

In 2016, the company added more than 220 customers, bringing its client base to more than 750, according to the release. Most recently, SailPoint announced last week it would collaborate with Microsoft Corp. to add identity governance capabilities to Microsoft's Azure Active Directory, an identity and access management product.

It's possible that despite its intent, SailPoint could get snapped up by a a corporate buyer or a private-equity firm before it enters the public market, given the large amount of capital such firms have available to invest. In December, cybersecurity company Optiv Security Inc. was purchased by KKR & Co. a little less than month after the company, which had been backed by Blackstone Group LP, disclosed plans for an IPO.

Active fund managers fight back against  Darwinian cull 

Chris Flood 
1,193 words
13 February 2017
05:07
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

A life or death fight confronts many traditional active asset managers. They face intense pressure to find more effective ways to combat the inexorable rise of low-cost index-tracking funds.

This challenge is most acute in the US, where the share of institutional assets allocated to active investment strategies fell from 79 per cent in 2012 to 67 per cent by 2015, according to Greenwich Associates, the consultancy.

But large active fund houses protest these challenges are not insurmountable. Many are preparing a range of tactics to fight back against the rise of "https://www.ft.com/topics/themes/Passive_Investing", which looks set to continue to take market share in the coming years.

Greenwich says around one in five large US investors plan to reduce their allocations to active US equity strategies significantly over the next three years, a trend that is being repeated in other regions where index-tracking funds are proving popular.

Recently Moody s, the credit rating agency, predicted that the rapidly expanding passive investment market will become larger than the active fund industry in the US by 2024 at the latest, intensifying competitive pressures for stockpicking managers.

Andrew McCollum, managing director at Greenwich, says success will be  difficult if not impossible  for active managers that stick to a traditional stockpicking approach, even if they enjoy temporary periods of prosperity with fashionable products or when markets rise.

A far bleaker assessment comes from Neil Constable, head of the global equity team at GMO, the $88bn Boston-based investment company that focuses purely on active management. He predicts a  Darwinian cull  as  there are too many mediocre active managers out there and they charge a lot .

 If an active manager is not generating returns that are reliably better than the market, then they have no business charging a fee for their services,  he says.

GMO, Franklin Templeton, Wells Fargo Asset Management and Legg Mason were among the large US managers that have been hit hardest by investor withdrawals in 2016.

But Joe Sullivan, chief executive of Legg Mason, the $710bn New York-listed investment manager, insists active managers can overcome the threat from passive without having to go down the route of launching their own index-tracking businesses. [It is an] easy but shallow argument to say that active is dead,  he says.

Mr Sullivan believes that future returns from equities and bonds are likely to remain muted. This means investors will require access to less-liquid asset classes such as "https://www.ft.com/stream/topicsId/MTY1MmRjNGYtZWI3Ny00NTE1LTk4MTUtNjk2MTM4Mzg3Y2Nj-VG9waWNz", property, "https://www.ft.com/topics/themes/Private_equity" and direct lending. These areas provide more opportunities for active managers, according to the chief executive.

 These asset classes have the ability to deliver higher returns than a balanced 60/40 equity-bond portfolio,  he says, adding that it would be  foolish for anyone to suggest that an investor has all of their money in passive strategies .

Allianz Global Investors, BlueBay, M&G and Hermes are among the large fund houses to have increased their focus on niche asset classes such as direct lending and private debt over the past two years. These investment strategies are widely considered difficult to replicate in a passive format.

Schroders, the UK s largest listed asset manager, similarly acquired an investment team focused on asset-backed securities, a niche area of the fixed income market, from Brookfield, the US asset manager, last May. It also entered into a strategic partnership with Neos, the Dutch direct lending firm, in April.

Peter Harrison, chief executive of the FTSE 100 fund house, told FTfm last year the logic behind the Brookfield deal was that the investment expertise Schroders was acquiring was  very hard to disrupt through passive .

Euan Munro, chief executive of Aviva Investors, the 319bn asset management arm of Aviva, the UK insurer, agrees active managers can fight back by providing sophisticated investment strategies, such as multi-asset funds, that are not easily replicated by passive indices.

He adds that fund managers are turning away from measuring their performance relative to traditional equity benchmarks to focus on providing absolute returns for investors in any market condition. This could help active managers retain business, he believes.

 Beating the FTSE 100 or S&P 500 might be evidence of stockpicking skill, but that doesn t solve a client s fundamental problems, whether that is saving for a pension or drawing an income for retirement,  he says.

Other stockpickers have turned to a second defence strategy: taking more aggressive bets by increasing the active share of their portfolios, which means their funds are less likely to reflect the index they are benchmarked against.  Many of our managers are running high active-share strategies to deliver alpha,  says Mr Sullivan.

Several large active fund companies, including Neptune, Columbia Threadneedle and Baillie Gifford, started to publish measures of the active share of their funds in 2015 to distinguish their strategies from those of their competitors.

A third defence tactic that is being adopted widely is to invest in quantitative strategies, whereby active managers run highly diversified, computer-driven portfolios that invest across a large number of stocks.

Quantitative funds fell out of favour after performing badly as a group in the 2007/08 financial crisis. But Inigo Fraser-Jenkins, a strategist at Bernstein Research, the brokerage, says these strategies are no longer stigmatised due to investors  growing appreciation of the possibilities of "https://www.ft.com/topics/themes/Big_data" and the wealth of information on the internet.  Active quantitative managers have an advantage in the use of new data sets that do not use standard accounting data but which require significant computational ability,  he says.

T Rowe Price, the $811bn Baltimore-based manager, launched three quantitatively driven US funds last April, while GAM, the Swiss-listed asset manager, paid $217m to buy Cantab Capital, a quantitative UK hedge fund company, to diversify its active management capabilities in June.

The final prong of the defence strategy employed by many large fund houses has been to hone their expertise and bring in specialists such as actuaries, political strategists and economists to advise on the challenges facing investors in a low-return environment.

This has led to an uptick in demand for high-profile political and economic experts at fund houses with large active investment businesses.

Last month BlackRock, the world s largest fund house, announced it had appointed George Osborne, the former UK chancellor of the exchequer, as a senior adviser. And in October, RWC, the purely active investment house, announced it had entered a strategic partnership with the consultancy launched by Condoleezza Rice, the former US secretary of state.

Pimco, the world s largest bond fund house, has similarly enlisted the help of Jean-Claude Trichet and Ben Bernanke, the former European and US central bank chiefs respectively, and Gordon Brown, the former British prime minister, to advise on economic and political events.

Mr Fraser-Jenkins says rising investor demand for passive strategies has  raised the bar  for success for active managers, who must demonstrate they possess stockpicking skills that can translate into market-beating returns.

 Organisations that fail to embrace these changes risk going the way of the video-rental store,  says Greenwich s Mr McCollum.
