Tracy's Take: Tarullo Leaves the Fed. How Durable are His Policies?

Mnuchin to Secure Confirmation as Soon as Monday

WSJ Pro Interactive: Looking Toward Choice 2.0

Cyberattacks on International Banks Show Links to Hackers Who Hit Sony

SEC Letter to Allergan Hints at Additional Non-GAAP Guidance

Tarullo Leaves the Fed. How Durable are His Policies?

Federal Reserve governor Daniel Tarullo finally ended speculation about his future Friday with a "https://www.wsj.com/articles/daniel-tarullo-federal-reserve-regulatory-point-man-resigning-1486751401", effective early April. Now comes the final test of his policies: How long will they endure?

Mr. Tarullo has become a force of nature on financial regulatory policy in large part through consensus building, and the durability of his legacy will depend on whether that consensus holds.

Given the opaqueness of decision-making by the Fed's top brass, it's always been hard to discern the precise extent to which Mr. Tarullo influenced outcomes. When the Fed failed Citigroup Inc . in its 2014 stress tests, apparently "https://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10001424052702303887804579504230938475574" of Citi's New York supervisors, was that Mr. Tarullo's doing? Would the Fed's capital rules or annual stress tests have been as tough without Mr. Tarullo in the room? On these choices and many others, the Fed doesn't engage in much public debate. But Mr. Tarullo wasn't the only voice, and the final decisions were almost always unanimous.

Within the Fed, Chairwoman Janet Yellen and other Fed board members had influence. Their terms will extend beyond April; Ms. Yellen's into early 2018.

Fed senior staff also mattered, and many of them will remain on the job long after Mr. Tarullo and Ms. Yellen are gone. (One notable exception is Fed General Counsel Scott Alvarez, who said last week he is "https://www.wsj.com/articles/top-fed-lawyer-scott-alvarez-to-retire-this-year-fed-says-1486571410".)

Under Mr. Tarullo, the Fed didn't just rewrite the rulebook, it also restructured to empower staff in the Division of Banking Supervision and Regulation. These public servants ultimately answer to political appointees, but their opinions and recommendations count because they, not Fed governors, see what is actually going on inside firms the Fed regulates. Future Fed decisions will be influenced at least in part by these staffers.

Outside the Fed, the question is whether Mr. Tarullo has succeeded in convincing other policy makers. He thinks so, telling The Wall Street Journal on Friday that for what he called "core" overhauls, "there will be durability." Those include capital rules, liquidity rules, better risk management, and viable resolution strategies.

In one early indicator that support for such policies may outlive Mr. Tarullo, Fed Vice Chairman Stanley Fischer "https://www.wsj.com/articles/feds-fischer-issues-warning-on-bank-capital-requirements-1486818344" that "significantly reducing capital requirements would reduce the safety of the system and we certainly hope it's not going to happen."

If Mr. Tarullo's successor isn't convinced and wants to change these rules, he or she will have to bring along other regulators. That includes Mr. Trump's choice for comptroller of the currency and chair of the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp., along with other members of the Fed's governing board and, perhaps, regulators in other countries (if internationally agreed regulatory accords are the target).

"There is way more of a grounding for this than me," Mr. Tarullo said in the interview, referring to those "core" overhauls. The coming months will bear out whether that is correct, starting with the people Mr. Trump puts in charge.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

Mnuchin to Secure Confirmation as Soon as Monday

By Nick Timiraos

WASHINGTON The Senate is expected to confirm Steven Mnuchin as the 77th Treasury secretary as soon as Monday night, which would end one of the most protracted confirmation battles for the position and place the 54-year-old financier in a leading position to advance President Donald Trump's promises to refashion the U.S. tax code and financial regulation.

Mr. Mnuchin has faced opposition from Democrats for several reasons, including his role rehabilitating the failed IndyMac Bank , later rebranded OneWest Bank , by moving thousands of defaulted mortgages through foreclosure.

Senators also have raised concerns about answers he provided the committee during the confirmation process pertaining to his complex financial disclosures and his record at OneWest.

Already, his confirmation has faced the longest delay of any recent Treasury secretary, and he appears likely to win confirmation for the position with a historically weak margin of support, a pattern reflected with other high-profile Trump nominees.

Mr. Trump was set to meet with Mr. Mnuchin on Sunday afternoon at his private club in Palm Beach, Fla. With Mr. Mnuchin not in office during the first three weeks of the administration, Gary Cohn, the former Goldman Sachs banker named as the director of the White House National Economic Council, has played an increasingly influential role shaping policy on tax, regulation and infrastructure. Mr. Mnuchin spent 17 years at the New York investment bank, leaving in 2002.

The vote will take place as senators work to approve remaining Trump administration cabinet appointees. The Senate on Monday also is expected to confirm Dr. David Shulkin as Mr. Trump's secretary of Veterans Affairs. Dr. Shulkin currently serves as the head of the VA's health-care system.

That would leave six more Trump cabinet picks whose confirmations are pending, including Commerce Secretary-designate Wilbur Ross, Energy Secretary-designate Rick Perry and Interior Secretary-designate Ryan Zinke. One of the pending nominees, Labor Secretary-designate Andy Puzder, is set to have a confirmation hearing on Thursday.

In the Republican-controlled Congress, lawmakers also plan to work this week to undo as many last-minute Obama administration regulations as possible on a simple-majority vote. Among expected targets in the House is a rule that blocks states from denying organizations that provide abortion services access to federal funds available for pregnancy testing and other family-planning services. Federal law prohibits the use of federal funds for abortions except in the cast of rape, incest, or the endangerment of the mother's life.

Mr. Mnuchin cleared the Senate Finance Committee earlier this month after Democrats boycotted the vote.

Democrats said they wanted more information from the nominee, though some had already announced their opposition, and Republicans subsequently suspended the rules to advance his nomination without any Democrats in attendance.

Mr. Mnuchin has rejected Democrats' critiques of his record, and a spokesman has said he has provided truthful answers to their questions. "Since I was first nominated to serve as Treasury secretary, I have been maligned as taking advantage of others' hardships in order to earn a buck. Nothing could be further from the truth," Mr. Mnuchin said at his Jan. 19 confirmation hearing.

The partisan row illustrates the challenge facing Mr. Mnuchin in fashioning a tax-code overhaul, infrastructure package, financial-regulation revamp and housing-finance legislation that can attract bipartisan support.

For example, Democrats have pressed Mr. Mnuchin to explain how he will uphold a pledge delivered hours after Mr. Trump announced his nomination to revamp the tax code in a way that provides "no absolute tax cut" for the upper class.

"He has failed to commit to following his own rule or to provide any specific answers on how we would reduce the tax burden on middle-class and working families," said Sen. Mark Warner (D., Va.) on the Senate floor last Friday.

The responsibilities of the Treasury secretary, who is fifth in the presidential line of succession, include everything from tax collection and fiscal policy to public-debt management, bank regulation and the implementation of international sanctions.

One pressing need for the new Treasury secretary will be to raise the government's borrowing limit, currently suspended through March 15, after which the department must use emergency measures to prevent the country from being unable to pay certain bills. Independent analysts believe those measures can last through the summer.

At his confirmation hearing, Mr. Mnuchin said he hoped lawmakers would move soon to increase the borrowing limit.

He said he believed existing sanctions on Russia should be maintained and he called for a boost in staffing at the Internal Revenue Service , where ranks have thinned partly due to spending cuts driven by congressional Republicans.

Delays in Mr. Mnuchin's confirmation also have pushed back the process of approvals for top Treasury deputies, meaning it could be weeks or months before those positions are filled. Mr. Mnuchin, who served as the Trump campaign's finance chairman, is expected to bring on another campaign official, Eli Miller, as his chief of staff.

He also has recruited two senior advisers, former BlackRock executive Craig Phillips and Reed Rubinstein, a Washington lawyer who previously held a top post at the U.S. Chamber of Commerce .

 Siobhan Hughes contributed to this article.

Daniel Tarullo, Federal Reserve Regulatory Point Man, to Resign

By Ryan Tracy

WASHINGTON The Federal Reserve's lead architect of postcrisis financial regulations plans to resign this spring, giving President Donald Trump more freedom to remake the central bank and to accelerate a deregulatory agenda by putting his own appointees in charge of overseeing Wall Street.

Daniel Tarullo, a 64-year-old Fed governor and the government's most influential overseer of the American banking system, wrote to Mr. Trump on Friday saying he would resign "on or about" April 5. The move had been expected, and will remove from the policy-making debate one of the strongest voices for imposing safeguards on big banks and nonbanks to protect against another meltdown. Mr. Trump and many of his advisers have criticized those rules as hampering economic growth, and have suggested they will fill vacancies with officials who will handle banking policy with a lighter touch.

Stock prices for megabanks jumped on the news of Mr. Tarullo's imminent departure, with shares in Bank of America Corp . and Citigroup Inc . rising almost 1% in the half-hour following the announcement.

Mr. Tarullo's resignation will also give the Trump administration broad discretion to put its own stamp on the central bank at a time when critics including top Republicans in Congress have accused the institution of lacking transparency and accountability. The departure could leave vacant three of the seven slots on the Fed's board of governors. In addition, Janet Yellen's term as chairwoman expires early next year.

Filling those vacancies would also give the new president the chance to redirect the course of monetary policy, though it is unclear whether he would seek officials who would alter Ms. Yellen's current course of cautious rate increases.

Mr. Tarullo's announcement came exactly a week after Mr. Trump signed an executive order instructing regulators to review the rules implemented since the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial overhaul, and as Republican lawmakers intensify their plans to rewrite that landmark law. But partisan gridlock on Capitol Hill makes it unlikely Congress can make big changes, leaving it to the regulators Mr. Trump nominates to change the way rules are written, implemented and enforced.

Mr. Tarullo's term doesn't expire until 2022, but many expected him to step down sooner because Mr. Trump's team wants to appoint someone else to the currently vacant post of Federal Reserve vice chairman in charge of bank oversight. President Barack Obama never filled the job that was created by Dodd-Frank, and let Mr. Tarullo effectively fill it on an informal basis. Mr. Trump's appointment would have diminished Mr. Tarullo's influence.

Mr. Tarullo said in an interview with The Wall Street Journal Friday he had a "fairly strong presumption" even before the November election that he would resign early this year after eight intense years on the job, and the Trump administration didn't ask him to leave.

He said it isn't clear yet whether his successor will want to undo financial regulations, and it is "hard to argue with" the regulatory principles Mr. Trump laid out in the executive order. Those principles included analyzing the costs of regulations.

"I am hopeful that the core things [the Fed has accomplished] capital, liquidity, better risk management and viable resolution strategies are things that will end up and do command a wide enough consensus, particularly for the largest institutions," he said in the interview. "There may be some other changes that I might not fully subscribe to, but that wouldn't cut to the heart of what I think is important for the stability of the American financial system."

Mr. Trump hasn't said whom he will appoint to the Fed board. David Nason, an executive at General Electric Co .'s financing arm who was an architect of the 2008 bank bailouts during a stint at the Treasury Department, has emerged as a leading candidate for the vice chairman job in recent weeks, according to people familiar with the matter.

But Mr. Trump's team has interviewed other candidates, these people said. For instance, Mr. Trump has met with former BB&T Corp. Chief Executive John Allison, who has been critical of what he views as undercapitalized big banks.

Gary Cohn, director of the White House National Economic Council, has said filling the Fed's banking post is a priority for the administration. Mr. Cohn weeks ago left his job as a top executive at Goldman Sachs Group Inc . and said in a Journal interview last week the Fed has clamped down too heavily on banks under Mr. Tarullo's watch indicating he would favor replacing Mr. Tarullo with somebody who would implement different rules.

Mr. Tarullo has presided over a significant expansion of the Fed's influence over Wall Street. Some financiers called him the "Wizard of Oz" for his mysterious yet powerful role enforcing bank rules. He came to the Fed as Mr. Obama took office with a mission to overhaul a regulatory system that had just failed to prevent a massive financial crisis.

Under Mr. Tarullo's watch, the Fed centralized regulatory power in Washington and charted an aggressive course, adopting rules that effectively taxed banks' size but avoiding directly forcing them to break up, as some industry critics had demanded.

He successfully pushed for tighter capital and liquidity requirements on big banks, was a force behind new rules for foreign-owned banks operating on U.S. soil, and helped design and implement new annual "stress tests" that big U.S. banks must pass or face restrictions on their ability to pay dividends.

Some of those changes will be hard to undo. There is broad support for tough bank capital rules, and rewriting those would likely require years of work and consensus among several U.S. regulatory agencies.

Other changes could happen more quickly. For instance, the Fed's leadership has significant discretion in deciding the toughness of stress tests, and whether banks pass or fail.

Supporters say Mr. Tarullo has made banks more resilient, and helped improve their risk management. Fed Up, a liberal activist group that regularly criticizes the central bank, praised Mr. Tarullo in a statement saying it was sorry to see him go. "Tarullo's departure makes all of us more vulnerable to unemployment, loss of wealth, and insecurity," the group said.

Critics say his policies have gone overboard, and held the economy back.

Mr. Tarullo's departure "is good news for our lending markets and good news for consumers of capital," said Richard Farley, a banking lawyer at Kramer Levin Naftalis & Frankel LLP . He pointed to the Fed's crackdown during Mr. Tarullo's tenure on banks' lending to highly leveraged companies a policy that isn't enshrined in federal regulation and could be walked back under new leadership.

Mr. Trump also could install Fed governors more welcoming of GOP lawmakers' efforts to revamp the central bank, which they say would make it more transparent and accountable to the public.

Legislative proposals under consideration include measures to subject the Fed to more congressional scrutiny, force it to adopt rules-based monetary policy-making, and limit its discretionary powers.

Ms. Yellen has argued against these efforts, saying they would erode the Fed's independence and subject it to political interference that could ultimately harm the economy.

Mr. Trump, however, could nominate a chair who would steer the Fed to voluntarily supply more of the information sought by Congress, such as more detail about its internal deliberations over interest rate policies.

The president could also reshape the Fed's monetary policy tilt with his appointments, but it is unclear whether he would prefer officials more inclined to raise interest rates aggressively "hawks" in Fed jargon. During the campaign he accused Ms. Yellen of holding rates low to help Mr. Obama, but also said in an interview, "I am a low interest-rate person."

 David Harrison contributed to this article.

Corrections & Amplifications

Daniel Tarullo's resignation letter to President Donald Trump didn't give a reason for his departure. An earlier version of this article incorrectly stated the letter was written to Fed Chairwoman Janet Yellen. (Feb. 10, 2017)

Cyberattacks on International Banks Show Links to Hackers Who Hit Sony

By Robert McMillan

Cybersecurity specialists have found evidence suggesting that recent attacks on institutions in Poland are part of an international hacking effort targeting financial institutions in the U.S., Mexico and the United Kingdom an attack that shares traits with the 2014 attack on Sony Corp .

The hacks began late last year, installing unauthorized code on websites belonging to financial regulators, then using those to attack computers belonging to a select list of global financial institutions, according to researchers who have examined the attacks at security vendors Symantec Corp . and BAE Systems PLC .

It is unclear to the researchers exactly how many banks were compromised or whether any suffered financial losses. But the researchers say it appears to be part of a well-organized and broad hacking effort that shares links to other attacks including the devastating 2014 hack that destroyed systems and exposed email messages at Sony Pictures Entertainment . U.S. officials have said North Korea was responsible for that attack. North Korea has denied that, though said its supporters might have done it.

Researchers at BAE Systems and Symantec say that some of the software and internet infrastructure in the global effort was also used in the Sony attack and more recently other attacks on banks in Asia. Security researchers call the North Korea-linked group they believe is behind these attacks "Lazarus." It has been active since 2009, according to Kaspersky Lab ZAO, a Russian cybersecurity company.

If the recent attacks are indeed by Lazarus, it suggests the group is broadening its banking attacks. The group's bank hacking previously had focused on Asia, said Eric Chien, technical director of Symantec's Security Technology and Response division. "We never saw them do anything, for example, to the U.S., let alone Europe," he said. "Now we see them targeting the U.S. and Europe."

In November the Federal Bureau of Investigation warned U.S. financial institutions that it was "monitoring emerging reports indicating that well-resourced and organized malicious cyber actors have intentions to target the U.S. financial sector."

The FBI didn't respond to requests for comment about the latest attacks.

The attacks started in October by compromising the website of the Polish Financial Supervision Authority , an incident that was reported last week by the Badcyber.com blog. The hackers programmed that website to attack banking computers that visited the site, the researchers say.

Security investigators call this technique a "watering hole." It lets criminals use one common access point to break into a range of other organizations. In this case, by infecting a website commonly visited by banking employees, the hackers could hope to spread malicious software onto computers within the financial institutions on their list, said Adrian Nish, head of BAE Systems ' Threat Intelligence team.

A Polish Financial Supervision Authority spokesman confirmed that the regulator had "identified an external attempt to interfere in the operating IT system," and had turned over evidence of the incident to law enforcement after restoring the website. The Polish National Police Agency didn't immediately respond to a request for comment Friday.

The hackers programmed the hacked web servers to attack computers only if they originated from a short-list of approximately 75 institutions an apparent effort to keep a lower profile and help evade detection, the researchers say.

This list includes 19 financial institutions in Poland, 15 in the U.S., nine in Mexico, and seven in the U.K., said BAE Systems , which declined to name the institutions.

The attacks also compromised a website belonging to Mexico's financial regulator, the National Banking and Securities Commission, and a state-run bank in Uruguay, Dr. Nish said. A spokeswoman for the National Banking and Securities Commission said that it has seen no evidence that its computers were compromised. "During the past weekend, we received notice of a coordinated attack addressed to banking institutions world-wide," she said. "Our Security Operations Center performed a thorough inspection, from which no abnormal behavior was detected." The Commission's investigation is continuing she said.

The attacks, with their use of the "water hole" technique, appear to be more sophisticated than previous Lazarus attacks, Dr. Nish and Mr. Chien said. In the shadowy world of cybersecurity, code can be stolen and reused, which makes the business of linking attacks to specific actors time consuming and often inexact. Dr. Nish, at BAE, said he has a "high confidence" that the group involved is Lazarus. "We know the tools that they're using very well and we know the infrastructure they're using and their tactics," Dr. Nish said. "And we can strongly confirm that the tools that have been found on the bank networks and in these [website] attacks are part of the group's tool kit."

Mr. Chien said that Symantec hadn't yet done analysis required to definitively make the connection, but that the tools used in these latest attacks are linked to Lazarus tools used in the past.

SEC Letter to Allergan Hints at Additional Non-GAAP Guidance

By Tatyana Shumsky

Details in a comment letter from the Securities and Exchange Commission to Allergan PLC may offer insight to the future of the regulator's crackdown on company use of non-GAAP figures.

The agency chided the maker of Botox over the way it calculated "adjusted earnings per share," and took an unusual step saying it would review financial reporting practices in the pharmaceutical industry. The letter, dated Jan. 11, but made public last week, said the SEC would consider issuing additional guidance on tailored financial measures.

The calculation of adjusted earnings per share is a metric that doesn't conform to U.S. generally accepted accounting principles; such figures are known as non-GAAP.

"We will evaluate the industry practices you described to us and consider whether additional comprehensive non-GAAP staff guidance is appropriate," the SEC said in the letter.

The comment is atypical in that it signaled the prospect of additional guidelines in a letter to a company, rather than a separate SEC statement, said Olga Usvyatsky, vice president of research at Audit Analytics. Comment letters are part of the SEC's nonauthoritative guidance, and not typically where the regulator announces new policy initiatives, she explained.

"The language itself suggests that they may consider additional guidance regardless of [the] administration," Ms. Usvyatsky said.

The SEC last year stepped up efforts to rein in the use of tailored accounting. The push, which included new guidelines on non-GAAP metrics and a wave of comment letters criticizing such practices, prompted many companies to change their presentation of results and cut back the use of adjusted figures.

The future of the SEC's clampdown is unclear amid the wider deregulatory efforts of President Donald Trump's administration. The correspondence with Allergan concluded nine days before Mr. Trump took office, and in the final days of former SEC Chairman Mary Jo White's tenure.

Ms. White stepped down on Jan. 20, handing the reins to acting SEC Chairman Michael Piwowar. Mr. Piwowar, a Republican, is already making swift changes at the agency, even before the president's nominee for SEC Chairman, Jay Clayton, is confirmed.

The SEC declined to comment.

As for Allergan, the SEC concluded the company should consider dropping the metric from its reports or renaming it. Allergan excluded "more than half of all operating expenses" when calculating adjusted earnings per share, the SEC said. With so much excluded, the figure deviated too far from standard earnings per share and needed to be retitled.

Allergan renamed the metric to performance net income per share when it reported fourth-quarter and full year 2016 results on Feb. 8 "to better reflect the fact that it is simply a measure that management uses to evaluate its performance," said Mark Marmur, Allergan's director of corporate affairs, in an email.

Co-operative Bank Puts Itself Up For Sale

By Margot Patrick

LONDON Co-operative Bank PLC put itself up for sale Monday, a surprise move by the troubled bank co-owned by U.S. hedge funds and a British supermarket chain.

Chairman Dennis Holt said the move is aimed at putting the bank in a stronger capital position, after needing a waiver from the Bank of England around its capital targets over the next few years. He blamed low interest rates and unforeseen expenses for the delay in the bank's return to health after a 2013 bailout by some of its bondholders.

Co-op Bank was saved by funds including Silver Point Capital and Perry Capital after bad loans, a costly technology upgrade and a failed acquisition wiped out a chunk of its capital that year.

Since then, it has cut costs by 20% and improved its systems, but struggled to rebuild capital. Co-op Bank continues to meet a baseline capital requirement for U.K. banks.

Mr. Holt said the bank's shareholders are also "waking up to this announcement" and will be consulted on the proposed sale and other options, such as a possible capital injection from new and existing shareholders. No potential buyers have been lined up, he said.

Who can buy the bank could be restricted for competition reasons. The country's major incumbents have previously been barred from swallowing rivals. But external interest in U.K. banks is currently weak, with shares in many lenders trading at depressed levels below book value.

A further bail-in of Co-op Bank's bonds is also possible. "At the moment" it is in the bank's liquidity plan to repay a 400 million ($499.4 million) senior bond maturing in September, Chief Executive Liam Coleman said.

Co-op Bank's biggest single shareholder, with around 20%, is former owner Co-operative Group Ltd ., a mutual that operates a chain of supermarkets.

Europe Vows to Maintain Postcrisis Financial Rules, Despite Trump Deregulation

By Julia-Ambra Verlaine

The European Union 's financial services chief vowed to maintain the continent's postcrisis regulations, even as the U.S. now moves to dismantle many of its rules.

"We very much hope to continue working with all our partners internationally, but this is not only in our hands," Valdis Dombrovskis, vice-president at the European Commission , the EU's executive arm, said in a speech in London Friday. "What is in the hands of the EU is to preserve recent reforms and firmly uphold our prudential framework.

In a separate appearance, Jean-Claude Trichet, the former head of the European Central Bank , said it "would be a total mistake" to embark on deregulation and expressed grave concern over stalled negotiations at the Basel Committee on Banking Standards, which is currently deciding on a final set of international capital requirements. Mr. Trichet, speaking at conference in Brussels on Thursday evening, warned that risks to the financial system remain high in light of increased public and private debt levels.

The men were speaking in the wake of pledges by new U.S. President Donald Trump to roll back the 2010 Dodd-Frank act, marking a sharp change in how the U.S. oversees its financial system and raising the prospect of creating gaps between the U.S. and Europe after years of attempts at close coordination.

Mr. Trump's new policies along with the U.K.'s Brexit vote to leave the EU puts Mr. Dombrovsksis in a precarious situation where his two largest allies, the U.K. and the U.S. are turning inwards and seeking to boost national industries.

"It is difficult not to notice when the Chair of the Federal Reserve is criticized for her approach to negotiating international rules and standards," said Mr. Dombrovsksis, referencing a recent letter sent by Rep. Patrick McHenry (R., N.C.), vice-chairman of the House Financial Services Committee, to Federal Reserve Chairwoman Janet Yellen. "Or when an American president talks about  doing a big number' on Dodd Frank."

While the commission hasn't taken a formal stance on Brexit or on Mr. Trump's financial services policies which EU officials say are still vague  Mr. Dombrovskis's speech reflects overall European concerns that the bloc may lose its prominence as a financial center if other jurisdictions begin to deregulate and put EU banks at a competitive disadvantage.

Mr. Dombrovskis says such "regulatory arbitrage" differences in rules from one country to another could put the global financial system at risk by incentivizing under-regulated activities for the sake of profit.

"Lax regulation in one country can create conditions for inadequate regulation and contagion throughout the world," he said. This is particularly alarming for some EU officials and international rulemakers who say financial stability shouldn't be taken for granted and that risks still loom on the horizon that could throw the EU and the world back into recession.

Mr. Dombrovskis also used his speech to cite examples of international cooperation between the U.S. and the EU: a recent agreement that allows clearinghouses on both sides of the Atlantic to operate under one set of rules and a deal struck last month smoothing the ability of insurance companies to operate in the two regions.

"Based on strong common international cooperation, the EU has been very open to recognize that our international partners' rules are equivalent and achieve the same objective as ours," said Mr. Dombrovskis.

Mr. Dombrovskis chose to open his speech with an evocative history lesson as he endeavors to preserve international coordination among financial regulators under threat from changing political tides.

"Five hundred years ago, a reformist movement started which would change the face of Europe: the Reformation," he said. "Five hundred years later, Europe faces a very different set of challenges to those pinned on a church door by Martin Luther. But there is a sense we too live in a period of disruptive change."

The year Martin Luther nailed his 95 theses to a church door in Germany decrying the status quo is often referenced by historians as a pivotal point in European history that provoked substantial change in the Western world.

Indeed, Mr. Dombrovskis's tenure has been marked by the disruptive political movements in the U.S. and the U.K. The former Latvian prime minister was thrown into his role as head of the EU's financial services portfolio in light of the U.K.'s vote to leave the union, which prompted his predecessor, Jonathan Hill, a U.K. citizen and commissioner at the time, to resign.

SEC Accuses Chinese Trader in Hollywood Insider-Trading Scheme

By Dave Michaels

U.S. securities regulators on Friday accused a Chinese private-equity firm partner of engineering an insider-trading scheme that reaped $29 million through buying shares of DreamWorks Animation SKG Inc . before it was acquired by Comcast Corp .

The Securities and Exchange Commission said it obtained a court order freezing brokerage accounts used by Shaohua "Michael" Yin, a Chinese citizen and graduate of the University of Pennsylvania's Wharton School who works at Hong Kong-based Summitview Capital Management Ltd. The SEC also sued five other people, including Mr. Yin's parents, who it said owned those accounts and were unjustly enriched by the operation.

A press release issued by the SEC on Friday said there was no known defense counsel for Mr. Yin, who didn't immediately return an email seeking comment.

The SEC alleged that Mr. Yin obtained inside information about DreamWorks' sale and then used five accounts owned by relatives and associates to buy $56 million in DreamWorks' stock over several weeks in April 2016. The trading yielded profits of over $29 million, the SEC said. The purchases in Mr. Yin's parents' accounts at Interactive Brokers LLC used borrowed money to finance much of the positions.

"Despite the defendant's alleged attempts to hide his control over these accounts, the SEC's data analytic investigative tools enabled us to determine who was behind the suspicious trading," said Michele Wein Layne, director of the SEC's Los Angeles office.

Comcast 's purchase of DreamWorks for $3.8 billion came after it caught word that the entertainment studio was in negotiations with Hong Kong-based investment firm PAG Asia Capital. In a complaint made public Friday, the SEC said the suspicious trading began after PAG and DreamWorks exchanged a confidential term sheet outlining the deal on April 4, 2016.

PAG has $16 billion in funds under management, according to its website. Last year, it teamed up with Apex Technology to buy U.S. printer company Lexmark International for $2.5 billion. PAG didn't immediately return an email seeking comment.

Comcast later increased its offer price for DreamWorks. The price of DreamWorks' stock shot up 47% after news of the deal leaked and was eventually confirmed by the company on April 28.

The SEC's complaint doesn't allege how Mr. Yin acquired the information about the impending deal. The 44-year-old Mr. Yin was listed as a partner at Summitview, according to regulatory filings. The SEC said he previously worked at UBS Investment Bank and Warburg Pincus Asia LLC.

Hong-Kong based Summitview isn't a registered investment adviser in the U.S. It manages a hedge fund, Summitview China Fund, that has assets of $500 million including borrowings, according to a regulatory filing.

The Federal Bureau of Investigation stopped Mr. Yin on Feb. 3 at the San Jose International Airport just before he flew back to China and served him with a search warrant, according the SEC's complaint. The SEC said Mr. Yin or the others involved in the scheme attempted to withdraw $22 million from the brokerage accounts in the past several days.

The owners of the brokerage accounts used to purchase DreamWorks stock included his parents, Lizhao Su and Zhiqing Yin, according to the SEC's complaint. The other accounts were opened by an electrical company employee, a teacher and a manager at PetroChina Coalbed Methane Co., the agency said.

The SEC said trading in the five accounts accounted for 17% of all trading in DreamWorks' shares over the three-week period in April.

WSJ Pro: Labor Department Takes Next Step on Retirement Savings Rule Delay

By Dave Michaels

WASHINGTON The Labor Department filed a formal notice Thursday seeking delay of a regulation widely opposed on Wall Street requiring brokers to elevate the interest of retirement savers ahead of their own financial gain.

The move follows an announcement last Friday by President Donald Trump that he would ask the department to review the rule, which the Obama administration had written and said would take effect April. 10.

The White House Office of Management and Budget is now reviewing the Labor proposal. Republicans have targeted the so-called fiduciary rule, completed during the final months of the Obama administration, for repeal or revision.

A delay would give Congress and the executive branch more time to consider ways to revise or eliminate the rule, which brokers say will cause them to abandon accounts that have relatively few assets in them.

The length of the proposed delay wasn't disclosed on a notice posted on OMB's website, which said it received the request Thursday.

A spokesman for the Labor Department didn't immediately return a message comment.

China's Securities Head Criticizes  Capitalist Crocodiles'

SHANGHAI China's securities czar once took a swipe at stock market speculators, calling them barbarians. Now, he's vowing to hunt down "big capitalist crocodiles" to protect ordinary investors.

Speaking at an annual work meeting of the China Securities Regulatory Commission on Friday, chairman Liu Shiyu took aim at deep-pocketed investors who try to manipulate stocks. He said authorities will catch these "capitalist crocodiles" who "skin and suck blood" from retail investors, according to people who attended the Beijing meeting, which lays out priorities for the year.

Mr. Liu's latest pledge highlights a prolonged effort by authorities to bring greater discipline to China's stock markets after a speculation-fueled boom ended in a stunning crash in mid-2015. The cleanup effort has included arrests for insider trading and market manipulation, as well as jawboning by the usually low-key Mr. Liu. In December he likened insurers to barbaric corporate raiders for using premium income to amass stakes in listed companies.

At a weekly media briefing, a spokesman said the commission aims to tackle illegal activities and risks at regional exchanges nationwide within the next six months, according to a statement posted on its website.

Mr. Liu didn't specify who the "crocodiles" are. Commission officials declined to elaborate. Last month a renowned Chinese fund manager, Xu Xiang, was sentenced to 5 1/2 years in prison for stock manipulation. A week later, billionaire Xiao Jianhua, founder of Tomorrow Holding, disappeared after being taken by Chinese authorities from his Four Seasons apartment in Hong Kong.

The disappearance of the politically-connected Mr. Xiao has sowed trepidation in some corners of Hong Kong, a capitalist enclave long used by members of the Chinese business elite as a safe harbor when political waters turned rough back home. It has also rattled Chinese finance circles.

Tomorrow Holding, a sprawling empire of investments from insurance to beet farming, hasn't responded to multiple requests for comment, including on Friday. A statement posted last week at one of its offices in Beijing said the company was operating normally and hasn't received notice of any investigation involving Mr. Xiao.

"The CSRC has been public about cleaning up the market, especially insider trading of small-cap stocks," said David Millhouse, head of China research at Hong Kong-based brokerage Forsyth Barr Asia. "If any person has been involved in this, it wouldn't be a surprise if the authorities are trying to prosecute them."

Stocks associated with Tomorrow Holding fell on Friday, despite a broad rally on the Shanghai market. Baotou Tomorrow Technology Co . slumped by 5%, while Baotou Huazi Industry Co. and Xishui Strong Year Co. Ltd Inner Mongolia fell by 1.3% and 0.4%, respectively.

Traders and analysts said the decline in those stocks was spurred by Mr. Liu's remarks. "Reactions so far to Mr. Liu's speech has been positive as he is seen as keen on rooting out irregularities," said Zhang Gang, an analyst at Central China Securities .

 Yifan Xie, Chao Deng and Josh Chin

How J.P. Morgan Won, Then Lost, Indonesia's Business

By Ben Otto, Rachel Rosenthal and I Made Sentana

On the Sunday after Donald Trump won the U.S. presidential election, sparking turmoil in emerging markets, J.P. Morgan Chase & Co . downgraded Indonesia's stock market. The move set off a chain reaction that resulted in the New York bank losing Indonesia as a client.

J.P. Morgan tried to smooth relations with Indonesia, as it had done several times in the past. At stake for the bank was a lucrative and decades-long partnership. Bank executives spoke with officials through back channels, pushed for meetings and pointed to subsequent reports with more-positive views on Indonesia's economy, said several people familiar with the efforts.

Analysts turned over to the finance ministry past market-research reports, and higher-level managers began to sign off on Indonesia research, according to one of the people. In January, the bank's analysts partly reversed their Indonesia equities call, bumping their recommendation up to "neutral" from "underweight."

But Indonesian officials were unmoved. They insisted that banks like J.P. Morgan , which were primary dealers for government-bond sales, have a responsibility to support the interests of the Southeast Asian nation. They were open to criticism, the officials said, but they felt J.P. Morgan 's report wasn't accurate or credible. Indonesia's finance ministry declined to comment for this article.

Investments banks have long weathered complaints that their research departments, which make calls on stocks, bonds and other assets, aren't truly independent from their investment-banking operations, which pitch deals to companies and governments.

But in Indonesia, and other parts of Asia, government officials' expectations for positive research are unabashed and unapologetic. That means global banks walk a fine line when publishing research their investment-banking clients don't like.

In Asia, some bankers say they are routinely quizzed about their research teams' reports when bidding for government business. In Indonesia, central-bank employees roam trading rooms, urging traders to be patriotic by refraining from selling the rupiah when the currency is under pressure, traders say.

A Bank Indonesia spokesman confirmed the bank sends officials to trading rooms during times of heavy selling pressure on the rupiah. He said he doesn't know what they say to dealers.

Rarely is the push to be positive as pronounced and public as it was with Indonesia's reaction to J.P. Morgan 's November report. Nor are the consequences typically as severe.

J.P. Morgan lost its Indonesian primary-dealer and underwriting business for government bonds, as well as its ability to receive payments made under the country's tax-amnesty program.

The dealer role gave J.P. Morgan important visibility into the much larger business of matching buyers and sellers of Indonesian government debt and currency, which brings in an estimated tens of millions of dollars in revenue for J.P. Morgan each year, analysts say. The bank can still trade government bonds and the rupiah, as well as continue to underwrite stock and debt for private companies.

J.P. Morgan says that its Indonesia business is operating as usual, that impact on clients from the government ban is minimal and that it is working with the finance ministry to resolve the matter.

J.P. Morgan 's first big run-in with Indonesia came after the collapse of Lehman Brothers in 2008, when lawmakers there blamed a bank recommendation to sell rupiah-denominated government bonds for accelerating a flow of money out of the country. Officials under Finance Minister Sri Mulyani Indrawati, a well-regarded, U.S.-trained economist who was almost three years into her first stint in the job, were primarily worried about controlling market reaction to the report, according to a person familiar with the matter.

Shortly thereafter, J.P. Morgan presented the ministry with a plan on how the bank would handle negative press and control the release of future reports, the person said, and flew in its Asia Pacific chief to meet with Indonesia's vice president.

Ms. Mulyani didn't publicly penalize J.P. Morgan . The bank's analysts toned down the language in Indonesian bond reports, changing "sell" calls to recommendations to reduce portfolio weighting, a person familiar with the research said.

In 2015, during the market tumult that followed a Chinese move to effectively devalue the yuan, the bank again lowered its investment recommendation on rupiah government bonds. A new finance minister, Bambang Brodjonegoro, called the move "unethical" and threatened sanctions, including, he joked, 100 push-ups.

Haryanto Budiman, a 48-year-old veteran of McKinsey & Co . and Indonesia's largest state bank who had become J.P. Morgan 's local chief four years before, led a blitzkrieg response, said people familiar with the matter. Mr. Budiman and colleagues met and texted with longtime friends and former colleagues at Bank Indonesia , the finance ministry and the financial-sector regulator. Mr. Brodjonegoro was eventually appeased, and recalls telling Mr. Budiman to be careful about research.

By the time Mr. Trump's election roiled emerging markets, Ms. Mulyani was back as finance minister, charged with building credibility for Indonesia's financial system. On the Friday after the election, the rupiah dropped as much as 2.7%, the Indonesian stock market fell 4% and Bank Indonesia poured an estimated $1 billion into rupiah purchases and bond buybacks. Behind the selloff were investor worries that emerging-market economies would suffer from Mr. Trump's protectionist trade policies, a strengthening U.S. dollar and outflows due to rising U.S. interest rates.

On the Monday after J.P. Morgan 's equities downgrade, a media story on the report began circulating "like a virus" on WhatsApp, one Indonesian banking executive said. The stock market slid another 2%. Ms. Mulyani, who had flown to Africa to attend a climate-change conference in Marrakesh, Morocco, canceled meetings and returned to Asia, people with knowledge of the matter said.

On Tuesday morning, Ms. Mulyani's officials called one of the report's co-authors to quiz him about the downgrade, according to people familiar with the call. J.P. Morgan 's Mr. Budiman was out of the country and tried to soothe tempers from abroad, one of the people said.

Ms. Mulyani declined to comment for this article.

Before the week was out, with the stock market, rupiah and bonds all down steeply, Ms. Mulyani wrote to Mr. Budiman. In the letter, she said Indonesia was cutting ties with the bank, according to people familiar with the letter.

Western diplomats met Indonesian finance ministry representatives to explain the division between research and investment banking and were told: "Yes, but they are the same. They are J.P. Morgan ," according to a person briefed on the conversation.

On Jan. 12, Ms. Mulyani hosted a meeting with analysts and economists. At the meeting, she cautioned them to be careful with their research reports, according to several attendees.

Since the meeting was only for primary dealers, J.P. Morgan wasn't invited.

WSJ Pro: Market Talk: Daily Analysis Roundup

Analyst: Tarullo's Exit is Good News for Bank Stocks

Fed Governor Daniel Tarullo's impending resignation, announced on Friday, is good news for bank stocks, writes analyst Brian Gardner of Keefe, Bruyette & Woods. Tarullo's exit will likely affect global systemically important banks the most, "because Tarullo was viewed by many investors and by the banking industry as the Fed's point man on regulation and one of the primary architects of the Dodd-Frank regulatory framework." Gardner expects Steven Mnuchin, once he is confirmed as Treasury secretary, to take the lead on choosing candidates to fill the Fed's three vacant seats on the Board of Governors. (gabriel.rubin@wsj.com; @rubinations)

Bank Investors Cheer Tarullo Resignation

Bank stocks spiked on news that Daniel Tarullo, the Fed's regulatory point man, will resign this spring. Bank of America (BAC) and Citigroup (C) saw almost a 1% rise in the half-hour following the announcement, while JPMorgan (JPM), Goldman (GS ) and Morgan Stanley (MS) saw smaller gains. Tarullo has been the face of tougher regulations since the crisis, which forced banks out of some profitable businesses and required them to hold more capital rather than share it with investors. Buy the resignation, sell the replacement? (liz.hoffman@wsj.com; @lizrhoffman)

Fed Up Campaign Laments Tarullo's Exit

Fed Up activists are sorry to see Fed Governor Daniel Tarullo go. "The financial crisis was devastating for working families and communities of color," Shawn Sebastian, Fed Up campaign co-director, says in a statement. "Without Daniel Tarullo at the Fed working to prevent the next crash, working people are the ones who will be hurt the most," he says, adding "Tarullo's departure makes all of us more vulnerable to unemployment, loss of wealth, and insecurity." (michael.derby@wsj.com)

HK Financial Regulator Working with R3 Blockchain Group

The Hong Kong Securities and Futures Commission is working with the R3 CEV blockchain group's member banks and technology providers to test blockchain online ledgers to record and settle securities and futures transactions, R3 managing director Charley Cooper tells WSJ. Financial services firms say blockchain can speed up and cut the costs of transactions but few have put systems into production, in part because US and other regulators haven't set clear guidelines. Other countries, including Canada, Hong Kong, Singapore and the UK, have collaborated on blockchain tests, putting those areas about a year ahead of the US, says Oliver Bussmann, a financial technology consultant and former UBS CIO. The entry of the Hong Kong Securities and Futures Commission into R3 formalizes work the two groups have done in the past several months, such as a proof-of-concept to show how blockchain ledgers may work in live financial systems, Cooper says. (kim.nash@wsj.com)

Chinese Companies Rush In With Nearly $2 Trillion Where Bankers Fear to Lend

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/chinese-companies-rush-in-with-nearly-2-trillion-where-bankers-fear-to-lend-1486636204"stepping in as lenders, as banks reduce their funding to struggling industries and the country's mammoth bond market comes under strain.

Company-to-company loans in China jumped by 20% last year to 13.2 trillion yuan ($1.92 trillion), according to research firm CEIC. That is roughly double the size of the loan book at Wells Fargo & Co ., the U.S.'s biggest lender. This entrusted lending, so named because banks serve as middlemen, is now the fastest-growing major component of the country's elaborate system of informal, or shadow, banking.

In the third quarter, prices rose in 87% of those markets from the same quarter a year earlier.

Hensarling Drops Bid For CFPB Commission | Pawlenty: No Big Finance Moves In Congress | Fiduciary-Rule Review Playbook | China's $2-Trillion Non-Bank Lending Rush | Hayashi's Take: Hensarling, Banks at Odds Over CFPB; The Wall Street Journal's financial regulation newsletter for Friday, February 10, 2017.
