Hayashi's Take: Hensarling's CFPB Salvo Illuminates Rift With Banks

Pawlenty: Congress Unlikely to Make Big Changes to Financial Policy

How the Fiduciary-Rule Review Is Likely to Play Out

Banks Sue U.S. Government Over Fed Dividend Cut

Chinese Companies Rush In With Nearly $2 Trillion Where Bankers Fear to Lend

Hensarling's CFPB Salvo Illuminates Rift With Banks

Jeb Hensarling, chairman of the powerful House Financial Services Committee, on Thursday "https://www.wsj.com/articles/hensarling-drops-bid-to-turn-cfpb-into-a-bipartisan-commission-1486678230" on the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau since the election, unveiling a raft of measures that would decimate the agency he has long criticized.

Don't expect an enthusiastic welcome from the banking industry.

The new blueprint, unveiled as part of the coming revisions to his financial deregulation legislation known as the Financial Choice Act, goes well beyond structural changes to the five-year-old agency previously discussed by Republicans and financial-industry representatives.

It would drastically shrink the bureau's authority in all of its main activities of rulemaking, supervision and enforcement, unwinding most of the consumer protection functions introduced after the financial crisis.

"It is a  blow-up-the-CFPB plan,' said Michael Calhoun , president of the Center for Responsible Lending , a nonprofit that supports the regulator.

While banks have long criticized the CFPB for what they see as regulatory overreach, many have made clear in recent weeks that eliminating the agency is not what they are looking for.

"We have already spent $40 billion for compliance of Dodd-Frank. The last thing we want to do is to go back to what it was pre-2008," Richard Hunt, president and chief executive of the Consumer Bankers Association, said in an interview this week. "We believe in consumer protection. It has to be there."

The CBA, along with three trade groups representing credit unions and community banks, have made a joint request to the Senate to overhaul the CFPB by turning its single-director structure into a five-member bipartisan commission. The goal is to reduce their compliance burden and slow the pace of new regulations coming from the bureau.

But departing from the "http://www.wsj.com/pro/financialregulation/graphics/understanding-the-financial-choice-act/", Mr. Hensarling's latest agenda doesn't include the introduction of a commission and instead calls for keeping the single-director structure, albeit with less independence for the director.

And surprising experts, it lists steps that offer an edge to nonbank lenders over traditional banks a plan banks are sure to have issues with. Under the proposed repeal of the CFPB's supervisory authority, nonbanks in mortgage and auto financing and payday lending would largely escape regulatory scrutiny. Banks with assets of more than $10 billion would also be freed of CFPB supervision but would continue to be tightly monitored by banking regulators such as the Federal Reserve and the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency .

"This is not a win for traditional banks," said Rohit Chopra, a former student-loan ombudsman at the CFPB.

It is unlikely the CFPB will be annihilated according to Mr. Hensarling's plan, and it's not just because of resistance from banks. The Trump administration has sent mixed signals about what it wants to do with the agency. White House spokesman Sean Spicer has called the CFPB unconstitutional, while Gary Cohn, chief economic adviser to Mr. Trump and a former Goldman Sachs president, has suggested his focus was on a "https://www.wsj.com/articles/trump-administration-looks-to-restructure-cfpb-1486116000", a step that would lead to Mr. Cordray's firing. Senate lawmakers, meanwhile, would likely seek less drastic measures than those unveiled by Mr. Hensarling, experts say.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

Hensarling Drops Bid to Turn CFPB Into a Bipartisan Commission

By Rachel Witkowski

WASHINGTON House Financial Services Committee Chairman Jeb Hensarling (R., Texas) is no longer seeking to turn the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau into a bipartisan commission, abandoning a long-sought goal for diffusing the bureau's single-director structure, according to a memo to committee leaders said.

Mr. Hensarling is expected to soon unveil a new version of legislation to significantly unwind key parts of financial laws enacted by the Obama administration, including limiting the power of the CFPB. A Feb. 6 memo reviewed by The Wall Street Journal outlines changes Mr. Hensarling expects to make from a version he introduced in the prior Congress.

Mr. Hensarling, among other things, intends to reduce the CFPB's authority, ease capital requirements for banks that are performing well and place limits on the stress testing of firms' ability to withstand shocks to the financial system. He also would trim other regulations the financial industry has said are overly burdensome.

People familiar with the matter said Republicans are now in favor of a single-director structure because eventually President Donald Trump could fill the position with someone who pursues a more business-friendly agenda.

Richard Cordray , appointed during the Obama administration, is currently the director in a term that runs through July 2018. Critics of the CFPB have pressed the Trump administration to remove him from the job before his term is up.

Mr. Hensarling in a Wednesday op-ed in The Wall Street Journal called for Mr. Trump to fire Mr. Cordray by "citing the president's constitutional responsibility to take care that the laws are faithfully executed."

"A new director could first undo all harmful actions taken by the CFPB during the Obama era," Mr. Hensarling wrote. "He could then implement policies that actually benefit consumers, such as limits on class-action lawsuits wherein plaintiff law firms get fortunes but injured financial consumers get pennies."

The memo also calls for significant curtailing of the CFPB's rule-making powers to be more like a "civil law enforcement agency" similar to the Federal Trade Commission "with additional restrictions on its authority." It would have a "sole director, removable by the president at-will." The proposal also would repeal the CFPB's consumer complaint database and eliminate its research and consumer education functions, among other changes.

Other significant proposals being considered would reduce regulatory oversight of smaller banks, according to the memo.

Mr. Hensarling's proposal faces opposition from Democrats who argue deregulating the financial system will harm consumers rather than help them.

Rep. Maxine Waters (D., Calif.), the top Democrat on the Financial Services panel in a written statement said, "This bill makes it crystal clear that Republicans mean to disarm our consumer protections, expose the American public to financial predators."

The ideas presented in the memo wouldn't wipe out the bureau completely but would leave it with much-reduced authority.

One significant change would be the repeal of its supervisory authority, which covers a vast swath of consumer financial markets from nonbank companies in mortgage and auto lending, student loan servicing, payday lending, debt collection, as well as banks and credit unions with assets over $10 billion. Resistance to the CFPB has been particularly strong from nonbank companies that had escaped regulatory scrutiny before the bureau was created,

It also includes steps that would prevent the CFPB from introducing new rules covering certain industries such as payday lenders, an industry that has fiercely opposed the bureau's new rules.

"This is obviously a huge windfall for payday lenders," said Rohit Chopra, a former student loan ombudsman at the CFPB and a senior Education Department official.

 Yuka Hayashi contributed to this article.

Pawlenty: Congress Unlikely to Make Big Changes to Financial Policy

By Gabriel T. Rubin

Congress is unlikely to make major changes affecting the financial-services industry despite Republican control of both chambers as well as the White House, said Tim Pawlenty , a financial lobbyist and former GOP governor of Minnesota.

"There's a lot of remaining uncertainty about where the priorities will land and what could actually get done," Mr. Pawlenty, chief executive of the Financial Services Roundtable, said in an interview with The Wall Street Journal.

He had higher hopes that Democratic senators, particularly those representing states President Donald Trump won in November, might cooperate with Republicans on a tax-code overhaul or changes to the Affordable Care Act, but was skeptical that they would go along with Republican plans to make significant changes to the financial-regulatory regime.

"You'd be hard pressed to say candidly that Democrats are going to cooperate substantially on financial-service reform," he said. "Absent a legal deadline or a crisis, it's  Katy, bar the door.' "

Without the cooperation of at least eight Democratic senators, Republicans would have trouble passing significant financial-services legislation. Most bills in the Senate must receive at least 60 votes to overcome the threat of a filibuster, and Republicans hold a slim majority of 52 seats. That makes more ambitious legislation, like the Financial Choice Act from House Financial Services Committee Chairman Jeb Hensarling (R., Texas), unlikely to find success in the Senate.

For that reason, Mr. Pawlenty and Financial Services Roundtable members which include many of the largest banks, investment firms and insurance companies are focusing intently on the appointments of senior regulators and administration officials who may have more of an impact on financial policy than Congress.

The key appointments include many of the second- and third-tier political appointees within agencies like the Treasury and Labor departments, as well as more prominent posts like the chairman of the Securities and Exchange Commission. Mr. Trump has nominated attorney Jay Clayton for that job, though he has yet to be confirmed. But perhaps most important among them is the Federal Reserve vice chair for supervision, a position that has been vacant since its creation under the 2010 Dodd-Frank regulatory-overhaul law. The role has been filled unofficially by Daniel Tarullo, the Fed's point person for regulatory matters.

Another post Mr. Pawlenty identified was the head of the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau , which is already the subject of significant political maneuvering in Washington. Several top Republicans, including Mr. Hensarling, have called on Mr. Trump to fire Director Richard Cordray immediately and replace him with a director more in tune with Republican concerns about the agency.

Mr. Hensarling removed language from the Choice Act that would have shifted the agency to a commission structure, according to a memo circulated among top Financial Services Committee Republicans that was reviewed by the Journal on Thursday.

That represents an about-face from the longstanding Republican position that the CFPB's structure should be changed to a five-person bipartisan commission, along the lines of the SEC or Commodity Futures Trading Commission .

Mr. Pawlenty said Republicans have leverage to persuade Democrats to support making the CFPB a commission.

"With Director Cordray's time being closer to the end than the beginning, [Democrats] may have to rethink their views on the commission structure," he said. "If they have a Trump person in there for six years, does that change their view on a commission?"

Mr. Pawlenty added, "There are 10 permutations to how this could go, but there's enough interesting political dynamics that you might be able to draw people in from both parties to get this done."

A CFPB spokeswoman has said Mr. Cordray had no plans to step down; his term runs through July 2018. In an interview at a WSJ Pro Financial Regulation event in late January, Mr. Cordray declined to say what he might do if Mr. Trump tried to fire him.

How the Fiduciary-Rule Review Is Likely to Play Out

By Lisa Beilfuss

New retirement-savings rules set to take effect in two months are under threat after President Donald Trump's order to review the regulation with an eye toward repealing or rescinding it, leaving the financial-advice industry guessing about next steps.

The fiduciary rule, due to take effect April 10, is meant to ensure that advisers who work with tax-advantaged retirement savings put clients' interests ahead of their own. It is unpopular with Republicans and some in the financial industry who say it would harm consumers more than help by limiting investment options, elevating costs and potentially cutting off low-balance customers from some forms of professional advice.

Some of the rule's backers say the work firms have done to become compliant and heightened consumer awareness mean it is too late to squash the spirit of the rule.

"The industry will ride to the rescue of the rule," said Paul Smith, chief executive of investment-professional group CFA Institute, adding that a decision to postpone the rule and then revamp or rescind it "may not be as popular a move as the administration thinks."

Here are some possible scenarios for how the review could play out.

Delay

Many experts say a delay in the regulation's April 10 implementation deadline is likely as the Labor Department conducts its review. A draft of last week's memo from the White House said the fiduciary rule's implementation date would be put off by six months, but the final memo didn't include specifics and left the call on a delay up to the department.

It's unlikely April 10 comes and goes without the Labor Department pushing out the applicability date, says Andrew Oringer, a partner at Dechert LLP and head of the firm's fiduciary practice. If the applicability date were to kick in, he said, "the path [to revising or revoking the rule] becomes cloudier."

Revision

The Labor Department could reopen the comment period on the fiduciary rule to reassess the industry's concerns, experts say, and could then under a new secretary rework the rule in a way that makes compliance less onerous. Mr. Trump's pick to lead the department, Andrew Puzder, is set for a confirmation hearing next week after a number of delays.

A revised rule could potentially relax the requirements of an exemption that allows brokers and other financial advisers to continue business as usual if their clients sign a contract freeing them from strict fiduciary care. It also could potentially drop the private right of action which allows for class-action suits and instead require clients to take their adviser to arbitration in the event of disputes over conduct and investments.

A revised rule along these lines "could take out a lot of the bite, but there would likely still be a means to enforce the rule," said Lisa Herrnson, a managing director at consultancy PwC.

The rule's review also could result in a carve-out of certain sales. Erin Sweeney, an attorney at Miller & Chevalier Chartered who represents parties in litigation regarding fiduciary obligations, said the analysis used to craft the rule as it now stands focused on mutual funds, underpinning complaints by the annuities industry that it is unfairly treated by the rule and that the threat would impinge upon sellers' business.

Restrictions on annuities have been at the heart of the rule as agents tend to earn commissions and other sales incentives. Chip Anderson, executive director for the National Association for Fixed Annuities, a trade association, said more than 50% of the annuities industry would be "severely affected," with potentially half of the industry's jobs at stake.

Repeal

Many experts say a repeal without replacement is an unlikely scenario, in part because the fiduciary rule is already a regulation, albeit one that isn't yet being enforced. That reality makes it difficult for the administration to quash the rule without putting forth an alternative rule.

"I don't think we're going back to where we started," said Ms. Sweeney.

In the final days of Barack Obama's presidency, the Labor Department issued a set of frequently asked questions targeting consumers that could help entrench the concept in the public's mind. The market was already trending toward a higher standard of care, Mr. Oringer said, and some firms "will point to it as a marketing opportunity."

A trifecta of court decisions upholding the fiduciary rule also makes it tougher for the new administration to altogether kill the rule, Ms. Sweeney said. A Texas judge this week handed the latest victory to the Labor Department, rejecting a challenge led by the Chamber of Commerce seeking to sink the regulation. The decision follows similar opinions in Washington, D.C. and Kansas. The court opinions upholding the rule, Ms. Sweeney said, "creates a stumbling block for the Trump administration" as it goes "back to the drawing board."

Congress or SEC Action

Arjun Saxena, a principal at PwC who works with financial-services clients, said the legislative route remains a viable one particularly if the implementation date is delayed as it would give Congress time to come up with a replacement.

Late last year, after Mr. Trump's presidential victory, Rep. Brad Sherman (D., Calif.), a member of the House Financial Services Committee , said he could conceive of a "pared-down rule that at least limits the size of commissions." Such pay, which brokers and annuities sellers generate by selling investment products, can usher in conflicts of interest and is at the heart of the fiduciary rule.

Some experts say a delay would give the Securities and Exchange Commission an opportunity to produce a uniform standard that covers all savings, not just retirement savings.

Others, though, say it would take at least a couple of years for the SEC to craft such a rule and give the industry time to comply. "We'd be comfortably into 2019," Mr. Smith said.

Whichever the scenario, Mr. Oringer said, a revamped rule would be "principles-based" and less stringent than the current regulation.

Banks Sue U.S. Government Over Fed Dividend Cut

By David Harrison

A major trade group for the banking industry sued the government Thursday over cuts to the money banks receive from the Federal Reserve enacted as part of a highway and mass-transit funding measure in 2015.

The American Bankers Association , joined by Seattle-based Washington Federal, says the law violates the contract between banks and the Fed by reducing dividends paid to member banks and looks to reimburse banks for their lost payments.

The 1913 law creating the Fed required that banks purchase stock in regional Fed banks as a way to fund the central bank and protect it from unexpected losses. In exchange, banks would receive a 6% annual dividend payment.

The transportation bill targeted those payments to help cover its $305 billion price tag. Banks with greater than $10 billion in assets would see their dividends set at 6% or at the yield of the 10-year Treasury note during the most recent auction before the dividend, whichever was smaller. Yields on Treasurys have been below 2.5% for much of the past year, which would represent a significant dividend cut.

The Congressional Budget Office estimates the dividend cut would raise almost $7 billion in new government funding over 10 years.

"The change to the statutory dividend rate upended Federal Reserve System policy that has been in place for more than 100 years," said ABA President Rob Nichols. "This action undercuts the work banks do every day to help drive economic growth and meet the needs of America's businesses and communities. It is particularly damaging for affected community banks, which have had a much more difficult time replacing the lost income."

The ABA lobbied against the dividend cut during congressional deliberations over the bill.

The legislation also tapped into a Fed surplus account by capping it at $10 billion. That move brought in another $53.3 billion over 10 years dedicated to the bill.

WSJ Pro: Pence Hires Cato's Calabria as Chief Economist

By Andrew Ackerman

WASHINGTON Vice President Mike Pence has hired a former Republican Senate aide and prolific critic of the mortgage-finance system as his chief economist, according to people familiar with the matter.

Mark Calabria, who formerly ran financial-regulation studies at the libertarian Cato Institute , began working on Mr. Pence's staff Wednesday, a person familiar with the move said. Mr. Calabria's portfolio would include a range of issues besides mortgage finance such as tax and trade matter, the person said.

From his perch in Mr. Pence's office, Mr. Calabria could play a key role in shaping deregulatory legislation as it works its way through Congress. One of Mr. Pence's roles in the Trump administration is to serve as a liaison with Congress.

A former aide to ex-Sen. Phil Gramm (R., Texas) and Sen. Richard Shelby (R., Ala.), Mr. Calabria has pushed to end government support of mortgage giants Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac , urged Congress to repeal the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial overall and called for curbs to the federal mortgage interest deduction.

Mr. Calabria backs a plan to replace Dodd-Frank authored by House Financial Services Committee Chairman Jeb Hensarling (R., Tex.) known as the Financial Choice Act. That plan, Mr. Calabria has said, would raise capital requirements for banks while reducing compliance costs and what he described as over-reliance on regulatory discretion.

Mr. Hensarling's legislation, which cleared the House last year and is expected to again advance through the chamber in the coming weeks, moves the financial system "in the direction of more stability and fewer bailouts," Mr. Calabria wrote this fall.

WSJ Pro: GOP Senator Criticizes Fed's Stress Tests

By Ryan Tracy

Sen. Pat Toomey wrote to Federal Reserve Chairwoman Janet Yellen Thursday criticizing the regulator's annual "stress tests."

The Pennsylvania Republican in his letter urged the Fed "to consider holding off on rules that would add to the existing regulatory framework" until the Trump administration reviews existing rules and the Senate confirms Mr. Trump's yet-to-be-named nominees to the Fed's governing board.

The letter comes as the Fed is working on a proposal that would make the stress tests harder for the very largest U.S. banks by adding a capital "surcharge" to the exams.

The Fed couldn't immediately be reached for comment.

BOE's Charlotte Hogg Named Deputy Governor for Banking and Markets

By Jason Douglas

Ms. Hogg will be one of the most powerful officials at the central bank, with a seat on both the interest-rate-setting Monetary Policy Committee and its Financial Policy Committee, which safeguards the stability of the U.K. financial system.

Her new role also combines her chief operating officer duties with those of the deputy governor, the BOE said, putting her in charge of the daily running of the institution as well as swaths of the bank's operations in financial markets. Both the COO job and deputy governor for markets and banking are relatively new posts, created in an overhaul of BOE governance implemented by Gov. Mark Carney soon after he joined in 2013.

Ms. Hogg joins the BOE's policy committees at a challenging time. The U.K. vote to leave the European Union in June has clouded the economic outlook and raised questions over the future of London's huge financial sector. The BOE cut its benchmark rate to a new low in August as part of a multipronged stimulus package to shore up the economy. But officials' easy-money stance is being tested by quickening inflation.

Ms. Hogg's views on interest-rate and financial-stability policy are unclear. Though she worked at the BOE early in her career, she has spent most of her working life elsewhere in finance, with stints in senior roles at Morgan Stanley and the U.K. unit of Spain's Banco Santander SA .

"She's a bit of an unknown quantity," said Peter Dixon, an economist at Commerzbank in London, referring to where she stands on policy. But he added it isn't unusual for the Treasury to draw on candidates with experience beyond academia and policy to fill such senior roles.

In a statement, U.K. Treasury chief Philip Hammond praised Ms. Hogg's leadership skills and experience, saying she steps into her new role at a critical time.

Separately, the BOE said Kristin Forbes, one of the four members of the nine-member MPC drawn from outside the BOE's ranks, won't seek a second term in office after her initial three-year spell expires June 30. Ms. Forbes, a U.S. academic who advised President George W. Bush on economic policy, plans to return full time to the Massachusetts Institute of Technology , where she is a professor of management and global economics.

Ms. Forbes is among the more hawkish members of the rate-setting panel, saying in a speech Tuesday that she may soon vote for higher borrowing costs to contain inflation. Her departure could "modestly dilute" the chance of an early interest-rate rise if the economy proves resilient this year, said Howard Archer, economist at IHS Global Insight in London.

Sinking Feeling: Shipping Is Latest European Banking Worry

By William Wilkes in Hamburg and Max Colchester in London

The global shipping crisis is stoking another European banking headache, this time in economic powerhouse Germany.

Commerzbank AG on Thursday warned that its losses on shipping loans could be as high as  ¬600 million ($641 million) this year after nearly doubling last year to  ¬559 million. Last week, Deutsche Bank AG , Germany's largest bank, said its expected losses from shipping loans nearly tripled, to  ¬346 million, from a year earlier.

In Germany's port of Hamburg one of Europe's richest cities state-owned HSH Nordbank is racing to find a buyer or face liquidation after suffering massive losses on shipping-related debt.

Analysts say the continued wave of write-downs indicates German banks remain hesitant to take the painful measures needed to restructure their businesses, almost a decade after the global economic crisis.

Before the crisis, German lenders became the world's biggest issuers of shipping loans. As of last year they owned roughly $90 billion worth, or almost one-fourth of all outstanding shipping loans made by large banks, according to Petrofin Global Bank Research. German banks and investors own more of the world's containership capacity than any other country roughly 29%, according to the German Shipowners' Association.

Plunging world shipping rates and the bankruptcy of South Korea's Hanjin Shipping last year helped create a bloodbath for the sector, which had previously seemed a safe bet for export-oriented Germany. Banks world-wide now are racing to dump bad shipping loans while shippers scramble to unload worthless ships.

"The market is deteriorating and everybody is trying to exit," said Basil Karatzas, founder of Karatzas Marine Advisors & Co. in New York.

Shipping losses are especially hard on German banks, which are trying to recover from the euro crisis, record-low interest rates and, at Deutsche Bank , scandals. Rather than aggressively write down shipping loans early, German banks let them linger and eat into profits. The eurozone's banking watchdog has said it is increasing on-site inspections at several German banks to resolve shipping-loan problems. Banks are lobbying global regulators to relax rules on how much capital must be held against these loans.

No bank epitomizes the problem more clearly than HSH Nordbank , whose lending spree left it owning a commercial fleet larger than the British, French and German military navies combined. European Union authorities have ordered the bank to be privatized by early next year or shut. Taxpayers in northern Germany face losses of up to  ¬15 billion, or over  ¬3,000 a person.

"It's a catastrophe," said Rainer Kerstin, the head of the taxpayer's association in the German state of Schleswig-Holstein, which with Hamburg owns HSH.

During the boom years, HSH bankers would mingle with local politicians and shipping magnates in a vast beer hall under Hamburg city hall. Several of those same politicians and businessmen sat on HSH's supervisory board and encouraged it to become the world's biggest shipping lender.

After the shipping collapse, HSH's owners rescued it by pumping in billions of taxpayer euros and pledging to reimburse HSH for  ¬10 billion of losses on dud loans. EU authorities deemed that illegal state aid. HSH's government owners were ordered to sell their 85% stake by next February.

The bank is racing to make itself attractive. It created a "bad bank" that last year took ownership of 253 container ships. HSH's management has conducted roadshows in London marketed to potential Asian investors.

Indicative bids for the bank are expected later in February. Any deal requires EU approval, adding another hurdle.

In Hamburg, the fallout is spreading. Hamburg businessman Bernd Kortm sat on HSH's advisory board from 2004 to June 2015, a period in which the bank extended billions of euros in credit to his shipping firm. Late last year, his company received some  ¬500 million in debt relief from HSH. Around that time Mr. Kortm purchased a 130-foot racing yacht.

Local politicians were outraged. "I'm totally disgusted by it," said Wolfgang Kubicki, member of Schleswig-Holstein's state parliament for the pro-business FDP party. "We have to build schools, fund police, fix streets, but this millionaire gets debts written off. I cannot see any justification for it."

Mr. Kortm said that if the shipping market improves, HSH will get all its money back. He declined to say more, referring to other interviews in which he said the timing of his yacht purchase was "unfortunate."

HSH Chief Executive Officer Stefan Ermisch recently told staff in a memo that "heated" discussions of the "sad" past events could damage the cleanup and privatization.

The short privatization time scale means the bank will probably need to sell bad loans at sharply discounted values, exhausting its  ¬10 billion in state guarantees, Mr. Ermisch told staff. Some costs are proving hard to cut: HSH by law must maintain headquarters in both the federal states that own it.

Corrections & Amplifications

Wolfgang Kubicki is a member of Schleswig-Holstein's state parliament. An earlier of this article incorrectly stated he was in Hamburg's parliament.

Chinese Companies Rush In With Nearly $2 Trillion Where Bankers Fear to Lend

By Rachel Rosenthal and Anjie Zheng

Chinese companies are increasingly stepping in as lenders, as banks reduce their funding to struggling industries and the country's mammoth bond market comes under strain.

Company-to-company loans in China jumped by 20% last year to 13.2 trillion yuan ($1.92 trillion), according to research firm CEIC. That is roughly double the size of the loan book at Wells Fargo & Co ., the U.S.'s biggest lender. This entrusted lending, so named because banks serve as middlemen, is now the fastest-growing major component of the country's elaborate system of informal, or shadow, banking.

The most recent surge came during the selloff in China's $9.3 trillion bond market late last year. Big, cash-rich companies mostly state-owned enterprises and some private companies stepped in: New entrusted loans rose to 405.7 billion yuan ($59.02 billion) in December, more than double the month prior, according to data tracker Wind Information, and the highest monthly issuance in two years.

But the practice poses broader risks for China's economy.

Instead of investing in their core business, companies can earn interest rates of up to 20% making entrusted loans, often with only cursory checks on borrowers' creditworthiness. Such lending often props up companies in sectors like mining and property where Beijing wants to reduce excess capacity. It also adds to China's $18 trillion corporate debt pile, already equal to 168% of gross domestic product, according to the Bank for International Settlements .

Some entrusted loans are between a company and its own subsidiaries, similar to how many big companies globally loan cash to different parts of their businesses. Still, between 2007 and 2013 more than 60% of entrusted loans were channeled to companies in industries with overcapacity, according to a study by the U.S.-based National Bureau of Economic Research .

"It's not a sustainable business model" for the lending companies, said Julian Evans-Pritchard, China economist at Capital Economics. "Their main operations are only staying afloat by acting like a shadow bank."

Aluminum Corp. of China Ltd ., or Chalco, is one major company where lending has become a big business. With its main smelting operations struggling due to a glut of aluminum, the company has ramped up its lending to boost profits. Chalco, a publicly traded unit of state-owned Chinalco , took in 30.7 million yuan in interest income from entrusted loans in the first six months of 2016 more than four times as much as a year earlier and nearly 50% of its net profit. In a texted response to a request for comment, Chalco said the increase in entrusted lending was part of an effort to centralize capital management and employ assets flexibly while lowering funding costs.

Company-to-company lending took off in China in the 1990s when, after a period of rapid growth, many state-owned firms started generating large amounts of cash. With no private shareholders pushing for dividend payouts, many put that cash to work by lending it out.

Big corporations in other countries have in the past built large financing arms. GE Capital , a unit of General Electric , at one point became a top lender in the U.S.

But entrusted lending is unusual. Banks are involved, but only as a middleman: Direct company-to-company lending is still legally prohibited. Banks can charge fees of up to 5% of the loan, according to BMI Research, but leave credit checks to the lending company.

"When a bank lends, they have to do their homework, they have experienced credit analysts," said Warut Promboon, chief ratings officer at Chinese firm Dagong Global Credit Rating Co. Mr. Promboon added that in his experience, few Chinese companies have the personnel to adequately assess credit risks.

In some cases, lending companies aren't pulling back even when loans sour.

 When a bank lends, they have to do their homework, they have experienced credit analysts.'

 Warut Promboon, Dagong Global Credit Rating

In June, Datang International Power Generation Co ., a state-owned energy company, said it would write off $8 billion of its $10 billion portfolio of entrusted loans made a year earlier to small coal companies in which it owned stakes.

"If our subsidiaries suffer more, it will make it more difficult for them to get bank loans," said a Datang International representative. "In this case, we may issue more entrusted loans."

Entrusted lending can provide a last-ditch option for companies blacklisted from other funding avenues. Chinese banks aren't allowed to extend normal loans to companies in default.

In December, China Cinda Asset Management Co . agreed to provide Shandong Shanshui Cement Group Ltd . an entrusted loan of up to 8 billion yuan ($1.16 billion). The loan's terms weren't disclosed; Shanshui declined to comment. China Cinda couldn't be reached for comment.

Shanshui, one of China's biggest cement makers, had defaulted on nearly 12 billion yuan ($1.75 billion) of its debt as of June 2016, according to disclosures. A slump in China's cement industry drove defaults and bondholder lawsuits.

Companies sometimes charge sky-high rates for loans to risky industries. Zhejiang Longsheng Group Co ., a privately owned chemicals producer whose customers include Nike Inc . and Wal-Mart Stores Inc ., according to its website, is charging 17% interest four times China's one-year benchmark lending rate for a one-year, 250 million yuan loan to Zhejiang Time Square Trading Co., a company involved in the property sector, according to disclosures. Neither company could be reached for comment.

 Carol Chan contributed to this article.

Corrections & Amplifications

A trillion yuan is equivalent to $145 billion. A chart accompanying an earlier version of this article incorrectly stated it was equivalent to $1.45 billion. (Feb. 9, 2017)

WSJ Pro: Market Talk: Daily Analysis Roundup

Italy's Bad Loans Flat in Dec. On-Year, Says Bank of Italy

Gross bad loans at Italian banks were roughly flat in December at EUR200.87 billion, from the same month a year earlier, the Bank of Italy says. The figure refers to the most toxic bad loans, or sofferenze, where a debtor is deemed insolvent. They rose 1% from November, the central bank adds. Bad loans net of provisions for losses stood at EUR86.88 billion in December, or 2% down from Dec 2015, the Bank of Italy also says. (giovanni.legorano@wsj.com)

Pence's New Chief Economist Could Help Craft De-Regulatory Legislation

Vice President Mike Pence has tapped Mark Calabria, director of financial-regulation studies at the libertarian Cato Institute , as his chief economist. The pick is significant because Mr. Calabria, a prolific critic of Dodd-Frank and the housing-finance system, could play a key role in shaping deregulatory legislation as it works its way through Congress. That's because one of Mr. Pence's roles in the Trump administration is to serve as a liaison with Congress. (andrew.ackerman@wsj.com; @amacker)

Analyst: House Republican Dodd-Frank Overhaul A Tough Sell in Senate

The contours of House Republicans' latest plan to overhaul the 2010 Dodd- Frank law are coming into focus with the leaking of a memo describing their latest bill. Jaret Seiberg, an analyst with Cowen & Co., questions whether much of what is contained in the memo could pass the Senate, where Democrat votes will be needed. Democrats are likely to a oppose significant changes to the authority of the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau , for example, Mr. Seiberg said in a note to clients. "We believe this bill is more about sending a message on how the GOP wants to change the law than it is about putting forward a package that could reach the President's desk," he wrote. (Ryan.Tracy@wsj.com; @ryanjtracy)

Republican Bill Would Keep CFPB's Single Director

Rep. Jeb Hensarling, the top Republican on the House Financial Services Committee , changed his Financial Choice Act to keep the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau as a single-director agency, albeit with more limited powers. Hensarling and other Republicans had previously favored a five-person bipartisan commission similar to the structure of the Securities and Exchange Commission . The change could be an attempt by Republicans to pressure Senate Democrats, who would be wary of a Trump-appointed director, to accept a commission structure in the future. (gabriel.rubin@wsj.com; @Rubinations)

Analysis: Banks Safer But Less Profitable Since Crisis

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/trump-fixes-flaw-in-post-crisis-regulatory-crackdown-1486582335?tesla=y"ordered a comprehensive review of financial regulations last week, brows furrowed. "Very worrisome," fretted Mario Draghi, president of the European Central Bank. Will banks "blow up our economy again?" asked Democratic senator Elizabeth Warren.

The worriers should relax. In the 10 years since the financial crisis began, the regulatory pendulum has moved relentlessly in the direction of tougher restrictions on finance. Mr. Trump's order reverses the direction of the pendulum but there is little sign his administration wants it back to where it was in 2007.

Trump's Trade Policy Impact on Financial Services

The Trump administration's "America First" protectionist trade policy could have deeply adverse consequences for cross-border financial services, according to an "http://www.fedfin.com/images/stories/client_reports/Issue by Federal Financial Analytics Inc ., a consulting firm. "No nation facing barriers to trade in goods at the U.S. will welcome U.S. finance, nor will any agreements on goods be revised without attention also to financial services the knot is simply too tightly tied in the fabric of global trade policy," they write. U.S. financial-services firms must quickly identify stress points for forthcoming trade talks, anticipate the adverse implications of changes in U.S. trade policy, and work with Congress and regulatory agencies to minimize the potential harm of trade policies to the financial sector, they write.

The Volcker Rule Is a 21st-Century Glass-Steagall Act

During his presidential campaign, Donald Trump supported a "21st-century Glass-Steagall Act" without outlining exactly what that might look like. Such a policy could come out of the existing Volcker rule, which puts restrictions on banks' proprietary trading, "https://www.americanbanker.com/opinion/a-21st-century-glass-steagall-its-called-the-volcker-rule". "Think of the Volcker rule as a halfway step back to the explicit and complete division between commercial and investment banking under Glass-Steagall," he writes. The Volcker rule must be overhauled so that it doesn't impose an undue compliance burden and doesn't have an overwhelmingly adverse effect on liquidity, he says. But nonetheless, "by separating the client activities such as lending and securities underwriting from principal trading for the bank's account, the Volcker rule addresses one of the most pernicious and least-discussed problems in the world of finance," Mr. Whalen writes.

You May Be in a Risky Business if Regulators Find It Distasteful

Charles W. Calomiris, a Columbia Business School professor and co-director of the Hoover Institution 's Program on Regulation and the Rule of Law, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/you-may-be-in-a-risky-business-if-regulators-find-it-distasteful-1486686512" questions regulatory practices by the government that he says "have shifted from issuing formal rules to providing guidance" and creates a "veil of secrecy because it maximizes their discretionary power and places the unpredictable and discriminatory costs on banks and their customers." Mr. Calomiris calls for a review of such practices and an overhaul of how regulation works. "Regulation should be based on clearly defined standards, debated in public and enforced transparently. To accomplish that, Congress will have to change the regulatory process fundamentally."

Outwitting the FBI and the SEC

Steve Cohen's fund was once the most successful on Wall Street. Then it pleaded guilty to insider trading. But Mr. Cohen was never charged. "https://www.wsj.com/articles/outwitting-the-fbi-and-the-sec-1486511759" "Black Edge: Inside Information, Dirty Money, and the Quest to Bring Down the Most Wanted Man on Wall Street" by Sheelah Kolhatkar.

Hayashi's Take: Hensarling's CFPB Salvo Illuminates Rift With Banks

Hensarling's CFPB Salvo Illuminates Rift With Banks

Jeb Hensarling, chairman of the powerful House Financial Services Committee, on Thursday "https://www.wsj.com/articles/hensarling-drops-bid-to-turn-cfpb-into-a-bipartisan-commission-1486678230" on the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau since the election, unveiling a raft of measures that would decimate the agency he has long criticized.

Don't expect an enthusiastic welcome from the banking industry.

The new blueprint, unveiled as part of the coming revisions to his financial deregulation legislation known as the Financial Choice Act, goes well beyond structural changes to the five-year-old agency previously discussed by Republicans and financial-industry representatives.

It would drastically shrink the bureau's authority in all of its main activities of rulemaking, supervision and enforcement, unwinding most of the consumer protection functions introduced after the financial crisis.

"It is a  blow-up-the-CFPB plan,' said Michael Calhoun , president of the Center for Responsible Lending , a nonprofit that supports the regulator.

While banks have long criticized the CFPB for what they see as regulatory overreach, many have made clear in recent weeks that eliminating the agency is not what they are looking for.

"We have already spent $40 billion for compliance of Dodd-Frank. The last thing we want to do is to go back to what it was pre-2008," Richard Hunt, president and chief executive of the Consumer Bankers Association, said in an interview this week. "We believe in consumer protection. It has to be there."

The CBA, along with three trade groups representing credit unions and community banks, have made a joint request to the Senate to overhaul the CFPB by turning its single-director structure into a five-member bipartisan commission. The goal is to reduce their compliance burden and slow the pace of new regulations coming from the bureau.

But departing from the "http://www.wsj.com/pro/financialregulation/graphics/understanding-the-financial-choice-act/", Mr. Hensarling's latest agenda doesn't include the introduction of a commission and instead calls for keeping the single-director structure, albeit with less independence for the director.

And surprising experts, it lists steps that offer an edge to nonbank lenders over traditional banks a plan banks are sure to have issues with. Under the proposed repeal of the CFPB's supervisory authority, nonbanks in mortgage and auto financing and payday lending would largely escape regulatory scrutiny. Banks with assets of more than $10 billion would also be freed of CFPB supervision but would continue to be tightly monitored by banking regulators such as the Federal Reserve and the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency .

"This is not a win for traditional banks," said Rohit Chopra, a former student-loan ombudsman at the CFPB.

It is unlikely the CFPB will be annihilated according to Mr. Hensarling's plan, and it's not just because of resistance from banks. The Trump administration has sent mixed signals about what it wants to do with the agency. White House spokesman Sean Spicer has called the CFPB unconstitutional, while Gary Cohn , chief economic adviser to Mr. Trump and a former Goldman Sachs president, has suggested his focus was on a "https://www.wsj.com/articles/trump-administration-looks-to-restructure-cfpb-1486116000", a step that would lead to Mr. Cordray's firing. Senate lawmakers, meanwhile, would likely seek less drastic measures than those unveiled by Mr. Hensarling, experts say.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

Hensarling Drops Bid to Turn CFPB Into a Bipartisan Commission

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/hensarling-drops-bid-to-turn-cfpb-into-a-bipartisan-commission-1486678230"Chairman Jeb Hensarling (R., Texas) is no longer seeking to turn the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau into a bipartisan commission, abandoning a long-sought goal for diffusing the bureau's single-director structure, according to a memo to committee leaders said.

Mr. Hensarling is expected to soon unveil a new version of legislation to significantly unwind key parts of financial laws enacted by the Obama administration, including limiting the power of the CFPB. A Feb. 6 memo reviewed by The Wall Street Journal outlines changes Mr. Hensarling expects to make from a version he introduced in the prior Congress.

Pawlenty: Congress Unlikely to Make Big Changes to Financial Policy

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/pawlenty-congress-unlikely-to-make-big-changes-to-financial-policy-1486720800" affecting the financial-services industry despite Republican control of both chambers as well as the White House, said Tim Pawlenty, a financial lobbyist and former GOP governor of Minnesota.

"There's a lot of remaining uncertainty about where the priorities will land and what could actually get done," Mr. Pawlenty, chief executive of the Financial Services Roundtable, said in an interview with The Wall Street Journal. He had higher hopes that Democratic senators, particularly those representing states President Donald Trump won in November, might cooperate with Republicans on a tax-code overhaul or changes to the Affordable Care Act, but was skeptical that they would go along with Republican plans to make significant changes to the financial-regulatory regime.

How the Fiduciary-Rule Review Is Likely to Play Out

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/how-the-fiduciary-rule-review-is-likely-to-play-out-1486660677" set to take effect in two months are under threat after President Donald Trump 's order to review the regulation with an eye toward repealing or rescinding it, leaving the financial-advice industry guessing about next steps.

The fiduciary rule, due to take effect April 10, is meant to ensure that advisers who work with tax-advantaged retirement savings put clients' interests ahead of their own. It is unpopular with Republicans and some in the financial industry who say it would harm consumers more than help by limiting investment options, elevating costs and potentially cutting off low-balance customers from some forms of professional advice.

Trump's Trade Policy Impact on Financial Services

The Trump administration's "America First" protectionist trade policy could have deeply adverse consequences for cross-border financial services, according to an "http://www.fedfin.com/images/stories/client_reports/Issue by Federal Financial Analytics Inc ., a consulting firm. "No nation facing barriers to trade in goods at the U.S. will welcome U.S. finance, nor will any agreements on goods be revised without attention also to financial services the knot is simply too tightly tied in the fabric of global trade policy," they write. U.S. financial-services firms must quickly identify stress points for forthcoming trade talks, anticipate the adverse implications of changes in U.S. trade policy, and work with Congress and regulatory agencies to minimize the potential harm of trade policies to the financial sector, they write.

The Volcker Rule Is a 21st-Century Glass-Steagall Act

During his presidential campaign, Donald Trump supported a "21st-century Glass-Steagall Act" without outlining exactly what that might look like. Such a policy could come out of the existing Volcker rule, which puts restrictions on banks' proprietary trading, "https://www.americanbanker.com/opinion/a-21st-century-glass-steagall-its-called-the-volcker-rule". "Think of the Volcker rule as a halfway step back to the explicit and complete division between commercial and investment banking under Glass-Steagall," he writes. The Volcker rule must be overhauled so that it doesn't impose an undue compliance burden and doesn't have an overwhelmingly adverse effect on liquidity, he says. But nonetheless, "by separating the client activities such as lending and securities underwriting from principal trading for the bank's account, the Volcker rule addresses one of the most pernicious and least-discussed problems in the world of finance," Mr. Whalen writes.

You May Be in a Risky Business if Regulators Find It Distasteful

Charles W. Mr. Calomiris calls for a review of such practices and an overhaul of how regulation works. "Regulation should be based on clearly defined standards, debated in public and enforced transparently. To accomplish that, Congress will have to change the regulatory process fundamentally."

The "https://www.wsj.com/articles/daniel-tarullo-federal-reserve-regulatory-point-man-resigning-1486751401", the Federal Reserve's de facto head of bank regulation, is another step forward for the Trump administration's plan to ease rules for Wall Street. It will also give the new administration a chance to quickly influence U.S. monetary policy.

Mr. Tarullo's departure, effective early April, brings the number of vacant seats on the Federal Reserve Board to three out of seven. Donald Trump's views on monetary policy are unclear. But he is clearly of the view that "https://www.wsj.com/articles/trump-reforms-are-stuff-of-banker-dreams-1486119392", harming the economy.

Mr. Tarullo, on the other hand, has been a leading advocate of forcing banks to hold more capital. "He was unusual in that he did have such strong views and such a forceful approach to decision-making," said Karen Petrou, analyst at Federal Financial Analytics, echoing a view of Mr. Tarullo "https://www.wsj.com/articles/daniel-tarullo-the-one-man-judge-and-jury-for-banks-1464720855".

Mr. Tarullo pushed for the "gold-plating" of U.S. capital requirements, exceeding internationally agreed minimums, hoping other countries would follow suit. Gary Cohn, the former Goldman Sachs No. 2 and current director of the White House National Economic Council, seemed to object to this view last week, when he griped that U.S. banks are "way, way, way" ahead of European rivals in terms of capital levels.

At the same time, Mr. Tarullo has insisted that big capital cushions alone aren't sufficient. "https://www.wsj.com/articles/feds-tarullo-criticizes-house-republicans-bank-reform-plan-1480702971" to a proposal by Republican Congressman Jeb Hensarling that would exempt banks from broad swaths of regulation in return for higher capital ratios, saying it could prompt them to swap safer, liquid assets for riskier ones.

With Mr. Tarullo gone, the likes of Mr. Cohn and Mr. Hensarling have freer reign to put in place their own regulatory framework. It will be far more pleasing to bankers than the one Mr. Tarullo leaves as his legacy.

(The following is a roundup of selected companies that have revealed securities sales in recent Form D regulatory filings. Ben Franklin Technology Partners of Southeastern Pennsylvania and Technology Commercialization Fund are backers.

Saudi Sovereign-Wealth Fund Hires Stock-and-Bond Chief; Veteran European banker Cyrille Urfer will become a top manager for Saudi Arabia's Public Investment Fund

By Justin Scheck and Summer Said

Saudi Arabia has hired a European banking veteran as the new stock-and-bond chief for its sovereign-wealth fund, a key step in the country's plan to build the fund into one of the world's biggest investors and help "https://www.wsj.com/articles/saudis-wager-on-higher-oil-prices-to-drive-economic-diversification-1480624372"

Cyrille Urfer, who most recently worked for Swiss bank Gonet & Cie, will become a top manager for Saudi Arabia's Public Investment Fund, said a person familiar with the matter, and will take charge of many of the fund's investments outside of private equity.

A spokesman for the PIF didn't respond immediately to a request for comment. Mr. Urfer didn't respond to messages seeking comment.

The PIF is at the center of the Saudi government's 15-year plan to overhaul its economy with measures including cuts to subsidies for power and gasoline, imposing taxes on its populace, and investing in businesses domestically and abroad.

Over the next two years, the country plans to take "https://www.wsj.com/articles/saudi-aramco-ipo-wall-streets-white-whale-1465464606" in an offering that could value the company at $2 trillion, and to use some proceeds of that offering for the PIF to make non-oil investments.

Those include tens of billions of dollars headed for private equity for which the PIF hired fund manager Kevin O'Donnell, formerly of California-based Kaiser Permanente, last year along with stocks, bonds and other investments.

Last year the PIF invested "https://www.wsj.com/articles/uber-raises-3-5-billion-from-saudi-fund-1464816529"Uber Technologies Inc. and committed $45 billion to SoftBank Group Corp. f "https://www.wsj.com/articles/softbank-group-launches-investment-fund-1476398189".

Mr. Urfer, who is currently based in Geneva, worked in the 1990s at Credit Suisse, where, according to his LinkedIn page, he specialized in private banking and "achieved a major and unusually early promotion to VP." His LinkedIn page says he later became head of private asset management at Swiss bank Lombard Odier before joining the Abu Dhabi Investment Council, a fund that invests globally for the Abu Dhabi government, as chief investment officer for fixed-income and equity investments. He left that role in 2011, and joined Gonet & Cie a year later.

At PIF, Mr. Urfer will be responsible for the stock and bond portfolios, key building blocks of a fund that has huge ambition but little experience. Typically, investing in stocks and bonds is the core strategy of a new fund, said Sven Behrendt, the Geneva-based head of consultancy GeoEconomica, which researches sovereign-wealth funds. "Then, slowly, you move into alternatives" like private equity, he said.

Saudi Arabia has taken a different route. For years, the country did little international investing compared with neighbors such as the United Arab Emirates. But last year, newly empowered Deputy Crown Prince Mohammed bin Salman "https://www.wsj.com/articles/saudi-crown-prince-returns-to-public-eye-as-succession-looms-1479378840".

Saudi Arabia, he said, needed to remake its economy. He hired a flotilla of consultants from the U.S., Europe and elsewhere and last year announced plans to build domestic industries like tourism and tech and sell a small portion of the state-owned Saudi Arabian Oil Co., known as Saudi Aramco.

He said the PIF would become the world's largest sovereign-wealth fund, and he made big private-equity deals before he finished restructuring the fund.

Prince Mohammed appointed Yasir al Rumayyan, a former Saudi banker and financial regulator, to head the fund. Like other sovereign-wealth funds, the PIF may rely heavily on hiring foreign investment experts. Setting up the fund "is going to take international experts to help Saudis navigate their new investment vehicle," said Theodore Karasik, a senior adviser at Gulf State Analytics, which consults with companies on investing in the region.

White House Weighs Suspending  Conflict Minerals' Rule | Texas Judge Upholds Fiduciary Rule | Trump Picks: Giancarlo for CFTC Chair; Puzder Hearing Next Week | Ackerman's Take: Fight Over Energy-Payments Regulation Not Over; The Wall Street Journal's financial regulation newsletter for Thursday, February 9, 2017.
