Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

BNP Paribas has unveiled a new three-year strategy to invest  ¬3bn on adapting its operations to the digital age after outstripping analyst expectations with a 15 per cent jump in annual net profits.

By doubling its investment in digitisation, France s biggest bank aims to achieve  ¬3.4bn of cost savings, of which  ¬2.7bn will be recurring.

While many of Europe s biggest banks are still in the midst of painful restructurings, BNP Paribas has rebounded strongly, raising its dividend and boosting profits since being fined almost $9bn by US regulators in 2014. Its shares have risen 44 per cent over the past year.

But the bank s inability to rein in its cost base, which was inflated by  ¬1.3bn of unexpected regulatory costs, led to its failure to meet one of its previous six targets: to cut its operating costs to no more than two-thirds of revenue.

The French bank said on Tuesday it aimed to lower costs as a proportion of revenues from 66.8 to 63 per cent by 2020, helped by planned investments in automation, improved data usage and artificial intelligence.

Its net profits rose from  ¬7bn to  ¬7.7bn last year, as revenues outpaced costs, while provisions for bad loans fell and it booked fewer one-off charges. It raised its dividend from  ¬2.31 to  ¬2.70 per share, giving investors a yield of 4.6 per cent.

The bank s top performing division was its international financial services arm, which includes its consumer finance, insurance, asset management and US retail banking. This unit reported a 4 per cent rise in pre-tax profits.

The domestic retail unit, which has a network of branches in France, Belgium and Italy, was held back by low interest rates, but still managed a 1.4 per cent rise in pre-tax profits. The corporate and investment bank suffered a 1.2 per cent decline in pre-tax profits, but this was blamed on currency movements and one-off items.

Alongside a relatively ambitious 2.5 per cent annual revenue growth target for the next three years, the bank aims to raise its dividend payout ratio from 45 per cent to 50 per cent.

It also aims to lift its return on equity from 9.4 per cent last year to above 10 per cent by 2020, and to increase its common equity tier one ratio   a vital benchmark of financial strength   from 11.5 per cent to above 12 per cent.

Markets
Big Investors Cut Back on Commercial Property as Bull Market Loses Steam; Lofty prices, recent rise in interest rates among reasons for investors' caution

By Peter Grant 
1,322 words
7 February 2017
10:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Some prominent real-estate investors are reducing their holdings and getting more selective about new deals, in a sign that the eight-year bull market for U.S. commercial property is coming to a close. 

Asset managers at pension funds and endowments, as well as private-equity firms and other big investors, are throttling back on new acquisitions, selling more assets and shifting to less risky strategies as a way to protect against potential losses in a downturn. 

Additional selling could put stress on the market because demand for property has started to flag. Commercial real-estate deal volume decreased by $58.3 billion, or 11%, in 2016, the first annual decrease since 2009, according to data firm Real Capital Analytics, a sign that investor appetite is waning. 

Caution among investors in the $11 trillion U.S. commercial-property sector is being driven by lofty prices, the length of the market cycle so far and the recent rise in interest rates, which makes bonds look more attractive compared with commercial property. Also, developers are adding new supply of some property types at the fastest rate since the recovery began. 

Few investors predict a crash along the lines of the 2008 downturn because debt levels aren't nearly as high and the economy continues to show signs of strength. Some believe office buildings, malls, apartment buildings and other commercial property will continue to enjoy rising rents and occupancy rates if President Donald Trump's pro-growth economic plans work as intended. 

But certain property types are weakening. Under siege by online shopping, retail space saw "signs of a correction" last year with 30 metropolitan areas showing an increase in vacancy over 2015, according to Reis Inc., a data firm. 

In the office market, the largest commercial real estate sector in terms of value, tenants occupied an additional 38.5 million square feet in 2016, thanks to the expanding economy, Reis said. But that was down from 44.5 million square feet of "absorption" in 2015. 

Also office rent growth decelerated partly because "tenants continue to lease less square feet per employee than they had in previous recoveries," Reis said. 

Investors that have picked up the pace of selling to lock in profits include private-equity firm Blackstone Group LP, real-estate giant Brookfield Asset Management, United Parcel Service Inc.'s pension trust and Harvard Management Co., which manages Harvard University's endowment. 

When these big investors do buy, they are focusing more on niche properties such as self-storage warehouses and biomedical facilities, which haven't seen the sharp price rise of trophy office buildings and rental apartments. 

"We definitely have a risk-off mentality," said Judy McMahan, a portfolio manager for UPS's $32 billion pension trust. "We're being careful." 

The pension trust sold more property than it bought last year, and its new acquisitions included senior housing and industrial space in the U.K., Ms. McMahan said. 

Brookfield also increased the pace of its selling, unloading about $3 billion in property in 2016 compared with about half that much in 2015. The firm is using the capital to buy niche property types that haven't seen such sharp increases in pricing, like self-storage facilities and student housing in the U.K. 

The company recently put on the block a 49% stake in its sprawling Brookfield Place complex in Manhattan. The complex just finished overhauling its retail space and filling the 2.5 million square feet of office space emptied in 2013. 

"We think now is an opportune time to reduce some of our exposure to that asset," said Brian Kingston, Brookfield senior managing partner. "We can recycle the capital into higher returning investment opportunities." 

Since 2009, investors have been handsomely rewarded for purchases of office buildings, warehouses, apartment buildings and other commercial property. 

Thanks to low interest rates and the improving U.S. economy, a valuation index published by Green Street Advisors has increased 107% since hitting its crash-era low in May 2009. But that rocketing growth is slowing. The Green Street index, which focuses on top-quality U.S. commercial property owned by real-estate investment trusts, has stayed flat since mid-2016, according to Green Street. 

Another closely followed metric an index compiled by the National Council of Real Estate Investment Fiduciaries showed total returns from commercial real estate rising 9.2% in the year ended Sept. 30, 2016, a sharp decline from 13.5% for the 12 months ended in the third quarter of 2015 and growth ranging from 11% to 14% in each of the previous five years. 

Fund investors are pulling back as well. Quarterly distributions and redemptions from open-ended funds that buy low-risk properties, a popular investment vehicle among institutional investors, doubled during the first nine months of 2016, after ticking up just 11% in 2015, according to the council. 

Much of the bull market has been fueled by low interest rates, which encouraged investors to forsake bonds and stretch for more yield. But rates have jumped since Election Day. Real-estate investment trusts took the first hit, with equity REITs declining 2.9% in the fourth quarter of last year compared with a gain of 5.3% for the S&P 500, according to Green Street. 

This year through Monday, equity REITs have had a total return of 0.38%, compared with 1.9% for the S&P 500, Green Street said. 

Also, until recently, the rise in property values was fueled by developers keeping new supply in check. But that, too, is beginning to change with certain property types. For example, more than 378,000 new apartments are expected to be completed across the country this year, almost 35% more than the 20-year average, according to real-estate tracker Axiometrics Inc. 

Private investors say the real estate they are chasing these days often is either real estate that is less risky or properties that can be improved and sold quickly, rather than those like developments that might not be finished until the economy is well into the next down cycle. 

For example, private-equity giant KKR & Co. moved quickly to find a buyer last year after it purchased the landmark Sullivan Center in the Chicago Loop for $267 million. A few months after the deal closed, KKR sold the retail portion of the 946,000-square-foot building to Acadia Realty Trust. 

"Given we are in the later stage of the real-estate cycle, we have been focused on business plans that require less time to create value," said Chris Lee, co-head of real-estate credit at KKR. 

Blackstone sold more property than it bought last year, according to Kenneth Caplan, the firm's chief investment officer for real estate. Sales have included more traditional property types such as apartment buildings and hotels. One of its biggest buys last year was BioMed Realty Trust Inc., which leases offices to the life-science industry. "It's later in the cycle where you have to be more targeted," Mr. Caplan said. 

Harvard's endowment is among the big institutions that sold more property last year than it purchased, according to people familiar with the matter. A spokeswoman for the endowment declined to comment. 

Institutions that sell property acknowledge values could keep rising, but said they want to play it safe. 

"Some of the investments we disposed of, if we held on to them another year or so, it's possible we'd make more money," said Ms. McMahan of UPS. "However, we felt it was the appropriate time to monetize our gains."

Citigroup Links With Tech Giants to Put Fintech to Work for Governments; Initiative link bank's public-sector group with tech firms like Facebook Inc, Microsoft and IBM

By Telis Demos 
550 words
7 February 2017
12:12
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Citigroup Inc. this week is launching a joint effort with software giants and startups to find ways to use new financial technologies to combat fraud and bribery in government services.

The initiative, called Citi Tech For Integrity Challenge, will partner Citigroup's public-sector group with tech companies including Facebook Inc., International Business Machines Corp., and Microsoft Corp., and startups globally, the bank said. The aim is to help programmers at tech firms to develop new applications using Citigroup's and the partners' software for governments to do things like detect and block illicit payments, protect financial data, or help deliver aid to poor or crisis-hit regions.

The initiative is the latest example of broadening "fintech," which has so far focused largely on new tools for consumers, like online loans or mobile payments. Banks also generate billions in revenue from corporations and governments, so finding ways to apply technology to those businesses would justify big investments.

That includes finding ways to apply technologies such as mobile payments apps, photo-recognition software, blockchain the networking system originally developed for bitcoin, but which banks have spent hundreds of millions trying to "https://www.wsj.com/articles/wall-street-backs-blockchain-for-savings-on-derivatives-1483979438" and artificial intelligence to government payments systems. These systems often use technology to manage the government's payment process, but ultimately then deliver funds in cash that isn't trackable.

"Officials elected on anti-corruption platforms told us they don't have the tools to change what's happening further down" the payments system, said Julie Monaco, global head of Citigroup's public-sector group in the bank's corporate and investment banking unit, noting that about 70% of fintech spending currently goes toward consumer usage. "We want them to talk to the fintech team and figure out how to plug in."

Citigroup may invest in some of the new initiative's applications, which could be sold to government clients, via its venture group, or partner to use them for its own services. But the bank says it is also hoping to spur investments by other partners and independent development for activities like communicating with citizens and reforming bureaucracies.

In a presentation on the initiative, Citigroup cited a Global Financial Integrity study that African countries in 2011 lost $77 billion due to illicit financial flows.

Governments including the U.S. are among Citigroup's customers in its business selling services to help move large volumes of payments and other money transfers globally, known as Treasury and Trade Solutions.

Citigroup doesn't disclose figures for government-client revenue. Public-sector transaction services revenue grew "double digits" last year, thanks in part to governments adding new technologies to their payments systems, the bank said. Overall Treasury and Trade Solutions revenue was $8.1 billion in 2016, up 4% from the prior year.

Banks are using technology to remake their businesses in the wake of post-financial crisis rules restraining risk-taking. Some firms like Goldman Sachs Group Inc. are seeking "https://www.wsj.com/articles/goldman-sachs-has-a-new-model-apple-1477863314", by opening parts of their systems for outside firms to build financial products.

Markets
How One Man Runs His ETF the Way He Played Blackjack; Blair Hull's ETF treats the S&P 500 the way Mr. Hull used to play cards but he concedes that market timing shouldn't be overdone

By Chuck Jaffe 
904 words
7 February 2017
22:55
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Blair Hull believes that investors owe it to themselves to do some market timing if they do it analytically, not emotionally, and don't do too much of it.

Mr. Hull is the founder of Hull Tactical Asset Allocation in Chicago, which runs the $95 million Hull Tactical US ETF (HTUS). He made a living at blackjack, spending the early 1970s as a card counter (the art of expertly tracking the cards that have been dealt).

As a trader, Mr. Hull always wanted to take an approach similar to card counting basing his moves more on a system than a hunch. So it's no surprise that Hull Tactical Asset Allocation plays the S&P 500 index the way Mr. Hull played blackjack, using an array of data to time each market move.

He spoke with The Wall Street Journal about why market timing makes more sense in the 21st century than it did in the past, and how it should fit into an investor's portfolio. Here are edited excerpts from that interview.

WSJ: You say there are similarities between market timing and counting cards. What are they, and how do they give the investor an edge?

MR. HULL: You have 10 different indicators in a deck of cards: You have 2 through 9, and you have 10s [including face cards] and aces. That's a total of 10 numbers.

And we have 10 indicators or something like that, it can be a few more or less depending on the model for our trading programs. How do we count them? We do it with science, looking at the various indicators, which show when there is a time to be in or out of the market.

WSJ: How big of an edge can you create?

MR. HULL: I played blackjack for five years from 1971-75, getting an advantage of, at best, 51-49 [wins to losses]. If we can get a slight advantage in the market, say 55-45, and we sustain that over a long period of time, we will have the odds in our favor and will actually gain more wealth than if we just went buy-and-hold. I think that's possible.

WSJ: Your system is all about removing emotion from the market, yet one indicator you rely on in some models is Twitter. Isn't that injecting emotion?

MR. HULL: We're not making emotional decisions about Twitter, or any of the other indicators used in any of our models.

We read the Twitter feed, and we look at the sentiment that occurs on Twitter, people tweeting about the S&P 500 and whether they are bullish or bearish. If they are bullish for a certain amount of time, it's time to sell the market; if they're bearish for a certain amount of time, it's a signal to buy.

If the sentiment is sustained long enough, it becomes meaningful and we factor it in with the other indicators; there's nothing emotional about that.

WSJ: Why have you changed your mind about buy-and-hold investing?

MR. HULL: I haven't. It's still right for most people and most of their money. Timing is very hard.

Jack Bogle [founder of Vanguard Group] was correct when he said that, in the last century, buy-and-hold was the best strategy, but since 2000 we have had this explosion of data, and along with that explosion has come predictive analytics and machine-learning techniques that we really didn't have before.

Right now, you have very few academic papers saying you can't time the market. Right now, they all say it's possible, and they have a vast number of ways people are trying to do it.

A lot of those approaches aren't going to work they are too susceptible to fear and greed and are run by emotions but I think it's possible to have a systematic approach that gives you better returns for less risk, adding something to your portfolio.

WSJ: Despite that belief, you don't believe in putting most of your money at risk through market timing, do you?

MR. HULL: The strategy fits in the alternative-investment area essentially, it's hedge-fund performance at retail prices but alternative strategies should be 5% or 10% of the typical investor's portfolio. That doesn't change just because there's a market-timing approach that you believe in; people can do both, they can buy-and-hold and time the market side by side in the same portfolio.

Not having all of your eggs in one basket certainly makes a lot of sense, no matter which approach you believe in.

Mr. Jaffe is a writer in Boston. He can be reached at "mailto:reports@wsj.com".

With President Donald Trump enacting hiring freezes across the federal government, private equity-backed information-technology services provider Salient CRGT Inc. plans to help government agencies fill the gaps through its agreement to acquire Information Innovators Inc.

Information Innovators, of Springfield, Va., provides technology products for health care-focused federal agencies.

The transaction was valued at $275 million, said people familiar with the matter. The acquisition is slated to close during the first quarter of this year, according to a news release.

DFW Capital Partners, the Teaneck, N.J., private-equity firm which has backed Information Innovators since 2012, will exit the company through the deal.

Bridge Growth Partners and Frontenac Co.-backed Salient CRGT was formed by the "https://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFLBO0120150810eb8askdp8"of CRGT Inc., which Bridge Growth purchased in 2014, and Salient Federal Solutions Inc., which was formed with backing from Frontenac in 2009. The transaction for Information Innovators will be the combined entity's first acquisition since its merger.

Salient CRGT provides agile software development, cybersecurity, data analytics and other IT services to government agencies such as the Defense Technology Security Administration, U.S. Postal Service, U.S. Air Force and U.S. Social Security Administration.

Alok Singh, a co-founder and managing principal at Bridge Growth, said Salient GRGT was attracted to Information Innovators because of its desire to expand the company's foothold in the health-care space. Mr. Singh added that with the recent governmental hiring freeze, it is likely more work will be driven to contractors, making outsourced IT providers more relevant.

"We think the sector will continue to do very well over the next several years," Mr. Singh said. "Especially with all the houses [of Congress] and the president under the same party, budget processes should be much smoother."

The newly acquired company holds contracts with a number of government agencies, including the Department of Homeland Security, the Department of Health and Human Services and the Department of Veterans Affairs, among others, according to the release.

Mr. Singh said Salient CRGT's revenue is expected to grow to more than $500 million after the Information Innovators acquisition closes, compared with $150 million when Bridge Growth first backed the company.

New York-based Bridge Growth backs companies in the technology and technology-enabled financial services sectors.

Frontenac, of Chicago, targets transactions in the food, services and industrial sectors.

DFW Partners invests in companies with less than $10 million in earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization within the health-care services, industrial services and business services sectors, according to the firm's website.

Pro Private Markets
Brainy Investors Put AI in Its Place

444 words
6 February 2017
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Brainy Investors Put AI in Its Place

Neural networks. Deep learning. The terms we use when talking about machine intelligence are inextricably tied to human intelligence.

The past year brought a steady drumbeat of technology companies acquiring artificial-intelligence startups while venture capital poured into the sector. From autonomous vehicles to voice assistants, much of today's conversation surrounding machine intelligence centers on how computing power could replace human brain power.

As startups use analogies about the human mind to explain leaps they have made in artificially intelligent models, scientists are realizing they still have much to learn about how the human brain works. That issue came into focus at a Bloomberg Beta San Francisco gathering last week that included experts from the University of Chicago and University of California, Berkeley, as they explored the link between neuroscience and machine intelligence.

It was an important reminder that artificial intelligence today works best in applied use cases. Despite the way some talk about the promise of artificial intelligence, machines can't actually operate like a human mind. They do perform some tasks much more effectively than people can, when dealing with hefty data sets and calculations. But they can't replicate certain other cases, such as tasks requiring creativity.

Computers are as far from replicating the exact processes of our brains as scientists are from understanding exactly how those processes occur.

The winners in the machine-intelligence boom will be companies that apply advanced models to specific problems where vast data sets are available. Artificial general intelligence remains a concept that is only realized in the pages of science fiction.

Shivon Zilis, a Bloomberg Beta partner who invests in artificial intelligence, says AI-based startups can help humans make better decisions, rather than make decisions for them.

"Machine intelligence wins on speed, memory, and large scale pattern recognition," she said. "Human intelligence wins on contextualization, creativity, and decision making."

Ms. Zilis said the companies her firm has invested in, like Mavrx and ShieldAI, are working on producing "better inputs" that can be more "consumable" for human decision makers.

"I honestly view a lot of these technologies as ways to extend my mind," she said. "At the end of the day, I'm still not letting any of these systems make critical decisions on their own, but they do help me make better decisions."

Investing in Funds & ETFs: A Monthly Analysis --- Q&A: He Shuffles the S&P Like Cards --- A former blackjack player runs an ETF that times the market

By Chuck Jaffe 
783 words
6 February 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
R7
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Blair Hull believes that investors owe it to themselves to do some market timing -- if they do it analytically, not emotionally, and don't do too much of it.

Mr. Hull is founder of Hull Tactical Asset Allocation in Chicago, which runs the $95 million Hull Tactical US ETF (HTUS). He made a living at blackjack, spending the early 1970s as a card counter (the art of expertly tracking the cards that have been dealt).

As a trader, Mr. Hull always wanted to take a similar approach -- basing his moves more on a system than a hunch. His fund plays the S&P 500 index the way Mr. Hull played blackjack, using data to time each move.

He spoke with The Wall Street Journal about why market timing makes more sense in the 21st century than before, and how it should fit into an investor's portfolio.

WSJ: You say there are similarities between market timing and counting cards. How do they give the investor an edge?

MR. HULL: You have 10 different indicators in a deck of cards: You have 2 through 9, and you have 10s [including face cards] and aces. That's a total of 10 numbers.

And we have 10 indicators -- or something like that, it can be a few more or less depending on the model -- for our trading programs. How do we count them? We do it with science, looking at the various indicators, which show when there is a time to be in or out of the market.

WSJ: How big of an edge can you create?

MR. HULL: I played blackjack for five years from 1971-75, getting an advantage of, at best, 51-49 [wins to losses]. If we can get a slight advantage in the market, say 55-45, and we sustain that over a long period, we will have the odds in our favor and will actually gain more wealth than if we just went buy-and-hold. I think that's possible.

WSJ: Your system is all about removing emotion from the market, yet one indicator you rely on in some models is Twitter. Isn't that injecting emotion?

MR. HULL: We're not making emotional decisions about Twitter, or any of the other indicators used in any of our models. We read the Twitter feed, and we look at the sentiment that occurs on Twitter, people tweeting about the S&P 500 and whether they are bullish or bearish. If they are bullish for a certain amount of time, it's time to sell the market; if they're bearish for a certain amount of time, it's a signal to buy.

If the sentiment is sustained long enough, it becomes meaningful and we factor it in with the other indicators; there's nothing emotional about that.

WSJ: Why have you changed your mind about buy-and-hold investing?

MR. HULL: I haven't. It's still right for most people and most of their money. Timing is very hard. Jack Bogle [founder of Vanguard Group] was correct when he said that, in the last century, buy-and-hold was the best strategy, but since 2000 we have had this explosion of data, and along with that explosion has come predictive analytics and machine-learning techniques that we really didn't have before. Right now, you have very few academic papers saying you can't time the market. Right now, they all say it's possible, and they have a vast number of ways people are trying to do it.

A lot of those approaches aren't going to work -- they are too susceptible to fear and greed and are run by emotions -- but I think it's possible to have a systematic approach that gives you better returns for less risk.

WSJ: Despite that belief, you don't believe in putting most of your money at risk through market timing, do you?

MR. HULL: The strategy fits in the alternative-investment area -- essentially, it's hedge-fund performance at retail prices -- but alternative strategies should be 5% or 10% of the typical investor's portfolio. People can do both, they can buy-and-hold and time the market. Not having all of your eggs in one basket certainly makes a lot of sense, no matter which approach you believe in.

---

Mr. Jaffe is a writer in Boston. He can be reached at reports@wsj.com.

FT WEEKEND SUPPLEMENT - MONEY
Robot apps aim to help you save more; SAVINGS

By Aime Williams 
699 words
4 February 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
03
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Digital developments put security concerns in the spotlight

The promise of hyper-smart robot traders has long attracted fund managers to artificial intelligence - but a flurry of new apps is now bringing AI to savers.

Plum, launched in January, and Chip, which began operations late last year, offer ordinary savers the ability to have their savings managed by an algorithm.

The apps require access to users' bank statements, which they analyse to identify how much they could afford to put away each month. The algorithm attempts to learn users' spending patterns, including when they pay regular bills and when they might be more likely to splurge on dinner and drinks. If the account holder spends less than usual, the app siphons off small amounts of money from their usual current account into a savings account - in both cases hosted by Barclays.

Simon Rabin, co-founder of Chip, says the idea for his app came about after he and co-founder Alex Latham realised that recent graduates were not saving. A recent graduate himself, Mr Latham found himself "perpetually in his overdraft and having bought a holiday on a credit card", while paying 850 a month to rent a flat in Balham, south London.

Chip's founders designed it to serve a generation that does not budget in the way its parents did. "Our parents planned more and had more rigid expenses," said Mr Rabin.

"At the beginning of the month it was about the mortgage and there was an allocated amount for food and costs for the kids," he said. "Nowadays it's more fluid. I'm not doing a monthly shop, I'm popping into Tesco Express on the way home."

Mr Rabin said millennials' spending is "more erratic and fluid". "For us to save effectively, we need to be able to save the same way," he added.

Plum, a rival app, operates through Facebook Messenger. This means anyone wanting to use the app must first download the messaging app to their phone before communicating with Plum through one of Facebook's "bots" - human-like characters that can converse with users.

Plum's founder, Victor Trokoudes, said the app's goal was to save money "in the most painless possible way".

"Rather than pulling money out once a month we try to save regularly between five to seven times a month, the whole idea being you're not feeling the fact that money came out as much as if it had all come out in one go," he said. "It's small, negligible."

The apps echo a similar app, Digit, currently only available to US investors and backed by a series of venture capital funds, including GV (formerly known as Google Ventures).

Warren Mead, global head of financial technology for consultancy KPMG, said using artificial intelligence for saving and investing was "definitely the direction of travel". "I would expect to see more and more developments like this in retail savings," he said.

Andrew Power, partner at Deloitte, agreed that while AI is already used by fund managers looking to generate investment strategies, it will quickly "roll out more broadly" to general savers and investors.

"AI is good at trend and pattern recognition, so one application is to monitor spending and how much you can save."

Mr Power suggested that AI could go further than analysing people's bank statements and in future could be used to help investors make better investment decisions.

"There's a wealth of research on mistakes people make around loss aversion and things like that," said Mr Power. "Because AI can process data quickly it can highlight to people that actions they're taking are not in their best interests."

Banks are under increasing pressure to share data with digital services as well as with comparison websites which could alert users to better deals. However, senior bankers have argued that digital developments could put customer data at risk of being stolen. Some 85 per cent of 300 bank managers surveyed by consultancy Accenture said that new digital products and services are putting data-handling concerns in the spotlight.

Banking & Finance: New Nasdaq CEO Leads a Reboot

By Alexander Osipovich 
906 words
4 February 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

When Nasdaq Inc. named Adena Friedman as its next chief executive in November, she won attention for being young, tech-savvy and the first woman to lead a major U.S. stock-exchange operator. Now, the hard part has begun.

Ms. Friedman, a 47-year-old black belt in taekwondo, has inherited a company whose traditional core business of U.S. stock trading has gotten much tougher since the heady 1990s when Nasdaq touted itself as "the stock market for the next 100 years."

Regulations in the late 1990s and the last decade brought competition to exchanges, opening the door to high-tech upstarts like Bats Global Markets Inc., which surpassed Nasdaq as the second-biggest U.S. exchange group by volume in 2014, and dozens of dark pools, or private trading venues. Two major market crashes in the last decade soured many investors on stocks altogether.

Analysts link a recent slide in Nasdaq's share of U.S. stock volume to a broad shift in trading toward securities listed on the New York Stock Exchange and its sister exchanges, including NYSE Arca, which specializes in exchange-traded funds.

Adding to Ms. Friedman's challenges, Nasdaq faces fierce competition from its historic rival NYSE for listings, even in the realm of hot tech stocks in which Nasdaq was once king. This week, the Big Board won the coveted initial public offering of Snap Inc., owner of the popular messaging app Snapchat.

To keep Nasdaq expanding, Ms. Friedman plans to keep pursuing a strategy, initiated under her predecessor, Robert Greifeld, of diversifying away from the stocks business into financial technology.

Ms. Friedman is perhaps most excited about the potential of artificial intelligence. Nasdaq uses AI in systems designed to spot wrongdoing at banks and tools that seek to enhance hedge funds' stock-trading strategies. Such efforts account for a tiny slice of Nasdaq's sales today, but Ms. Friedman sees huge potential in AI.

"We think in the next decade it could really transform the business," said Ms. Friedman, who became CEO on Jan. 1.

Nasdaq is pursuing initiatives to draw more trading back to its stock exchanges, which Ms. Friedman describes as a "long game." The company is also building its ETF business. More than 50 exchange-traded products switched from NYSE to Nasdaq last year, even though NYSE Arca remains the dominant player.

Ms. Friedman grew up steeped in finance. Her father, David Testa, was a longtime employee of T. Rowe Price Group Inc. who rose to the position of chief investment officer before retiring. As a child, Ms. Friedman recalls visiting the T. Rowe trading floor in Baltimore and doodling on the whiteboards with a marker. Both she and her brother -- now a hedge-fund manager -- took up careers in financial services.

"It was a big part of what attracted me to the industry," Ms. Friedman said.

Ms. Friedman joined Nasdaq after finishing business school at Vanderbilt University. From 1993 to 2011, she held job titles including head of data products, head of strategy and chief financial officer, and sometimes played second base on a company softball team.

She was a key player in the 2005 acquisition of Instinet Group Inc.'s computerized trading division, a deal aimed at shoring up Nasdaq's trading technology. While Mr. Greifeld shaped the vision for the deal, Ms. Friedman was one of his top lieutenants in implementing it and worked many late nights to make it happen, recalled John Jacobs, a former Nasdaq executive.

Despite the pressure on Ms. Friedman, "I never saw her lose her cool," said Mr. Jacobs, who is now a fellow at Georgetown University's business school.

In 2011, Ms. Friedman left Nasdaq to become CFO of private-equity powerhouse Carlyle Group LP. The move surprised many, not least because she was seen as a potential successor to Mr. Greifeld.

Ms. Friedman said she left because Carlyle offered her an "amazing opportunity" and she wanted to spend more time with her husband and two sons in Washington, D.C., where the buyout firm is based, instead of commuting to New York. She spent three years at Carlyle before returning to Nasdaq as president in 2014.

Former colleagues describe Ms. Friedman as hardworking, intelligent and a gifted communicator. When she joined Carlyle, the firm was moving toward becoming a public company. This pushed Ms. Friedman, as CFO, into the crucial role of explaining Carlyle's complicated financials to prospective investors.

Her performance during the roadshow before Carlyle's 2012 IPO impressed David Rubenstein, co-founder and co-CEO of the firm. "She's a star," Mr. Rubenstein said in an interview. "She knew the numbers inside out and upside down."

So far, Ms. Friedman's early moves as CEO at Nasdaq -- including a decision to exit its struggling European interest-rate futures business, announced Tuesday -- have been welcomed by Wall Street analysts.

Analysts say Ms. Friedman is well positioned to oversee Nasdaq's tech-centric strategy, citing her previous leadership of the firm's data and technology businesses. "She's been very involved in some of the newer, cutting-edge initiatives," said Richard Repetto, an analyst at Sandler O'Neill + Partners LP.

Ms. Friedman is betting that the tech focus will pay off. "We are more than an exchange company today," she said.

Banking & Finance: Finance Watch

473 words
3 February 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
B10
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

CME GROUP

Energy, Metals

Lift Revenue, Net

CME Group Inc. said surging energy and metal markets helped propel quarterly revenue and income.

The Chicago-based futures market, the world's biggest, reported a profit of $373.4 million, or $1.10 a share, compared with $291.7 million, or 86 cents, a year earlier. Excluding certain items, per-share profit rose to $1.14 from 97 cents. Revenue improved 12% to $912.9 million. Shares rose 0.3% to $121.26.

CME said average daily volume rose 24% to 16.3 million contracts. Clearing and transaction fees rose to $768.5 million from $678 million. Energy and metals trading jumped, with volumes rising 27% and 49%, respectively.

-- Ezequiel Minaya

---

REAL ESTATE

Germany Bests U.K.

On Europe Property

Germany has unseated the U.K. as the most active commercial-real-estate market in Europe, reflecting greater caution toward Britain after it voted to leave the European Union.

Britain had held the top spot for more than a decade.

Nearly 59 billion euros ($63.5 billion) of commercial property traded hands in Germany last year, compared with about 57 billion euros in the U.K., says data tracker Real Capital Analytics.

The Brexit vote in June caused a level of caution among investors who were already wary of high U.K. prices after a multiyear property boom. U.K. deal volume fell 43% in 2016 from a year earlier, according to data from Real Capital.

Transaction volumes also fell in Germany and across Europe overall, albeit less than in the U.K. In Germany, the biggest economy in the region, deal volume fell 19% last year, Real Capital said. European volumes dropped 21% on average. Investors and analysts say a lack of property for sale, rather than shrinking demand, caused the broad fall in deal volumes. Italy, Sweden, the Netherlands and Finland all had record levels of property investment in 2016.

-- Art Patnaude

---

VISA INC.

Deal for Overseas

Unit Drives Results

Visa Inc.'s profit rose 6.7% in the latest quarter, as the card company's acquisition of Visa Europe drove higher payments volume and processed transactions.

Results beat Wall Street estimates, and shares, which have grown by 12% over the past 12 months, added 2.8% to $85.13 after hours.

For the December quarter, Visa earned $2.07 billion, or 86 cents a share, up from $1.94 billion, or 80 cents a share, a year earlier.

Operating revenue surged 25% to $4.46 billion, lifted by the inclusion of Europe and continued growth in processed transactions and nominal payments volume.

Payments volume for the quarter rose 39% on a constant-dollar basis to $1.8 trillion. Total processed transactions climbed 44% to 27.3 billion.

-- Anne Steele

Uber's Asian Competitors Beef Up

By Resty Woro Yuniar and Liza Lin 
977 words
3 February 2017
The Wall Street Journal (Asia Edition)
AWSJ
B1
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

In the race for customers in Asia's fast-growing ride-hailing market, Uber Technologies Inc. is facing a new challenge: local rivals that have expanded their offerings to include mobile-payment tools and other on-demand services.

After exiting from China following a costly battle for passengers last year, the world's most valuable startup has doubled down on its expansion in India and Southeast Asia, expanding its array of services to include carpooling and motorcycle hailing.

Uber's competitors, however, are upping the ante, hoping to create new revenue streams by converting their tech-friendly customers into users of added offerings such as mobile wallets.

Ola, owned by ANI Technologies Pvt., allows customers to pay for meals and utility bills using money loaded on its mobile-wallet app, Ola Money.

Users of Go-Jek, a ride-hailing app valued at $1 billion and backed by private-equity firms KKR & Co. and Warburg Pincus LLC, can request other on-demand services such as manicures and home cleaning through the app. PT Go-Jek Indonesia, which launched its mobile app in January 2015, now offers 15 services beyond ride-hailing including its mobile wallet, Go-Pay. The Jakarta-based company plans to introduce five more offerings this year, but declined to provide specifics.

Southeast Asia is a potentially lucrative battleground with its ride-hailing market forecast to grow 18% annually to $13.1 billion by 2025 from $2.5 billion in 2015, according to a report by Alphabet Inc.'s Google and Singapore state investment firm Temasek Holdings Pvt. Ltd.

By contrast, growth in the U.S. market is already slowing. Research firm eMarketer predicts that the number of people who use a ride-hailing service in the U.S. will rise 7.2% in 2018, compared with an estimated 13% gain this year.

While expanding in Southeast Asia and India, Uber has traditionally mimicked steps taken by local rivals, introducing services such as motorcycle hailing after competitors began offering them. Low credit-card use in the region drove Uber to enable cash payments for its rides in India starting in May 2015 and in Southeast Asia three months later, said Andrew MacDonald, regional manager of Uber's Asia-Pacific and Latin America operations.

Uber set up a foreign investment company in Indonesia and bolstered its offerings by adding a two-wheeler hailing service called UberMoto and chauffeur-driven rental cars in Bali.

Mr. MacDonald said the company has no plans to launch a mobile-wallet service, preferring to team up with existing mobile-payment service providers such as One97 Communications Ltd.'s Paytm in India. The U.S. company operates UberEats, a prepared-food delivery service, in about 60 cities globally, including six in Asia. It also offers package-delivery services in the U.S.

Go-Jek user Novisia, a 34-year-old supervisor at a clothing store in Jakarta who like many Indonesians goes by one name, is embracing the company's expanded app, having initially downloaded it to hail a two-wheeler. She now uses Go-Jek's food- and package-delivery services.

"We've noticed that when a user uses more than one product, the retention numbers jump exponentially," said Go-Jek Chief Executive Nadiem Makarim. The company tripled its transactions to 35 million in December from a year earlier, he said.

Singapore-based ride-hailing startup GrabTaxi Holdings Pte. Ltd., valued at $3 billion and co-founded by Malaysian Anthony Tan, has introduced more ways for consumers to load money on its GrabPay mobile-wallet app, including at convenience stores. The mobile wallet can be used to pay for Grab services in Singapore and Indonesia, and will soon be launched in the Philippines, Thailand, Malaysia and Vietnam, tapping mall operators as partners.

"That's going to be our big, big push," Mr. Tan said.

On Thursday, Grab said that it planned to invest $700 million in Indonesia over the next four years. The money will be used for a research center in Jakarta and a $100 million investment fund to put money into local financial-technology companies.

In India, Ola, which is backed by SoftBank Group Corp. of Japan, is expanding Ola Money, which can be used at over 500 brick-and-mortar and online merchants. The Bangalore-based company also offers its riders e-commerce and video-on-demand services, as well free in-car Wi-Fi.

It isn't clear whether the companies generate any profits from these additional services. Ola, Grab and Go-Jek don't disclose numbers of daily active users, transaction numbers or the amount of revenue they earn.

In terms of regional downloads, Grab and Go-Jek are ahead of Uber, according to San Francisco-based mobile-apps analytics firm Sensor Tower. In the fourth quarter, Grab's app was downloaded 6.7 million times in Southeast Asia's six largest markets where it competes with Uber, compared with the U.S. company's 5.3 million downloads. In Indonesia, Go-Jek had 147,000 more downloads than Uber in the same period.

India was a sweet spot for Uber, however, registering 8.8 million downloads, more than Ola's 5.3 million, according to Sensor Tower.

Diversifying into new services will require hefty investments in recruitment and training with no guarantee that these businesses will ever turn a profit, said Rushabh Doshi, a Singapore-based analyst at Canalys.

But for Fitrie Ratnasari, 26, who is an account manager in Jakarta and likes to try different apps, the new services, promotions and discounts have helped her choose her preferred ride-hailing app.

"Go-Jek first, Grab second, then Uber, in that order," Ms. Ratnasari said.

---

Greg Bensinger in San Francisco contributed to this article.

Tech
Uber's Asian Rivals Race Ahead With Services Like Manicures, Home Cleaning; Go-Jek, Ola, Grab offer mobile-payment tools, other on-demand options in bid for new revenue streams

By Resty Woro Yuniar in Jakarta and Liza Lin in Singapore

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/china-s-didi-chuxing-to-acquire-rival-uber-s-chinese-operations-1470024403" following a costly battle for passengers last year, the world's most valuable startup "http://www.wsj.com/articles/ubers-drive-into-india-relies-on-raw-recruits-1482402603" in India and Southeast Asia, expanding its array of services to include carpooling and motorcycle hailing.

"We've noticed that when a user uses more than one product, the retention numbers jump exponentially," said Go-Jek Chief Executive Nadiem Makarim. The company tripled its transactions to 35 million in December from a year earlier, he said.

Singapore-based ride-hailing startup GrabTaxi Holdings Pte. Ltd., valued at $3 billion and "http://www.wsj.com/articles/uber-rival-grab-seizes-lead-in-southeast-asia-1467620502", has introduced more ways for consumers to load money on its GrabPay mobile-wallet app, including at convenience stores. The mobile wallet can be used to pay for Grab services in Singapore and Indonesia, and will soon be launched in the Philippines, Thailand, Malaysia and Vietnam, tapping mall operators as partners.

"That's going to be our big, big push," Mr. Tan said.

On Thursday, Grab said that it planned to invest $700 million in Indonesia over the next four years. The money will be used for a research center in Jakarta and a $100 million investment fund to put money into local financial-technology companies. It will also spend on broadening the use of GrabPay.

In India, Ola, which is backed by SoftBank Group Corp. of Japan, "http://www.wsj.com/articles/ola-valued-at-5-billion-in-latest-fundraising-round-1442291034", which currently can be used at over 500 brick-and-mortar and online merchants. The Bangalore-based company also offers its riders e-commerce and video-on-demand services, as well free in-car Wi-Fi.

It isn't clear whether the companies generate any profits from these additional services. Ola, Grab and Go-Jek don't disclose numbers of daily active users, transaction numbers or the amount of revenue they earn.

In terms of regional downloads, Grab and Go-Jek are ahead of Uber, according to data from San Francisco-based mobile-apps analytics firm Sensor Tower. In the fourth quarter, Grab's app was downloaded 6.7 million times in Southeast Asia's six largest markets where it competes with Uber, compared with the U.S. company's 5.3 million downloads. In Indonesia, Go-Jek had 147,000 more downloads than Uber in the same period.

India was a sweet spot for Uber, however, registering 8.8 million downloads, more than Ola's 5.3 million, according to Sensor Tower.

Diversifying into new services will require hefty investments in recruitment and training with no guarantee that these businesses will ever turn a profit, said Rushabh Doshi, a Singapore-based analyst at Canalys.

But for Fitrie Ratnasari, 26, who is an account manager in Jakarta and likes to try different apps, the new services, promotions and discounts have helped her choose her preferred ride-hailing app.

"Go-Jek first, Grab second, then Uber, in that order," Ms. Ratnasari said.

Greg Bensinger in San Francisco contributed to this article.
