Rubin's Take: Senate Dysfunction Bad Omen for Deregulatory Push

Fintech Startups Want to Save One Key Page of Dodd-Frank

Scaramucci's White House Role Now Uncertain

Credit Card Fraud Keeps Rising, Despite New Security Chips Study

Bundesbank's Dombret: No Deal on Basel III Better Than Bad Deal

Senate Dysfunction a Bad Omen for Deregulatory Push

Comity in the Senate has fizzled out, the latest casualty of the extraordinarily volatile political climate. And that could spell trouble for any major Republican legislative agenda, including comprehensive changes to financial regulation.

Responding to anger in the streets and concerns over President Donald Trump's cabinet choices, Democrats decided to boycott two consecutive committee votes on the nominees to lead the Treasury Department and the Health and Human Services Department Steven Mnuchin and Rep. Tom Price, respectively. According to the rules of the Senate Finance Committee, at least one Democrat must be present for such a vote to take place. But on Wednesday morning, the second day of the boycott, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/mnuchin-price-nominations-advance-with-no-democrats-present-1485963439" and send the nominations directly to the full Senate.

Lawmakers on both sides decried their counterparts' actions as unprecedented.

"The Senate Finance Committee has traditionally been able to function in even the most divisive political environments," Chairman Orrin Hatch of Utah said after the committee vote. "That all changed yesterday."

Sen. Ron Wyden of Oregon, the committee's top Democrat, criticized Republicans for flouting the panel's rules and said his party was simply demanding answers to their questions about the two nominees.

Senate Republicans don't need Democratic votes to approve Mr. Trump's cabinet, since Democrats abolished the filibuster for cabinet nominees back in 2013. But they do need 60 votes to avoid Democratic filibusters of legislation.

That means the GOP will need at least eight Democrats to vote for any sweeping changes to financial regulation, a key goal for both congressional Republicans and Mr. Mnuchin. Without the agreement of Senate Democrats, Republicans will have to use the budget process to make changes to the regulatory regime but their room to maneuver would be badly crimped.

Under budget reconciliation rules, only a simple majority in the Senate is required to make changes to regulations that are "scored" by the Congressional Budget Office  meaning they have an impact on federal funds. But most regulations, while they may have costs for private entities, don't require congressional appropriations. For example, no federal funds are needed to maintain bank capital requirements or the existing structure of the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau , both of which Republicans would like to change. Any big changes to those regulations or agencies would require Republicans to go through normal legislative channels and that means they would have to surpass the filibuster threshold of 60 votes in the Senate.

Even Democrats up for re-election in states that Mr. Trump won may find it not politically advantageous to join Republicans on anything other than small, noncontroversial legislation, given the "https://www.wsj.com/articles/donald-trump-democrats-dig-in-for-fight-1485909946" and Senate leaders toward cooperating with the Trump administration.

On Tuesday, Republicans on the Finance Committee took turns criticizing their Democratic colleagues for their boycott. But Sen. Mike Crapo of Idaho, who is also the chairman of the Banking Committee, refrained from making a fiery statement, instead expressing mild agreement with fellow Republicans. The affable senior lawmaker may have been attempting to preserve whatever chance at bipartisan cooperation remains otherwise, he and his colleagues are in for a long and frustrating 115th Congress.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

WSJ Pro First: Fintech Startups Want to Save One Key Page of Dodd-Frank

By Telis Demos

President Donald Trump said this week that his administration is going to do a "big number" on the Dodd-Frank financial overhaul and dismantle it. A brewing fight between financial startups and banks over one small section of the law shows how rancorous, and complex, this process could prove.

The Dodd-Frank Act, which was signed into law in July 2010, is 849 pages long and has numerous provisions that have spurred hundreds of rules covering everything from bank regulation to debit- and credit-card fees to derivatives trading. Each of those issues often has passionate supporters and detractors; changes likely will set off renewed lobbying battles, just as happened when the law was initially crafted.

Consider Section 1033. While much attention around Dodd-Frank has focused on its proprietary trading provisions or the consumer-protection agency it created, this one-page, 342 word provision has garnered the attention of startup venture-backed companies.

Although there is no indication so far that the administration is focused on this little-known section, some are gearing up to fight to protect it. Section 1033 says that banks must "make available to a consumer, upon request &information relating to any transaction, series of transactions, or to the account" and "in an electronic form that can be used by computer applications."

Fintech startups argue this language enshrines their right to pull data from customers' bank accounts when the customers give them permission. Companies such as Betterment LLC , an online investment manager, say that accessing bank-account data helps them make it easier for consumers to use investing apps, borrow money or move dollars between accounts.

Banks, on the other hand, say that while they support customers' right to share their account data, there should be certain restrictions as well. These are needed, they add, to protect consumers from third parties accessing more data than is authorized, or to track how data is used.

Already, Betterment and a group of well-funded fintech startups have created an industry group, called the Consumer Financial Data Rights group, in hope of protecting Section 1033 and pushing for broad implementation of it. The group, formed in January, said it would work with policy makers to promote "consumer choice and access."

Other group members include Personal Capital Corp., Affirm Inc., Kabbage Inc ., Varo Money Inc., and Hello Digit Inc.

"We seek to speak with a unified voice to Trump administration on this issue," said Steven Boms, vice president of government affairs at EnvestnetYodlee , a bank-data aggregation provider.

SECTION 1033

SEC. 1033. CONSUMER RIGHTS TO ACCESS INFORMATION.

(a) IN GENERAL. Subject to rules prescribed by the Bureau, a covered person shall make available to a consumer, upon request, information in the control or possession of the covered person concerning the consumer financial product or service that the consumer obtained from such covered person, including information relating to any transaction, series of transactions, or to the account including costs, charges and usage data. The information shall be made available in an electronic form usable by consumers.

(b) EXCEPTIONS. A covered person may not be required by this section to make available to the consumer 

(1) any confidential commercial information, including an algorithm used to derive credit scores or other risk scores or predictors;

(2) any information collected by the covered person for the purpose of preventing fraud or money laundering, or detecting, or making any report regarding other unlawful or potentially unlawful conduct;

(3) any information required to be kept confidential by any other provision of law; or

(4) any information that the covered person cannot retrieve in the ordinary course of its business with respect to that information.

(c) NO DUTY TO MAINTAIN RECORDS. Nothing in this section shall be construed to impose any duty on a covered person to maintain or keep any information about a consumer.

(d) STANDARDIZED FORMATS FOR DATA. The Bureau, by rule, shall prescribe standards applicable to covered persons to promote the development and use of standardized formats for information, including through the use of machine readable files, to be made available to consumers under this section.

(e) CONSULTATION. The Bureau shall, when prescribing any rule under this section, consult with the Federal banking agencies and the Federal Trade Commission to ensure, to the extent appropriate, that the rules 

(1) impose substantively similar requirements on covered persons;

(2) take into account conditions under which covered persons do business both in the United States and in other countries; and

(3) do not require or promote the use of any particular technology in order to develop systems for compliance.

Separately, Plaid Technologies Inc., which provides software tools for firms such as Betterment to access bank data, has been collecting signatures in favor of open data access from its customers and plans to present a petition to regulators and lawmakers in the coming weeks.

The fintech firms argue they need a provision like Section 1033, on which the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau has been gathering industry input as it considers potential rules, to ensure access for their apps and services. Because they aren't typically set up as banks, they don't have direct access to users' checking and savings accounts.

"The most important issue is access to customers' own financial data," said Jon Stein, chief executive of Betterment, in an interview. "Customers have a right to manage their own."

But banks haven't been so enamored with the current setup. They warn that handing over online banking passwords to third parties is risky, and that outside services constantly pulling data from bank servers is a technological burden and could destabilize financial networks.

In 2015, banks such as Bank of America Corp ., J.P. Morgan Chase & Co ., and Wells Fargo & Co . at times suspended access for services that were "scraping" data by scanning online banking websites.

Upstarts and banks have discussed compromise proposals, such as creating new ways to create anonymous passwords. Some banks also plan to submit comments to the CFPB on data-safety guidelines for sharing.

"We know customers love sharing their data, and banks are working hard to make sure they can share their data regardless of whether there's a law or not," said Robert Morgan, vice president for emerging technologies at the American Bankers Association , a banking industry group.

Banks and fintech companies have managed to reach some accommodations. J.P. Morgan Chase recently struck a deal with Intuit Inc ., which owns the Mint.com service, to enable the bank's customers to share data without giving up their password to Mint and other Intuit services.

Fintech firms argue that so long as Section 1033 or something like it remains, both sides have to deal with each other. With Dodd-Frank's future up in the air, though, the dynamics between the two groups could change.

(This article is available to Pro Financial Regulation subscribers first, before being published to wsj.com.)

Scaramucci's White House Role Now Uncertain

By Lisa Beilfuss, Michael C. Bender and Dave Michaels

SkyBridge Capital founder Anthony Scaramucci , who left his role as one of Wall Street's splashiest showmen in order to work for President Donald Trump, may not serve on the new, bigger stage after all.

Mr. Scaramucci, who last month prominently promoted Mr. Trump's agenda as his informal representative at the World Economic Forum in Davos, Switzerland, will "most likely" not take a position in the White House, an official in the Trump administration said Wednesday. The Trump official, who asked not to be identified, didn't give a detailed reason for the fresh doubts over an appointment that had been publicly disclosed by the Trump transition team in mid-January.

Despite new hesitation about Mr. Scaramucci inside the White House, it's not clear he intends to step away from the job, a sign that his role is caught up in the infighting that has marked the new administration.

"Donald Trump hasn't rescinded the job offer," said a person familiar with Mr. Scaramucci's situation. That person suggested that Mr. Scaramucci's role has stirred opposition from other aides worried about his ties to the first family. "Mr. Scaramucci has a direct relationship with the president and with the president's children," that person said.

Lawyers for Mr. Scaramucci could not be reached for comment Wednesday.

Mr. Scaramucci's uncertain future in the administration marks an awkward moment for the man who worked tirelessly to sell Mr. Trump to skeptical mainstream Republicans after he joined his campaign in May.

Instead of taking a flashy role within the White House one that would have involved frequent television appearances and scrumming with business leaders Mr. Scaramucci may become an ambassador. The White House official said that Mr. Scaramucci has been offered a diplomatic post, but declined to say in which country he could serve.

The reason for Mr. Scaramucci's iffy status within the Trump administration wasn't immediately clear. Several major GOP fundraisers close to him said they didn't know what had caused the change.

Mr. Scaramucci, an affable salesman and former stockbroker dubbed "The Mooch," had been slated to become head of the Trump administration's Office of Public Engagement and Intergovernmental Affairs. Under former President Barack Obama, the job was held by Valerie Jarrett, a longtime friend and adviser to Mr. Obama.

To assume the job, Mr. Scaramucci had to divest his sizable stake in Skybridge, a firm that pitches high-cost, hedge-fund investments to wealthy individuals. He quickly assembled a team of advisers for the sale who included Greg Fleming, a former president of Morgan Stanley's wealth and investment management businesses. Days after the White House confirmed Mr. Scaramucci's role in the administration, he struck a deal to sell his interest in SkyBridge Capital in a deal that valued the firm at around $200 million, people familiar with the matter said.

The buyers included a unit of Chinese conglomerate HNA Capital, an ambitious group that announced $21 billion in overseas acquisitions last year, and comes at a time when hedge-fund returns have lagged a mix of traditional stock and bond investments. The deal gives HNA a foothold in the global asset-management business as China's increasingly wealthy population looks for places to invest.

The extravagant hedge-fund conference Mr. Scaramucci launched during the nadir of the 2008 recession has also been flagged for sale or a possible spinoff after he accepted the White House Job. SALT, for SkyBridge Alternatives, is synonymous with Mr. Scaramucci and is known for attracting world leaders and famous athletes to the annual Las Vegas affair.

RushCard, Mastercard Fined Over 2015 Customer Lockout

By Yuka Hayashi

WASHINGTON UniRush LLC and a unit of Mastercard International Inc . agreed to settle accusations that "preventable failures" caused a 2015 debacle in which tens of thousands of customers lost access to their money stored on prepaid cards, setting off a cascade of unpaid bills and other personal-finance crunches.

The Consumer Financial Protection Bureau said Wednesday the two companies are paying about $10 million to customers affected by the temporary lockup of their accounts, triggered by a technical glitch. The companies are also paying a civil penalty of $3 million.

UniRush is the parent company of RushCard, a major prepaid-card business founded by hip-hop producer Russell Simmons . Mastercard Payment Transaction Services, a unit of Mastercard International , is RushCard's payment processor. The glitch occurred as RushCard was switching to using Mastercard from another provider.

The settlement came two days after Green Dot Corp ., a leading prepaid-card company, said it reached a deal to buy UniRush for $147 million.

The incident caused severe disruptions for customers, many of whom use prepaid cards for basic financial services including direct deposit of paychecks and government benefits as well as making purchases and paying bills. As customers were shut out of their accounts for days, and in some cases for weeks, some took to social media to air their grievances, complaining that they didn't have money to pay for basic necessities or they were hit with late fees on unpaid bills. Many prepaid-card customers are "unbanked" or "underbanked," meaning they have no or limited access to regular banking services such as checking accounts and credit and debit cards.

"All of this stemmed from a series of failures that should have been anticipated and prevented," CFPB Director Richard Cordray said on a conference call. These failures, he said, "caused serious problems for the financial lives of many customers."

RushCard said it welcomed the settlement with the CFPB but it didn't admit any wrongdoing. "Since the event in 2015, we believe we have fully compensated all of our customers for any inconvenience they may have suffered," a company representative said. The company settled a class-action lawsuit with customers last year that included reimbursement of $20 million. Mr. Simmons described the incident as "the most challenging periods in my professional career" and thanked customers for remaining loyal.

"We are pleased to bring this matter to a close, allowing us to further enhance the best practices, policies and procedures for prepaid cards," a Mastercard spokesman said. He added that the company understands "the critical role prepaid cards play in how people manage their money."

The RushCard settlement is part of the CFPB's scrutiny of the prepaid-card industry, a segment that has grown rapidly in recent years. The bureau last year finalized a new rule to bring oversight of the sector closer to regulations covering banks.

New payment technologies have elevated prepaid cards into popular financial-management tools rivaling bank checking accounts. Nearly 10% of U.S. households used such cards in 2015, according to a study by the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp .

The CFPB alleged that in October 2015, as RushCard was switching to Mastercard as payment processor, UniRush failed to transfer all customer accounts to Mastercard accurately and delayed processing direct deposits for more than 45,000 consumers. It also failed to provide customer service properly to those affected by the lockup.

Mastercard , according to the CFPB, didn't make sure it was sending accurate information about customers' accounts to UniRush.

The RushCard settlement is the latest action in a spree of enforcement activities by the CFPB in recent weeks, a move industry experts attribute to the bureau's desire to get done as much work as possible amid concern that the new Republican administration will move to constrain its authority. Mr. Cordray said in an interview last week at a WSJ Pro Financial Regulation event that the bureau's enforcement work would continue at a "steady and vigorous" pace, and warned some major cases were still in the pipeline.

Since the beginning of January, the CFPB made eight announcements of enforcement actions, including a high-profile lawsuit filed against Navient Corp ., the nation's largest student-loan servicer, for allegedly obstructing repayments from customers. Navient denies the accusations.

WSJ Pro: International Regulator Publishes Draft Standards Around Too-Big-To-Fail Clearinghouses

By Julia-Ambra Verlaine

BRUSSELS Global financial supervisors are advancing plans to safeguard clearinghouses they consider too-big-to-fail and that could topple the financial system in the case of collapse.

The Swiss-based Financial Stability Board published draft standards Wednesday on how companies such as London-based LCH.Clearnet Group Ltd . and Deutsche Brse AG 's Eurex should plan for default and manage recovery plans, both of which international regulators have found insufficient to date.

Clearinghouses, platforms known as central counterparties that stand between buyers and sellers of financial instruments, have come under the gaze of officials and supervisors given their increased role in the financial system after the 2008 crisis.

"They have grown substantially over the last years. The failure of a CCP would have a significant impact on financial stability," said Elke Knig, chairwoman of Europe's Single Resolution Board , which is in charge of handling Europe's failing banks.

Mostly used by banks and investment firms, the clearinghouses ensure that either party in a derivatives transaction gets paid if the other side falters. The FSB published a note in August calling for public comment from the industry to help develop resolution strategies and plans for winding them down in case of a global financial shock.

Wednesday's draft guidelines are a first step toward international standards for clearinghouse recovery and resolution plans.

Similar to post-crisis rules built to deal with failing banks, the FSB would like to avoid leaving taxpayers on the hook in case a large clearinghouse were to fail. The FSB guidance stipulates details around the reimbursement of any temporary government money used to fund a wind-down.

Arrangements for the recovery of temporary public funding should be written into the legal framework and the clearinghouse rules, the FSB said in the paper. The clearinghouses should also have a legal basis to collect cash from both domestic and foreign participants of the clearinghouse in order remove potential cross-border conflicts.

The FSB guidelines define the role national supervisors and authorities have to play in handling a failing clearinghouse, alongside creditor rights. It also urges to establishment of international, cross-border "crisis" groups to coordinate plans for clearinghouses that are systemically important across multiple jurisdictions.

The FSB's efforts are complemented by work carried out by the European Commission , the EU's executive arm, which put forward a legislative proposal in November that would require the clearinghouses to develop frameworks for resolution in a similar vein.

The FSB is open to consultation and feedback, which will last until March 13 before proceeding with final guidelines.

A Revamp at the Fed? Ways Lawmakers Have Sought Central-Bank Change

By Kate Davidson

House Republicans are hopeful that a Trump White House will be sympathetic to their efforts to revamp the Federal Reserve, including measures to subject the central bank to more congressional scrutiny and limit its discretionary powers.

President Donald Trump hasn't said whether he would support the measures, but his own public remarks and those of some of his economic advisers suggest he is skeptical of the Fed's independence and the job it has done steering the economy.

Though the chances of such bills becoming law are higher than they were under the Obama administration, they still would have to get through Congress first. A number of factors could influence whether and when that happens.

Central-bank legislation appears to be low on the GOP's priority list, which already includes plans to repeal the Affordable Care Act, overhaul the corporate tax code and pass a big infrastructure spending plan. There may be little time left to debate the merits of auditing the Fed's interest-rate decisions or requiring it to follow a rules-based approach to monetary policy.

It also hasn't gotten any easier for legislation to make it through Congress.

While Fed-related measures have broad Republican support in the House, most bills or amendments still require 60 votes to advance in the Senate, and Republicans control two fewer seats than they did previously 52 compared with 54 in the last Congress. That means supporters of such legislation would need to convince at least eight Senate Democrats and the entire GOP caucus to support their efforts a heavy lift for such a polarizing issue.

"It's going to be hard for them to do a lot of what they want to do anyway," Andy Laperriere, a policy analyst at research firm Cornerstone Macro, said of the Republican majority.

Recent history has demonstrated, however, there is more than one way to move Fed-related changes through Congress.

Twice in 2015, Republicans attached Fed-related provisions to unrelated bills that Democrats weren't willing to vote against, despite the Fed's opposition.

A law reauthorizing the Terrorism Risk Insurance Program included a requirement requiring the U.S. president to nominate at least one member to the Fed board with community banking experience. Congress also tapped the Fed's capital surplus account to help pay for new federal highway spending, over strong objections from Fed officials who warned such a move would undermine its independence.

Some Republican senators also are considering the possibility of using a special legislative procedure known as budget reconciliation that would allow them to make changes to the 2010 Dodd-Frank law with just a simple majority, rather than a 60-vote super majority. It isn't clear whether such changes would include provisions related to the Fed's monetary policy operations.

Finally, there could be room for some bipartisan compromise on changes to the Fed, such as limiting its emergency lending powers. But that depends greatly on whether those provisions are included in a broader package to overhaul the Dodd-Frank law, which may not win much Democratic support.

Some senior Fed officials think the central bank should pursue changes on its own that would satisfy Republicans' calls for more public information about its deliberations, such as releasing transcripts of its policy meetings sooner than the current five-year lag, or putting more information in its reports to Congress about which rules it has consulted in its interest-rate decisions.

What measures may come up now that Mr. Trump is in the White House? Here are the major legislative proposals that Republicans and a few Democrats have tried to advance in recent years. Each measure has be reintroduced in the new Congress, which began in January 2017, to be considered.

Audit the Fed

What is it: Legislation to require the Government Accountability Office to audit the Fed's monetary policy decisions. The Fed's financial statements are already audited, and the GAO can examine most other Fed operations, but Congress has long exempted monetary policy decisions from its purview.

Who supports it: Former Rep. Ron Paul had been pushing a version of this legislation for years before his son Sen. Rand Paul (R., Ky.) joined the Senate and took up the cause. It has gained support since the financial crisis among Republicans unhappy with the Fed's actions and its role in the economy.

Where it stands: Rand Paul and Rep. Thomas Massie (R., Ky.) reintroduced measures in the Senate and House, respectively, in January. The Senate blocked the bill in early 2016 on a vote of 53-44, short of the 60 votes needed to clear a procedural hurdle. The provision was included in a broader bill that passed the House 241-185 on a party-line vote.

What the Fed says: Chairwoman Janet Yellen and other officials oppose the measure, warning it could subject the Fed to political pressure on monetary policy.

The FORM Act

What it is: A main feature of the Fed Oversight Reform and Modernization Act is the so-called Taylor Rule requirement, which would require Fed officials to establish a mathematical formula to guide their interest-rate decisions, and require them to report to Congress if they deviate from the rule. The bill also would allow the GAO to audit the Fed's policy decisions; widen membership of the Fed's rate-setting committee; require the Fed leader to testify four times each year, instead of two; and place new restrictions on the Fed's emergency lending powers; in addition to other provisions related to regulatory policy.

Who supports it: The measure was introduced in the last Congress by Rep. Bill Huizenga (R., Mich.), the former chairman of the House monetary policy subcommittee, and had 20 Republican co-sponsors.

Where it stands: The bill passed the House 241-185 on a party-line vote in the last Congress, but the Senate never voted on it. Rep. Jeb Hensarling (R., Texas), the chairman of the House Financial Services Committee, has said advancing the bill in the current Congress would be a priority.

What the Fed says: Fed officials oppose it. "I believe, and I think most of my colleagues would agree, that we shouldn't mechanically follow that rule or any other rule but that we need to take into account how the economy is performing," Ms. Yellen told the House panel in February.

Financial Choice Act

What it is: In addition to the changes that would be required under the FORM Act, the bill would require the Fed's spending on activities unrelated to monetary policy to go through the congressional appropriations process; require the Fed to report to Congress on any negotiations or setting of financial standards with international regulators; and establish a "centennial commission" to study the long-term effects of Fed policy on the economy, and make recommendations to Congress for possible changes to Fed operations, transparency or governance.

Who supports it: Mr. Hensarling introduced the bill in the last Congress.

Where it stands: The House Financial Services Committee approved it, but it never made it to the House floor for a vote last year. It hasn't been reintroduced.

What the Fed says: Because the Fed provisions of this bill are nearly identical to the FORM Act, the Fed has many of the same reservations.

Bailout Prevention Act

What it is: The measure would significantly curtail the Fed's powers to lend to financial firms in an emergency. The 2010 Dodd-Frank law put some restrictions in place, but lawmakers on both sides of the aisle aren't satisfied the Fed has taken the necessary steps to implement those restrictions.

Who supports it: This measure has bipartisan support. Sen. Elizabeth Warren (D., Mass.), one of the Fed's most high-profile critics, introduced a version of the bill in the Senate, and a similar measure was introduced in the House. Republicans and Democrats, including Ms. Warren, signed letters calling on the Fed to toughen up its own rules for lending to banks in an emergency.

Where it stands: Lawmakers haven't held hearings on either bill the first step in the process but a similar provision was included in the Financial Choice Act, suggesting Republicans are interested in tackling this issue in some way. The measures haven't been reintroduced yet.

What the Fed says: Fed officials oppose it. Fed governor Jerome Powell said in February 2015 that "further restricting or eliminating the Fed's emergency lending authority will not prevent future crises, but it will hinder the Fed's ability to limit the harm from those crises for families and businesses."

Fed Capital Stock

What is it: The measure would require the Fed to pay back billions of dollars in capital that banks paid to be members of the Fed system. The bill was introduced in 2015 after Congress voted to lower the dividend the Fed pays banks on that capital to help pay for federal highway programs.

Who supports it: Former Republican Rep. Randy Neugebauer introduced the measure, which has strong backing from the banking industry, but it isn't clear whether it has any support in the Senate or who may reintroduce it in the House.

Where it stands: Mr. Neugebauer tried to convince his House colleagues to include the provision in a year-end spending bill in 2015, but it was ultimately left out. He introduced it as a stand-alone bill last year, but it was never taken up by the House Financial Services Committee .

What the Fed says: Fed officials have expressed a variety of concerns about the proposal.

Credit Card Fraud Keeps Rising, Despite New Security Chips Study

By AnnaMaria Andriotis and Peter Rudegeair

More consumers became victims of identity fraud last year than at any point in more than a decade despite new security protections implemented by the credit-card industry, a report released Wednesday said.

Some 15.4 million U.S. consumers were victims of identity fraud in 2016, resulting in $16 billion in total losses, according to the report by consulting firm Javelin Strategy & Research and identity-theft-protection firm LifeLock Inc . The number of victims rose 18% from 2015 and was the highest since Javelin, a unit of Greenwich Associates LLC , started tracking the phenomenon in 2003.

The increase in identity fraud, the bulk of which comes from card activity, was driven in part by a 15% rise in cases of fraudulent online purchases, the study noted. That activity led to "existing-card" fraud, which involves criminals counterfeiting debit and credit cards already held by customers, reaching a new peak.

Big increases also occurred in rarer frauds that have been steadily rising in recent years and that are harder for consumers and lenders to detect. The incidence of new-account fraud, for example, in which new accounts are opened in consumers' names without their knowledge or knowledge by the lender, rose 40% to 1.8 million, while the number of cases of consumers having a bank account, credit card or other account taken over improperly increased by over one-fifth to 1.4 million.

The increases happened despite the rollout of tougher security measures around debit and credit cards over the past couple of years. Most major card issuers in the U.S. have replaced consumers' magnetic stripe cards with chip cards, and merchants have increasingly shifted to more secure payment terminals, which combined are supposed to make it harder for consumers' card information to be stolen.

But the findings of the report suggest that swindlers are finding ways around the new measures. "Fraud is kind of like squeezing Jell-O," said Stephen Coggeshall, chief analytics and science officer at LifeLock . "Stop it one place, and it migrates to somewhere else."

With online shopping, there is no way to use the chips on cards. Most of those merchants still rely on the basic card numbers, expiration dates and security codes on the cards. That is largely why "card-not-present" fraud, in which criminals use card information to make transactions online without needing to present the actual card, affected 3.4% of consumers, up from 2.4% in 2015, according to the study.

Card networks, including Visa Inc ., say chip cards have helped bring down the counterfeit fraud rate, in which card data are stolen from merchants and used to create fake cards. Visa also said last month that its fraud rate at online merchants was down 1% in September from a year earlier.

In response to the persistence of online card fraud, credit-card networks, banks and technology firms are investing in more secure methods of sharing payment details online, such as "tokenization," in which a unique code is generated for each transaction to be approved instead of cardholders' account numbers.

MasterCard Inc. Chief Financial Officer Martina Hund-Mejean said on an earnings call Tuesday that the company is increasing spending in 2017 largely to boost customer and merchant usage of its digital wallet that uses tokenization, known as Masterpass.

Separately, the study showed that nearly half of the instances of card fraud involved chip-enabled card accounts that have been used online or at stores that don't yet have chip-enabled terminals. Some 64% of storefront merchants still only accept cards' magnetic stripe, according to the study.

While other forms of identity fraud are less common than existing-card fraud, swindlers are exploiting them in part because they take longer to detect. With new-account fraud, total losses reached $3.6 billion, up 24% from 2015, even though less than 1% of consumers were affected.

Some 30% of all new-account fraud victims last year had a general-purpose credit card opened in their name, up from 21% in 2015, which often resulted in card issuers giving out chip-enabled cards to swindlers, said Al Pascual, research director and head of fraud and security at Javelin.

Separately, total losses associated with a swindler taking over a consumer's account rose nearly two-thirds to $2.3 billion.

Still, chip usage has helped bring down the amount of losses on existing card fraud to $961 on average per incident in 2016, down 2% from a year prior and down 30% from 2013.

House Passes Resolution to Kill Extractive Anti-Graft Rule

By Samuel Rubenfeld

The House of Representatives voted to repeal a Securities and Exchange Commission rule on oil, gas and mining companies that is aimed at preventing corruption.

The House passed, on a vote of 235-187, a joint resolution under the Congressional Review Act disapproving the rule established by Section 1504 of the Dodd-Frank Act that requires oil, gas and mining companies to annually disclose the payments they make to foreign governments for things such as licenses and permits.

The Congressional Review Act, a rarely-applied law, allows legislators to repeal government-agency rules on a majority vote if they were finalized in the last 60 legislative days. The U.S. Senate is expected to vote Thursday on the resolution. The SEC rule on oil, gas and mining companies was finalized in June 2016.

Activists and observers have said payments to governments for permits can be used to hide bribes, but industry groups have said the rule's compliance requirements are costly and anti-competitive. The hour-long debate on the House floor focused on whether the rule, in fact, prevents corruption, or is an example of regulatory overreach.

"Even without this rule in effect  & corruption will still be corruption, and it should be punished with the full extent of the law," said Rep. Bill Huizenga (R., Mich.), who had introduced the legislation to remove the rule.

"Conducting American foreign policy is not what Congress created the SEC to do," he said.

Democratic lawmakers countered, saying the compliance requirements of the rule aren't onerous, and they are merely a way to prevent resource-sector companies from hiding bribes that are illegal under the Foreign Corrupt Practices Act.

"That's all we're talking about here transparency," said Rep. Carolyn Maloney (D., N.Y.).

The rule is set to go into effect in 2018, but some companies already report such payments under similar rules imposed in Europe and Canada. It has been fought over since the passage of the Dodd-Frank in 2010. The oil industry successfully sued to vacate the first iteration of the rule approved in 2012, but after pressure from non-profits, the SEC rewrote its rule.

Activists immediately condemned the House vote, saying it supports corruption.

"Voting to roll back basic transparency rules provides zero benefit for the public but will instead allow corrupt elites continue to stuff their pockets with oil money and steal from their citizens," said Isabel Munilla, senior policy adviser for extractive industries at Oxfam America, in a statement.

The two authors of the Dodd-Frank provision, former Sen. Richard Lugar and current Sen. Ben Cardin (D., Md.) had called for the rule to stay in place, saying Tuesday in an op-ed that the provision, when passed, showed U.S. leadership on fighting corruption in the resource sectors, and the argument that the provision is anti-competitive is a red herring because of rules now in effect in 30 countries.

"With Europe and Canada in the same disclosure system, the playing field is now level. And the companies already filing have suffered no commercial harm nor revealed vital secrets," they wrote.

J.P. Morgan to Pay Lehman Post-Bankruptcy Estate $797.5 Million

By Patrick Fitzgerald

J.P. Morgan Chase & Co . and the remnants of Lehman Brothers Wednesday resolved their remaining disputes over the investment bank's 2008 collapse with a deal that will result in the failed investment bank's creditors recovering nearly $800 million.

Lawyers for Lehman said in a filing with the U.S. Bankruptcy Court in New York that the global settlement "finally resolves the last of [Lehman's] disputes with J.P. Morgan , its largest secured creditor, and enables creditor distributions of nearly $800 million."

In return for the $797.5 million payment, Lehman agreed to drop its objection to J.P. Morgan 's $30 billion in so-called Tassimo claims related to losses the bank says it suffered when it was forced to liquidate Lehman's securities following the investment bank's bankruptcy.

J.P. Morgan served as Lehman's clearing bank. The bank provided cash advances of up to $100 billion a day to Lehman to facilitate overnight repurchase, or repo, agreements. That role resulted in J.P. Morgan being one of Lehman's adversaries in numerous disputes surrounding the investment bank's demise as well as one of its largest creditors.

When the dust from Lehman's bankruptcy settled, J.P. Morgan said, some $30 billion it had advanced to Lehman was left unpaid. The bank had filed claims against Lehman's bankruptcy estate for that amount.

"We are pleased to have resolved Lehman's remaining claims and to put the matter behind us," said J.P. Morgan spokesman Brian Marchiony. He added the bank had previously reserved for this settlement.

A Lehman spokeswoman declined to comment.

J.P. Morgan had earlier agreed to pay Lehman Brothers $1.42 billion in cash to settle most of a 2010 lawsuit over what it called J.P. Morgan 's "cash grab" in the weeks before the investment bank's September 2008 collapse.

That deal, which a bankruptcy judge approved last year, also put to rest Lehman's challenges over J.P. Morgan 's closeout of thousands of derivatives contracts between the two financial firms. A hearing on the latest settlement, which also requires court approval, is set for Feb. 16.

Lehman, once the nation's fourth-largest investment bank, collapsed into the largest bankruptcy ever in September 2008, and its U.S. brokerage business was quickly sold off to Barclays PLC .

A team of bankruptcy professionals under the direction of turnaround firm Alvarez & Marsal managed Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc .'s assets until the holding company's exit from chapter 11 in 2012, when a reorganized company emerged, overseen by a new board.

The remains of the holding company, which has returned more than $113 billion to creditors, is expected to exist in some form for several more years as it unwinds its remaining holdings.

Bundesbank's Dombret: No Deal on Basel III Better Than Bad Deal

By Todd Buell

FRANKFURT Deutsche Bundesbank wants a compromise in protracted negotiations over international banking standards that is good for the country, the German central bank's representative for banking supervision said Thursday. He added, however, that not getting a deal is better than achieving a bad deal.

The comments come as international regulators continued to work to iron out a final arrangement over an agreement known as Basel III. The completion of these rules has been held up by a clash between bankers and regulators over how lenders calculate the riskiness of assets and, therefore, the amount of capital they have to hold. Many European banks use "internal models," which U.S. regulators argue have allowed them to underestimate capital needs.

Regulators put off a meeting scheduled for January that was supposed to complete a deal. An agreement could potentially come in March.

Andreas Dombret of the Bundesbank said that his institution is working to ensure that a final deal doesn't mean higher capital requirements. "The motto must thus be: better no deal than a bad deal," he said, according to a speech text prepared for delivery in Berlin.

"At the same time, however, it is true that an international standard has great importance which cannot be underestimated," he said. In times in which countries are becoming ever more introverted, this is even more true, he said.

"The Bundesbank will continue to work for a compromise on Basel III, a compromise which is good for Germany," he said.

Deutsche Bank Posts Loss as Litigation Costs Bite

By Jenny Strasburg and William Wilkes

FRANKFURT Deutsche Bank AG on Thursday reported a worse-than-expected net loss in the fourth quarter, ending another tumultuous year hurt by litigation costs, client concerns about the lender's stability and economic headwinds that challenged European banks broadly.

Germany's biggest lender made a net loss of  ¬1.9 billion ($2.05 billion) after taking  ¬1.6 billion in litigation charges in the fourth quarter. That compared with a year-earlier loss of  ¬2.1 billion in the fourth quarter.

Analysts had expected a fourth-quarter net loss of  ¬1.57 billion, according to consensus figures compiled by Deutsche Bank .

Its shares were down more than 5% Thursday morning.

Deutsche Bank said it was stung late last year by a swirl of speculation about what it might have to pay to settle its biggest overhanging legal issue, related to U.S. probes into toxic mortgage securities. Clients pulled away, denting its global trading operation and other pockets of the bank, which didn't fare as well as competitors' despite spikes in market volatility that pumped up fixed-income trading.

That meant that the U.K.'s decision to leave the European Union and U.S. election-driven trading didn't benefit Deutsche Bank as much as it did many others in bond trading and other areas where it is historically strong. Nervous clients also pulled back from trading stocks through Deutsche Bank , it said Thursday. Asset-management clients have withdrawn some of their business, hurting fees in that unit. The concerns about Deutsche Bank 's financial position last year increased the costs needed to fund its businesses.

In December, the lender agreed to pay $7.2 billion to settle the high-profile U.S. mortgage-securities cases in a deal completed last month with the U.S. Justice Department . The $3.1 billion cash portion of that settlement came in below worst-case fears but is still a burden on the lender's thin capital cushion.

In total, the bank has in the past two months agreed to pay out around $8 billion to settle some of its biggest long-running legal matters with the U.S. Justice Department and U.K. and New York regulators, but unresolved investigations still concern investors. The lender said Thursday it has set aside  ¬7.6 billion for litigation. Of that, around  ¬4.7 billion is earmarked for settlements already reached. Some of the payouts, such as relief to mortgage consumers hurt by the toxic securities, aren't due until next year or after.

Quarterly net revenue rose 6% to  ¬7.1 billion but for the full year was down 10% at  ¬30 billion. For the full year, Deutsche Bank 's net loss was  ¬1.4 billion, following a 2015 loss of  ¬6.8 billion.

Deutsche Bank 's shares have declined about 30% since Chief Executive John Cryan and his revamped management board announced a multiyear turnaround plan in October 2015. Their strategy has been to shrink some businesses and sell others, overhaul the lender's technology and compliance systems and cut thousands of employees while trying to preserve the strength of profit engines such as bond trading, deal advising and corporate lending.

But some of those key businesses have lost momentum, in part to stronger U.S. banks.

Deutsche Bank said its common equity Tier 1 ratio, a key measure of financial health, was 11.9% at the year-end, up from 11.1% at the end of the third quarter. That closely watched figure is subject to increasingly strict regulatory requirements, and Deutsche Bank 's 2018 target is a primary focus of investors' concerns about whether the bank will need to sell shares to raise cash. Mr. Cryan has said he doesn't want to do that and that the bank meets its capital requirements.

European banks broadly have been hurt by low and negative interest rates, which eat into lending profit. Deutsche Bank 's challenges are exacerbated by an especially low-margin retail banking industry in Germany, and by the persistent questions surrounding its capital position that have driven away some clients.

Deutsche Bank said last month it was slashing bonuses for many senior bankers and other staff, and that its top executives won't get bonuses for a second year. The cost-cutting moves have been cheered by analysts and investors who for years criticized the bank for promising to trim expenses only to disappoint. But morale among Deutsche Bank employees also has suffered, and competitors have poached some of its senior bankers and traders.

Mr. Cryan said Thursday that the lender has had "a promising start to this year." The bank's liquidity reserves were  ¬218 billion at year-end, an increase from  ¬200 billion at the end of September.

The bank cited as accomplishments its lower costs and the wind-down of a unit that housed noncore holdings. The full-time employee head count was slightly more than 98,000 at year-end, compared with 101,000 at the end of 2015.

Deutsche Bank 's 2016 provisions for credit losses increased to  ¬1.38 billion, from  ¬960 million in 2015, chiefly due to its shipping portfolio. Several European banks are wrestling with high exposure to a yearslong global crisis in the shipping sector.

Some Democratic senators sent a letter to Wells Fargo (WFC) following a WSJ article about the bank giving advance notice of branch reviews and some employees attempting to cover up sham accounts, according to a copy of the letter reviewed by WSJ. Bernie Sanders (D, Vt) and opposed a measure to audit the Fed; and Sen Bob Corker (R, Tenn), one of the few Senate Republicans willing to openly support the central bank. (kate.davidson@wsj.com)

UniRush and a Mastercard (MA) unit agree to settle charges from the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau over a 2015 debacle where tens of thousands of customers were locked out of their prepaid accounts. UniRush, the program manager of RushCard, founded by hip-hop producer Russell Simmons, didn't admit any wrongdoing. It is common among companies to settle outstanding regulatory enforcement cases before closing mergers and acquisition deals. (yuka.hayashi@wsj.com; @TokyoWoods)

Ip Analysis: Contrasting Growth Views Could Unite Trump, Yellen on Rates; The Fed leader will be setting interest rates according to her economic forecast, not the president's

By Greg Ip 
556 words
2 February 2017
11:03
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

President Donald Trump thinks he can return the economy to 4% annual growth. Janet Yellen, the Federal Reserve chairwoman, is expecting little more than 2%.

This looks like a recipe for a collision course. In fact, the dichotomy could help keep the peace between a growth-hungry president and inflation-wary central bank. The reason is that Ms. Yellen will be setting interest rates according to her forecast, not Mr. Trump's, and if her forecast is right, interest rates aren't going very high.

The statement "https://www.wsj.com/articles/fed-leaves-policy-rate-unchanged-offers-no-hint-on-when-it-might-next-move-1485975718" underlined this point. As expected, the Fed left its benchmark short-term interest rate between 0.5% and 0.75%. More important, it gave no clue it was in a hurry to raise it again. Last December, Fed officials indicated they would raise rates three times this year, and there's no sign they have a higher number in mind.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/fed-raises-rates-for-first-time-in-2016-anticipates-3-increases-in-2017-1481742086", in December, they expect just 2.1% economic growth in 2017. Wednesday's statement did note the surge in business and consumer confidence since Mr. Trump was elected in November. But officials are wary of reading too much into confidence readings; they want to see actual behavior follow suit. "It has seen false dawns repeatedly in this expansion," notes Krishna Guha, a policy analyst at Evercore ISI.

Recent readings on purchasing managers' activity are upbeat, as was Wednesday's reading of "https://www.wsj.com/articles/private-sector-hiring-off-to-strong-start-in-2017-1485956456?tesla=y" by Automatic Data Processing Inc. and forecasting firm Moody's Analytics. Nonetheless, investors' bets on "Trumpflation" appear to have peaked. The stock market and bond yields have both been range-bound since mid-December. The dollar has weakened. Roberto Perli at Cornerstone Macro infers that markets are expecting a moderate boost to growth from Mr. Trump's fiscal plans this year. My colleague James Mackintosh notes that the "https://www.wsj.com/articles/the-markets-dont-believe-in-trump-for-the-long-term-1485790384" can be construed the same way. There's no pressure on the Fed from inflation. In recent months, core inflation (which excludes food and energy prices) has stabilized "https://www.wsj.com/articles/u-s-consumer-inflation-firmed-spending-accelerated-in-december-1485783234", using the Fed's preferred measure.

At this point, Fed officials have no firm views on how fiscal policy will unfold. Yet if baseline expectations of only a modest boost to growth prove correct, it's hard to see why the Fed would change its plans or why the markets would sell off. Bond yields have already priced in the Fed's projected path of gradual rate increases.

Unlike other recent presidents, Mr. Trump probably feels no compunction about commenting on the independent Fed. If 4% growth eludes him, he may blame Ms. Yellen. Yet if Ms. Yellen tightens as glacially as she now expects, it's difficult to see the economic or market mayhem that would set Mr. Trump off.

What if, instead, unemployment falls sharply and growth does start to accelerate? The Fed will, of course, tighten faster. Yet Mr. Trump may be so busy taking credit for the economic boom and soaring stock market, he may not bother to complain.

Banking & Finance: Credit-Card Fraud Keeps Rising --- Industry adds chips and other protections, but number of victims increases by 18%

By AnnaMaria Andriotis and Peter Rudegeair 
587 words
2 February 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
B6
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

More consumers became victims of identity fraud last year than at any point in more than a decade despite new security protections implemented by the credit-card industry, a report released Wednesday said.

Some 15.4 million U.S. consumers were victims of identity fraud in 2016, resulting in $16 billion in total losses, according to the report by consulting firm Javelin Strategy & Research and identity-theft-protection firm LifeLock Inc. The number of victims rose 18% from 2015 and was the highest since Javelin, a unit of Greenwich Associates LLC, started tracking the phenomenon in 2003.

The increase in identity fraud, the bulk of which comes from card activity, was driven in part by a 15% rise in cases of fraudulent online purchases, the study noted. That activity led to "existing-card" fraud, which involves criminals counterfeiting debit and credit cards already held by customers, reaching a new peak.

Big increases also occurred in rarer frauds that have been steadily rising in recent years and that are harder for consumers and lenders to detect. The incidence of new-account fraud, for example, in which new accounts are opened in consumers' names without their knowledge or knowledge by the lender, rose 40% to 1.8 million, while the number of cases of consumers having a bank account, credit card or other account taken over improperly increased by over one-fifth to 1.4 million.

The increases happened despite the rollout of tougher security measures around debit and credit cards over the past couple of years.

Most major card issuers in the U.S. have replaced consumers' magnetic stripe cards with chip cards, and merchants have increasingly shifted to more secure payment terminals, which combined are supposed to make it harder for consumers' card information to be stolen.

But the findings of the report suggest that fraudsters are finding ways around the new measures. "Fraud is kind of like squeezing Jell-O," said Stephen Coggeshall, chief analytics and science officer at LifeLock. "Stop it one place, and it migrates to somewhere else."

With online shopping, there is no way to use the chips on cards. Most of those merchants still rely on the basic card numbers, expiration dates and security codes on the cards. That is largely why "card-not-present" fraud, in which criminals use card information to make transactions online without needing to present the actual card, affected 3.4% of consumers, up from 2.4% in 2015, according to the study.

Card networks, including Visa Inc., say chip cards have helped bring down the counterfeit fraud rate, in which card data are stolen from merchants and used to create fake cards. Visa also said last month that its fraud rate at online merchants was down 1% in September from a year earlier.

In response to the persistence of online card fraud, credit-card networks, banks and technology firms are investing in more secure methods of sharing payment details online, such as "tokenization," in which a unique code is generated for each transaction to be approved instead of cardholders' account numbers.

Mastercard Inc. Chief Financial Officer Martina Hund-Mejean said on an earnings call Tuesday that the company is increasing spending in 2017 largely to boost customer and merchant usage of its digital wallet that uses tokenization, known as Masterpass.

Online lender SoFi adds to portfolio with Zenbanx deal

Ben McLannahan in New York

"https://www.ft.com/stream/organisationsId/MjVlZTFhZGQtZTYyOC00MTAzLTgxZmUtNWZkY2RhZWM5MGI3-T04=", the biggest online lender in the US, has taken a big step in its attempt to build a retail banking empire by buying Zenbanx, another  fintech  challenger that offers bank accounts, money transfers and debit cards.

San Francisco-basedSoFi has beenat the forefront of a new breed of lenders that have sprung up following the financial crisis, promising to supply cash to borrowers at speeds and rates that the big brick-and-mortar banks cannot match. Since its founding six years ago it has raced to about $15bn of assets generated, pushing into new products such asmortgages and personal loans from its roots in student loans.

The core product of Zenbanx, which was founded in 2012 by Arkadi Kuhlmann, a former head of ING Direct, is a mobile banking account that lets people save, spend and send money in multiple currencies, both domestically and internationally. Accounts are insured through a partnership with WSFS Bank, the banking arm of a $6.6bn-in-assets group headquartered in Delaware.

Terms of the acquisition were not disclosed, but a person familiar with the deal said that SoFi would pay Zenbanx s venture capital backers less than $100m in stock when the deal closes in mid-February. Zenbanx accounts will then be rebranded as SoFi accounts, allowing SoFi to broaden the range of products offered to its 225,000 customers, or  members .

Mr Kuhlmann is joining SoFi as a member of the executive team, in charge of banking products.

 SoFi and Zenbanx share a vision that banking should and can be better, and have built products and services to do it,  said Mike Cagney, SoFi s co-founder, chief executive and chairman.He said SoFi was moving  closer to becoming the centre of our members  financial lives .

Many online lenders have had atough time over the past year, aschoppy credit markets, rising delinquencies and a governance scandal at Lending Club prompted many loan buyers to put programmes on hold. That, in turn, has caused the groups to cut back ongenerating assets, squeezing their bottom lines as revenues falter.

Data from Orchard, a technology and data provider, show that volumes across US consumer-loan platforms slipped for a third successive quarter between July and September, dropping 21 per cent to $1.86bn. That was less than half of the peak in the fourth quarter last year.

One of SoFi s big buyers of student loans,Citizens Financial Group, told the Financial Times last month that it planned to taper its purchases this year, as it originates more loans of its own.  If we re doing more on our own we need a little less from them,  said Bruce van Saun, chief executive of the Providence, Rhode Island-based bank.

Other online lenders have made efforts to diversify sources of fee income.Lending Club, the San Francisco-based specialist in supplying personal loans over the web, branched out into car loans in October, while Avant of Chicago signed a deal to supply its underwriting technology toRegions Financial Corp of Birmingham, Alabama.

Several big platforms have foundered and more will probably follow, said James Wu, chief executive of Monja, a loan-analytics firm.

 Honestly, I don t think many of the venture-backed lending platforms will survive in their current form,  he said.  Unless you can find a strategic investor who wants a big share of the loan supply, the only plausible path forward is to be acquired by a bank.

Computer-driven hedge funds join industry top performers

Lindsay Fortado and Miles Johnson 
603 words
1 February 2017
05:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Computer-based hedge funds have been admitted to a list of the all-time top 20 best performers for the first time in a sign that the dominance of traditional human investing is being radically challenged by technology.

DE Shaw, Citadel and Two Sigma, which all incorporate so-called  systematic strategies  that trade using computer algorithms, joined the closely followed annual list compiled by LCH Investments, the fund of hedge funds run by the Edmond de Rothschild group.

The 20 best-performing hedge fund managers of all time index measures the total dollars made for investors since inception. DE Shaw, which manages $27bn in assets, entered the list in third spot, while Ken Griffin s Citadel joined at number five. Two Sigma came in 20th place.

However, some well known hedge funds have fallen from the list this year. Lansdowne Partners, which joined the list for the first time in 2015 at number 20, fell out after its main fund lost 15 per cent in 2016. Lee Ainslie s Maverick and Jonathan Jacobson s Highfields fund managers also dropped from the list.

The biggest loss among the 20 managers last year was by John Paulson. His fund management group, Paulson & Co, fell six spots in the ranking to 13th place following a net loss of $3bn, according to LCH.

LCH estimates that the three newcomers, along with Bridgewater s Pure Alpha Fund, which also has a systematic element, have made $90bn of gains for investors over the past 10 years.

The move to include systematic funds by LCH shows the prevalence of modern quantitative trading strategies at hedge funds. The industry has largely shifted away from using solely discretionary methods to incorporating systematic trading and analysis of big data.

 The increasing capabilities of technology-based investment systems is evident in these results,  said Rick Sopher, chairman of LCH Investments.

 These systems are being used by many of the most successful investment firms to provide alternative data sources, processed investment analysis and artificial intelligence. 

Ray Dalio s Bridgewater, the world s largest hedge fund, held the top spot on the list, having made $49.4bn in net gains since it launched in 1975. George Soros s Soros Fund Management stayed in second place with $41.8bn made since 1973, despite a net loss of $1bn last year.

While hedge funds  returns in 2016 mostly improved from the prior year, some managers came under fire for the high fees being charged for lacklustre results.

LCH estimates that the top 20 managers generated $16.1bn in gains after fees last year on a weighted basis, an average increase of 2.6 per cent.

 The overall returns from hedge funds in 2016 were disappointing,  Mr Sopher said.  Even the managers with the greatest long-term records did not perform strongly and their results were no better than the average. This underperformance by the world s greatest money managers reflects the difficulties experienced by most active managers for much of 2016. 

The research also found that the top 20 managers generated 46.9 per cent of all the gains made by the hedge fund industry since inception, while only accounting for 17.6 per cent of the assets.

 Although 2016 was a difficult year overall for active managers, including those which had performed strongly in the past, results improved sharply toward the end of the year,  Mr Sopher said.

 This, combined with the continuing trend to lower fees, should improve the prospects for hedge fund managers to generate positive returns after fees for their investors.

COMPANIES
China couriers learn to tap ecommerce boom; Robust competition in the country's $2.2tn logistics sector is fuelling tech-driven growth and innovative cost-cutting

By Louise Lucas in Hong Kong 
1,317 words
1 February 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
13
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Couriers and hulking warehouses are to China's $600bn ecommerce sector what shovels were to the gold rush: the crucial element of a rapidly growing industry.

What China's logistics sector lacks in glamour it makes up for in sheer heft. The mainland industry is worth some $2.2tn, out of $9tn globally, according to logistics consultancy Armstrong & Associates.

Of that, express - effectively ecommerce delivery - accounted for $43.5bn last year and is the fastest growing segment, expanding at a compound annual rate of more than 30 per cent.

These numbers have piqued investors' interest , while cheap deliveries have wowed shoppers.

"To some degree Chinese consumers are spoiled," says Duncan Clark, chairman of consultancy BDA. "Because of the low-cost delivery options, the balance has shifted much more towards the consumer in China than it has in the US or Europe."

The average Rmb13 ($1.90) delivery fee, against $5-$10 in the US, means shoppers have no qualms about buying half a dozen jumpers and sending back five, he says. Meantime, the couriers who shuttle parcels around the towns and cities can often be spotted sorting their parcels kerbside, bikes askew.

But such scenes are being erased by the logistics industry .

"It is still changing very fast," says John Song, who leads Deloitte China's logistics and transportation practice.

"At least some of them call themselves tech companies, which means they invest big amounts of money in IT systems, big data, automation and also the last-mile delivery, using, say, drone deliveries and driverless vehicles."

Spearheading that drive is Cainiao, the tech-driven logistics network in which ecommerce giant Alibaba holds a 47 per cent stake.

Rather than an empire of warehouses and fulfilment centres like Amazon or local rival JD.com, Cainiao has assembled a network of 15-16 of the biggest delivery companies and built a data platform that enables speedier and more efficient service by letting couriers bundle deliveries in the same area.

Standardised addresses and mapping allow them to more easily find their destination, and Cainiao now processes seven out of every 10 packages delivered daily in China.

Mr Clark notes that Cainiao was born out of the same frustration with an inefficient national post office that prompted Wells Fargo to launch its own private parcel delivery and banking company during the California gold rush in the mid-19th century.

Wang Wenbin , chief technology officer at Cainiao, says that even with improved IT systems, Chinese couriers are still lagging behind the likes of UPS and FedEx in the US, although by starting later, China is able to leapfrog transitional changes.

China's market is more labour intensive, he says, and has other obstacles: inaccurate zip codes, for example, and in the rapidly growing cities new addresses are appearing all the time. By standardising addresses and using digital labelling codes, Cainiao has cut time, cost and errors in delivery.

Eliminating paper printing in favour of coded eshipping labels saved almost a fifth of the courier's estimated Rmb0.54 operating income per package, Mr Wang reckons. "Last year we probably saved close to Rmb1bn on shipping," he says. While that does not drop down to Cainiao's bottom line - the three-year-old company has yet to turn a profit - industry players and analysts have hailed the collaborative approach.

Jim Tompkins, who heads supply chain consultancy Tompkins International, dubs it "pre-click competition and post-click collaboration ".

By joining hands, Cainiao and traditional couriers have greater scale, he says. "It's really smart because that's the ultimate collaborative play: your stuff and my stuff is delivered together." That means more data, more packages delivered per minute. "So the cost goes down and the speed goes up."

Big data can also help with managing inventory and knowing where to place it, says Mr Wang.

"If we can combine with the upstream selling data [on Alibaba platforms Taobao and Tmall] we can make a pretty good prediction beforehand how many packages, for example, will be going to Shanghai tomorrow," he says. "Eventually, we hope we can do this end-to-end forecasting" for express couriers, warehouses, and those doing supply chain management, he adds.

According to Elinor Leung, analyst at CLSA, numbers show that Amazon's model of owning the logistics assets would not work in China.

Amazon handles about 5.8m daily Investors are piling into couriers, truckers and warehouses, but with mixed results.

Private equity groups including Warburg Pincus are circling the $8bn Singapore-based warehouse operator Global Logistic Properties while Cainiao was among investors ploughing $760m into Huangzhou-based Best Logistics, which runs warehouses & distribution centres.

Last year's biggest US listing was a Chinese express delivery company, ZTO Express, which raised $1.4bn in October. ZTO's fellow couriers SFO Express and YTO Express both listed last year and SF Express is on the verge of completing its own backdoor listing.

Venture capital, too, has been active. Uber-style trucking company Huochebang ("truck gang"), backed by Tencent and the International Finance Corporation, raised funds that valued it at $1.1bn.

ZTO's performance , however, shows that the roads are not all paved with gold. Shares fell 15 per cent on its debut, and three months later are down roughly one-third. Some see the drop as a timely reminder that logistics companies may ride on the coat-tails of ecommerce but do not merit the same valuation multiples.

ZTO trades on a forward price/earnings multiple of 29.5 times, on Bloomberg numbers, or just a shade below Alibaba's 30 times.

orders with a headcount of 230,000, she says; JD.com, which apes the Amazon model, handles 3.5m with about 100,000. Scaling those up to Alibaba 's 33m daily orders would imply 1m employees, she says. Instead, Cainiao has just 1,200.

While Cainiao and its partners can piggyback off ecommerce's growth, challenges remain.

Deloitte's Mr Song says that further consolidation could be on the cards, noting that globally there are only three big players - UPS, FedEx and DHL. Extending the business model into, say, trucking or supply chain management could also prove tough, he reckons.

And competition is robust, says Evan Armstrong, president of Armstrong & Associates. Major international players "have spent significant sums building up their domestic China express delivery networks," he says. "We anticipate further investment and consolidation as the market continues its rapid growth."

Revolution Backs IoT Startup Uptake at $2 Billion Valuation; Groupon co-founders try their touch on industrial software player in Chicago

By Yuliya Chernova 
646 words
1 February 2017
12:42
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Uptake Technologies Inc., the new act from Groupon Inc., co-founders Brad Keywell and Eric Lefkofsky, has been valued at $2 billion in a new deal, according to a person familiar with the situation.

New investor "https://www.wsj.com/articles/revolution-growth-looks-beyond-silicon-valley-with-525m-fund-1465943921", a firm started by Steve Case, Donn Davis and Ted Leonsis, is backing the company with $40 million as part of the Series C round. "http://www.revolution.com/"is focused on what it calls "the rise of the rest" companies the ones that are located outside of Silicon Valley.

Uptake sells predictive analytics software that collects and interprets sensor data for clients in mining, rail, energy, aviation, retail, and construction industries. The Chicago company aims to leverage the data to improve safety, efficiency, and productivity of the clients' operations.

"It's about delivering an insight to a person who then takes action," Mr. Keywell said.

"There's a lot of interest and potential here," said Frank Gillett, an analyst at Forrester Research Inc., referring to the industrial Internet-of-Things sector generally. "We believe it will unfold slowly. It requires retrofitting or adding sensors to your assets, requires adopting new technologies and learning new practices," he said.

Uptake, which sells software only, has seen a quick rise. Founded in 2014, the company employs about 700 people, up from 309 at the beginning of 2016, according to Mr. Keywell. It attracted such hires as Sonny Garg, former chief information officer at Exelon, as the head of its energy team, and Joseph Bradley, a former vice president of the Internet of Everything practice at Cisco, as Uptake's head of the business ventures team.

Uptake's valuation almost doubled from October 2015, when it was $1.1 billion. The company's revenue run-rate is now more than $100 million a year, the person said. Uptake expects to raise more capital in the latest round, said Mr. Keywell, the company's chief executive.

Mr. Leonsis, who's joining Uptake's board, has known Mr. Keywell and Mr. Lefkofsky, for many years. He was, before Revolution Growth, on the boards of their previous ventures, MediaBank (now MediaOcean) and Groupon. He also knew Ray Lane, another director on Uptake's board, who is a partner emeritus at Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers and former president and CEO at Oracle. Mr. Lane had "https://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFVW00120151027ebarnqw28", as part of GreatPoint Ventures.

"We got invited in," Mr. Leonsis said of the way they deal came about.

In October he said he got Mr. Keywell tickets to a Green Day concert at the 930 Club in Washington, where Mr. Leonsis is based. They ended up getting dinner and catching up before the concert, Mr. Leonsis said. Soon thereafter Mr. Keywell called him about investing in Uptake. Revolution invested out of its $525 million Revolution Growth III fund.

The company's other backers include Lightbank, a fund headed by Mr. Lefkofsky, New Enterprise Associates, and Caterpillar, which is also a client. Last year Caterpillar opened a Chicago office, in part "http://www.marketwatch.com/story/downsizing-caterpillar-opens-first-chicago-office-as-analytics-focus-grows-2016-10-22", according to a MarketWatch report.

Large companies such as Cisco, General Electric Co., SAP, Bosch, and others have made a push in creating IoT products, as have numerous startups.

"I expect companies like Uptake to make some hay for a while, before life gets complicated," Mr. Gillett said about the potential of smaller, more nimble companies.

To better understand its clients, Uptake sends its employees to the field.

"We give [our data scientists] steel-toed boots," Mr. Keywell said. "That's part of the uniqueness of being in Chicago."
