Markets
Credit-Card Fraud Keeps Rising, Despite New Security Chips Study; Increase in identity fraud driven by rise in fraudulent online purchases

By AnnaMaria Andriotis and Peter Rudegeair 
807 words
1 February 2017
19:17
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

More consumers became victims of identity fraud last year than at any point in more than a decade despite new security protections implemented by the credit-card industry, a report released Wednesday said.

Some 15.4 million U.S. consumers were victims of identity fraud in 2016, resulting in $16 billion in total losses, according to the report by consulting firm Javelin Strategy & Research and identity-theft-protection firm LifeLock Inc. The number of victims rose 18% from 2015 and was the highest since Javelin, a unit of Greenwich Associates LLC, started tracking the phenomenon in 2003.

The increase in identity fraud, the bulk of which comes from card activity, was driven in part by a 15% rise in cases of fraudulent online purchases, the study noted. That activity led to "existing-card" fraud, which involves criminals counterfeiting debit and credit cards already held by customers, reaching a new peak.

Big increases also occurred in rarer frauds that have been steadily rising in recent years and that are harder for consumers and lenders to detect. The incidence of new-account fraud, for example, in which new accounts are opened in consumers' names without their knowledge or knowledge by the lender, rose 40% to 1.8 million, while the number of cases of consumers having a bank account, credit card or other account taken over improperly increased by over one-fifth to 1.4 million.

The increases happened despite the rollout of tougher security measures around debit and credit cards over the past couple of years. Most major card issuers in the U.S. have replaced consumers' magnetic stripe cards with chip cards, and merchants have increasingly shifted to more secure payment terminals, which combined are supposed to make it harder for consumers' card information to be stolen.

But the findings of the report suggest that swindlers are finding ways around the new measures. "Fraud is kind of like squeezing Jell-O," said Stephen Coggeshall, chief analytics and science officer at LifeLock. "Stop it one place, and it migrates to somewhere else."

With online shopping, there is no way to use the chips on cards. Most of those merchants still rely on the basic card numbers, expiration dates and security codes on the cards. That is largely why "card-not-present" fraud, in which criminals use card information to make transactions online without needing to present the actual card, affected 3.4% of consumers, up from 2.4% in 2015, according to the study.

Card networks, including Visa Inc., say chip cards have helped bring down the counterfeit fraud rate, in which card data are stolen from merchants and used to create fake cards. Visa also said last month that its fraud rate at online merchants was down 1% in September from a year earlier.

In response to the persistence of online card fraud, credit-card networks, banks and technology firms are investing "https://www.wsj.com/articles/tips-on-avoiding-identity-theft-1485975729"of sharing payment details online, such as "tokenization," in which a unique code is generated for each transaction to be approved instead of cardholders' account numbers.

MasterCard Inc. Chief Financial Officer Martina Hund-Mejean said on an earnings call Tuesday that the company is increasing spending in 2017 largely to boost customer and merchant usage of its digital wallet that uses tokenization, known as Masterpass.

Separately, the study showed that nearly half of the instances of card fraud involved chip-enabled card accounts that have been used online or at stores that don't yet have chip-enabled terminals. Some 64% of storefront merchants still only accept cards' magnetic stripe, according to the study.

While other forms of identity fraud are less common than existing-card fraud, swindlers are exploiting them in part because they take longer to detect. With new-account fraud, total losses reached $3.6 billion, up 24% from 2015, even though less than 1% of consumers were affected.

Some 30% of all new-account fraud victims last year had a general-purpose credit card opened in their name, up from 21% in 2015, which often resulted in card issuers giving out chip-enabled cards to swindlers, said Al Pascual, research director and head of fraud and security at Javelin.

Separately, total losses associated with a swindler taking over a consumer's account rose nearly two-thirds to $2.3 billion.

Still, chip usage has helped bring down the amount of losses on existing card fraud to $961 on average per incident in 2016, down 2% from a year prior and down 30% from 2013.

U.S. & CANADA

Buyouts

Acon Investments, Washington

Acon Investments raised at least $965.5 million toward a $1.07 billion goal for Acon Equity Partners IV LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The SEC filing doesn't indicate whether the fund reached a final closing. Closed or not, the vehicle has gathered more capital than its predecessor, which amassed more than $750 million in 2013. Among the latest fund's investors is Oregon State Treasury, according to a pension document.

Alpine Investors, San Francisco

Alpine Investors is looking to raise $500 million for Alpine Investors VI LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The firm didn't record any capital commitments to the fund in the filing. Alpine raised capital in 2014 for the vehicle's predecessor, which collected $406 million. The firm invests in U.S. companies with annual earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization of $5 million to $15 million in the software, business-services and online-services sectors.

Argand Partners, New York

Argand Partners has collected a total of at least $242 million so far across four funds, according to Securities and Exchange Commission filings. Argand Partners Fund LP has gathered at least $206 million, and a co-investment vehicle, Argand Partners Institutional Co-Invest Fund LP, has taken in at least $25 million. Two other vehicles, Argand Partners SEA Fund AI LP and Argand Partners SEA Fund QP LP, have collected a total of at least $11 million, though it isn't clear from the filings how the funds are affiliated with Argand Partners Fund LP. Argand aims to take controlling stakes in midmarket industrial companies with $20 million or more in earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization.

Bison Capital Asset Management, Santa Monica, Calif.

Bison Capital Asset Management collected $377 million for Bison Capital Partners V LP. The vehicle initially was seeking $300 million, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The growth-capital firm's fourth fund gathered $218 million in 2013. Bison invests $10 million or more in lower midmarket companies, targeting businesses in the business-services, health-care services, technology and distribution and logistics sectors with annual revenue of $20 million to $500 million and earnings before interest taxes, depreciation and amortization of $5 million or more.

Brentwood Associates, Los Angeles

Brentwood Associates is aiming to raise $750 million for Brentwood Associates Private Equity VI LP and a related vehicle, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The consumer-focused firm held a final closing for its previous fund at $688 million in 2014, surpassing a $500 million target. ClassWallet.com Inc., which provides a platform through which school systems can disburse and track funds, in January said it received a minority investment from Brentwood through a funding round in which existing investors also participated.

Carousel Capital, Charlotte, N.C.

Carousel Capital hit the $400 million hard cap for its fifth buyout fund to seek investments in companies based in the Southeast U.S. The firm was targeting $300 million for Carousel Capital Partners V LP. Carousel backs businesses with earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization of at least $3 million. The firm initially invests up to $150 million for companies providing services in the business, consumer and health-care sectors. Carousel in 2013 announced the final closing of its fourth fund at $265.5 million.

EIV Capital, Houston

EIV Capital gathered at least $341.7 million so far for EIV Capital Fund III LP, which has a $450 million offering amount, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The energy investor's second fund, EIV Capital Fund II LP, closed in 2015 at $267 million, above its $200 million target. EIV Capital invests $10 million to $60 million in companies with earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization of $3 million to $30 million and enterprise values of $20 million to $200 million. The firm targets the midstream and downstream segments of the energy industry.

Gamut Capital Management, New York

Gamut Capital Management wrapped up its debut fund, Gamut Investment Fund I LP, at $1 billion. The fund hit its hard cap, exceeding a $750 million target. Gamut plans to seek buyouts, corporate carve-outs, strategic partnerships and distressed-for-control deals across a variety of sectors within the middle market. It will invest $50 million to $150 million at a time, though it can scale up its commitments with co-investments. Louisiana State Employees' Retirement System is an investor in the fund. The firm was founded in 2015 by Stan Parker and Jordan Zaken, previously both senior partners at Apollo Global Management LLC.

HGGC, Palo Alto, Calif.

HGGC wrapped up its third buyout vehicle with $1.84 billion in capital commitments after about three months of fundraising. HGGC Fund III LP surpassed its $1.5 billion target and hit its $1.75 billion hard cap with outside commitments. The general partner committed the balance of Fund III. The vehicle collected $1.25 billion from existing investors and $500 million from new backers, many of which are in Asia and the Middle East, said Rich Lawson, the firm's co-founder and chief executive. The latest fund represents a step up from the firm's second fund, which closed on $1.33 billion in 2015.

Hidden Harbor Capital Partners, Fort Lauderdale, Fla.

Hidden Harbor Capital Partners aims to raise $250 million for Hidden Harbor Capital Partners I LP and a parallel fund, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm invests in North American companies generating $50 million to $500 million in annual revenue and up to $25 million in earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization. Hidden Harbor seeks investments in the industrial, transportation and logistics, consumer products, retail and business-services sectors.

Huron Capital Partners, Detroit

Huron Capital Partners is seeking $500 million for its fifth fund, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The offering amount for Huron Fund V LP is the same as the $500 million the firm raised for its fourth fund, which wrapped up in 2013 to seek investments in the lower midmarket sector. Huron targets deals with values of up to $200 million across a range of transaction types.

J.C. Flowers & Co., New York

J.C. Flowers & Co. collected at least $628.1 million so far for its first new fund since the financial crisis, according to a recent filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The filing doesn't list an offering amount for J.C. Flowers IV LP. The firm, which focuses on financial-service businesses, gathered $2.3 billion for J.C. Flowers III LP in 2008 and $7 billion for J.C. Flowers II LP in 2006.

JMC Capital Partners, Boston

JMC Capital Partners held a first and final closing for JMC Platform Fund II at the vehicle's hard cap of $206 million in capital commitments. The fund is the successor to JMC Platform Fund I, which gathered $125 million in capital commitments in 2013. JMC invests in the industrial-product and technology sectors, acquiring companies with annual revenue of $5 million to $35 million.

Kohlberg & Co., Mount Kisco, N.Y.

Kohlberg & Co. held the final closing for its eighth private-equity fund, Kohlberg Investors VIII LP, with $2.2 billion in total commitments. The firm attracted more than 70 investors for the latest fund, which reached its hard cap. Kohlberg invests $50 million to $300 million of equity in North American companies with annual earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization of $25 million to $150 million and enterprise values of $100 million to $1 billion. The firm backs businesses in sectors including industrial manufacturing, consumer products, business services, financial services and health-care services.

LongueVue Capital, Metairie, La.

LongueVue Capital gathered at least $96.2 million so far for LongueVue Capital Partners III LP, which has a $102 million goal, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The lower midmarket investor in 2012 held a final closing on $175 million for LongueVue Capital Partners II LP. LongueVue at the time of the closing said the vehicle would access a portion of its available capital through the U.S. Small Business Administration's Small Business Investment Company program. The firm looks to back companies with annual revenue of $10 million to $100 million and earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization of $2 million to $10 million.

Morgan Stanley Capital Partners, New York

Morgan Stanley Capital Partners, the private-equity arm of investment bank Morgan Stanley, gathered $1.5 billion for North Haven Capital Partners VI LP as well as its related vehicles and committed co-investments. The fund, which targets the middle market, so far has invested $378 million in six investments. Sectors of interest include the consumer, industrial and business-to-business services sectors.

Oak Hill Capital Partners, New York

Oak Hill Capital Partners closed on $1.68 billion so far for its latest fund, according to Securities and Exchange Commission filings. The firm initially was seeking $3 billion in total for multiple Oak Hill Capital Partners IV vehicles, according to SEC documents filed in 2015, but lowered its goal to $2 billion, according to documents filed in July. Oak Hill in 2009 collected $3.8 billion for Oak Hill Capital Partners III LP. The firm targets equity investments of $100 million to $300 million in North American companies across four core areas: retail, consumer and distribution; industrials; media and communications; and services.

Olympic Resource Management, Poulsbo, Wash.

Olympic Resource Management, a subsidiary of timberland developer Pope Resources LP, held a final closing for its fourth private-equity fund to invest in commercial timber properties in the Pacific Northwest. ORM Timber Fund IV collected $381 million, $57 million of which is co-investment capital from Pope Resources, a publicly traded limited partnership. The fourth fund's predecessor completed fundraising at $180 million in 2012. Along with its line of private-equity funds, Pope Resources owns Olympic Property Group, which manages land-development projects.

Peak Rock Capital, Austin, Texas

Peak Rock Capital is coming back to market with two new pools of capital, according to Securities and Exchange Commission filings. The firm is seeking $720 million for Peak Rock Capital Fund II LP, the successor to its debut fund, and $180 million for Peak Rock Capital Credit Fund II LP, according to the filings. Peak Rock's first private-equity vehicle closed in 2013 at its $700 million hard cap, beating a $400 million target. California State Teachers' Retirement System committed $50 million to that fund, according to a pension document. Peak Rock backs midmarket companies in North America and Europe with annual revenue of $50 million to $1 billion. It seeks companies in which it can invest $20 million to $150 million of equity and debt in markets including the business-services, consumer, distribution, energy, health-care, food and beverage, industrials, metals and technology sectors.

Rock Hill Capital, Houston

Rock Hill Capital collected $150.6 for its third fund, Rock Hill Capital III LP. The firm initially was seeking $175 million for the vehicle, according to Securities and Exchange Commission filings. Rock Hill held a final closing on $87.7 million for its second fund in 2013. The firm invests in companies in the industrial products and services sectors throughout the Southern and Southeastern U.S. It targets businesses with annual earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization of about $4 million to $10 million and enterprise values of up to $60 million.

Signet Healthcare Partners, New York

Signet Healthcare Partners closed its fourth life-sciences fund, Signet Healthcare Partners IV LP, with $137.2 million of capital commitments. The firm intends to back 10 to 12 companies through the vehicle, typically investing $10 million to $15 million per business, with co-investment from its limited partners. Signet, which held a final closing on $129 million for its third fund in 2011, provides growth capital to health-care companies.

Varsity Healthcare Partners, Los Angeles

Varsity Healthcare Partners reached the $300 million hard cap for its second flagship fund, said people familiar with the situation. The midmarket investor initially set out to raise $250 million for the pool in May and reached its hard cap in less than seven months, one of the people said. The amount collected for Varsity Healthcare Partners II LP and a related vehicle consists only of third-party commitments, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The pool is a step up from the firm's first fund, Varsity Healthcare Partners I LP, which closed in 2014 with $240 million, made up of commitments from family offices, Private Equity Analyst previously reported. The firm expects to make eight to 10 investments from the new vehicle, the person said. Varsity invests in health-care services companies with $5 million to $15 million in earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization. It focuses on multisite health-care providers and businesses that offer outsourced services to providers and payers.

Veritas Capital Fund Management, New York

Veritas Capital Fund Management is aiming to collect $3 billion for its latest fund, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. If the firm collects the amount it is seeking for Veritas Capital Fund VI LP, it will surpass the $1.88 billion it gathered for its previous fund in 2014. That pool had a $1.5 billion target. Limited partners disclosing commitments to Veritas Capital Fund V LP include Arizona State Retirement System, Fire & Police Pension Association of Colorado and Virginia Retirement System. Veritas targets investments in midmarket companies supported largely by a government-related customer base in sectors such as aerospace and defense, communications, education, energy, government services, health care, national security and technology, according to a separate regulatory filing.

Yellow Wood Partners, Boston

Yellow Wood Partners is aiming to collect $360 million for its latest fund, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. No capital commitments for Yellow Wood Capital Partners II LP were recorded in the filing. The firm in 2012 said it raised $225 million for its debut fund. Through that fund, the firm said it would invest $10 million to $80 million of equity for control or minority stakes in businesses within the consumer packaged goods industry.

Funds of Funds

Constitution Capital Partners, Andover, Mass.

Constitution Capital Partners banked $755 million for its fourth family of funds focused on the lower midmarket sector in North America. The firm raised $444 million to back funds ranging from $200 million to $1.5 billion in size, and about $311 million for direct investments and co-investments in U.S. companies. According to the firm, the money is divided among fund-of-funds pool Ironsides Partnership Fund IV LP; direct-investment vehicle Ironsides Direct Investment Fund IV LP; and across various affiliated funds and segregated accounts. Constitution for its latest offering added at least 30 new names to its LP base. The Nebraska Investment Council approved a $20 million commitment to the fund of funds in May, according to meeting minutes posted on the council's website. One longtime investor is absent from the limited partner list, however: the Universities Superannuation Scheme, the U.K. pension-fund manager that helped put Constitution in business in 2008 with a $750 million mandate. Constitution has committed about $180 million so far from the new fund of funds to seven midmarket partnerships, said Dan Cahill, a co-founder of the firm. The firm also has backed four co-investment deals with $71 million total from the direct and co-investment pool, Mr. Cahill said.

Twin Bridge Capital Partners, Chicago

Twin Bridge Capital Partners rounded up about $700 million for its fourth fund of funds, said a person familiar with the matter. The final tally for Pacific Street Fund IV LP is roughly 55% larger than the $452 million the firm raised for its previous midmarket fund of funds in 2014. The latest fund attracted commitments from investors including Thrivent Financial for Lutherans, the person said, adding the firm plans to commit about two-thirds of Fund IV's capital to midmarket private-equity funds and the remaining one-third to direct co-investments in midmarket companies. Twin Bridge typically commits $25 million to $35 million at a time to North American private-equity funds ranging from $200 million to $1.5 billion in size or to funds focused on investments in companies with less than $1 billion in enterprise value, with an emphasis on businesses with $500 million or less in enterprise value. The firm generally co-invests $10 million to $20 million at a time.

Mezzanine & Other Debt

Crescent Capital Group, Los Angeles

Crescent Capital Group held a final closing for Crescent Mezzanine Partners VII LP with $4.6 billion in investor commitments, exceeding a $3 billion target. The vehicle so far has deployed about $900 million in total across nine transactions. Crescent Capital collected $3.4 billion in investor commitments for the vehicle's predecessor, which closed in 2013. Through its mezzanine strategy, the credit-focused firm seeks to invest in companies controlled by private-equity sponsors with enterprise values of more than $300 million.

CRG, Houston

CRG hit its $1.25 billion hard cap for CRG Partners III LP, which surpassed a $1 billion target and, including debt financing, is expected to have about $2 billion to spend. The firm's previous fund, Capital Royalty Partners II LP, closed with equity commitments of $805 million in 2013. CRG makes structured debt and equity investments of $20 million to $300 million in public and private commercial-stage health-care companies. The firm so far has committed more than $900 million total from its newest fund in 16 investments. The vehicle attracted investors from North America, Europe, Asia and the Middle East.

HPS Investment Partners, New York

HPS Investment Partners, a subinvestment-grade debt investor, wrapped up Mezzanine Partners III LP at $6.6 billion. The fund closed above its initial $5.5 billion target and exceeded the $4.4 billion its 2013 predecessor raised. The new fund, like its predecessor, will invest mainly in junior debt and equity securities in North American and Western European companies. HPS formerly was a subsidiary of J.P. Morgan Asset Management called Highbridge Principal Strategies. In March 2016, the firm's principals spun the subsidiary out as an independent entity, leaving J.P. Morgan with Highbridge's hedge-fund business.

Prudential Capital Partners, Newark, N.J.

Prudential Capital Partners completed fundraising at $1.8 billion for its fifth mezzanine vehicle, Prudential Capital Partners V LP, which surpassed its $1.4 billion target. It also is more than the $1.15 billion the firm collected for its fourth mezzanine fund, which closed in 2012. The firm expects to invest $10 million to $100 million per deal to fund acquisitions, recapitalizations, growth-capital financing and management-led and sponsored leveraged buyouts for midmarket companies in North America. The fund also has the ability to back companies in the U.K. and Western Europe. Prudential Capital Partners is the midmarket mezzanine fund business sponsored by Prudential Capital Group, the private-debt investment arm of Prudential Financial Inc.'s PGIM. Investment professionals of Prudential Capital Group will co-invest alongside Fund V through a separate $98 million vehicle.

Riverside Co., Cleveland

Riverside Co. collected at least $222.9 million so far toward a $350 million goal for Riverside Strategic Capital Fund I LP and a related vehicle, according to Securities and Exchange Commission filings. Riverside Chief Operating Officer Pam Hendrickson in 2013 told reporters the firm was raising a fund that primarily would provide junior capital to small businesses.

Secondary

Hamilton Lane, Bala Cynwyd, Pa.

Hamilton Lane collected at least $573.3 million so far for its latest secondary fund, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. Private Equity Analyst, citing people familiar with the matter, in 2015 reported the firm was targeting $1.2 billion for Hamilton Lane Secondary Fund IV LP. The amount recorded for the pool is almost two-thirds of the $900 million the firm collected for its third secondary fund, which wrapped up at its hard cap in 2013. Hamilton Lane in 2016 collected $516 million for its latest fund of funds, Hamilton Lane Private Equity Fund IX LP, to target small and midcap vehicles, as well as more than $210 million in limited partner commitments for a credit pool, Hamilton Lane Strategic Opportunities 2016 Fund LP.

Neuberger Berman Group, New York

Neuberger Berman Group sealed $2.5 billion for a fund seeking secondhand stakes in private-equity funds. The firm blew past its $2 billion target for NB Secondary Opportunities Fund IV LP and surpassed the $2 billion the pool's predecessor wrapped up with in 2013. Neuberger's secondary group can do deals as large as $500 million, but its typical transactions are about $20 million in size, and many of the trades involve single-fund stakes. The group expects to deploy about 30% to 40% of the newest fund in deals that involve fund restructurings, manager spinouts and other transactions initiated by general partners to release cash to investors early, said people familiar with the matter. Although the bulk of Neuberger's new fund will go into buyout investments, some 20% of the pool could be placed in other strategies, from energy holdings and credit positions to hedge-fund side pockets.

Venture Capital

Camber Creek, Rockville, Md.

Camber Creek, the corporate venture arm of real-estate holding company Berman Enterprises LP, is aiming to raise $30 million for a new fund, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The firm didn't record any capital commitments for Camber Creek Fund VI LP in the filing.

Cendana Capital, San Francisco

Cendana Capital is aiming to raise $31.5 million for Cendana Investments II LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The firm didn't record any capital commitments for the fund in the filing. Cendana, which primarily makes fund-of-funds investments at the seed stage, attracted $17 million for Cendana Investments LP in 2014 to make direct investments in the startups of its venture funds, Private Equity Analyst previously reported. That fund was seeking $25 million, according to an SEC filing. The firm raised at least $160 million for Cendana Co-Investment Fund LP in 2016, according to another SEC filing.

Core Innovation Capital, Los Angeles

Core Innovation Capital, a financial-technology firm that invests primarily in the San Francisco Bay Area, held a final closing for Core Innovation Capital II LP at more than $75 million, said founder and Managing Partner Arjan Schtte.The firm closed its last fund at $45 million in 2012. Core Innovation makes initial investments of $1 million to $4 million and has backed businesses such as loan provider Oportun Inc., payments company Ripple Labs Inc. and NerdWallet Inc., which helps users choose credit cards.

Correlation Ventures, Palo Alto, Calif.

Correlation Ventures, which uses proprietary analytics to power its co-investment strategy, has closed a second fund, Correlation Ventures II LP, at $200 million. The firm closed its first fund in 2012 and, with its latest fund, has $366 million in assets under management. The firm co-invests in outside venture capital-led rounds in U.S.-based companies across all investment stages and industry segments. The firm tapped its first fund to invest in 121 companies and currently is investing out of its second fund.

Data Collective, Palo Alto, Calif.

Data Collective, a venture investor that primarily backs data-focused startups, raised at least $212 million so far for Data Collective IV LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The fund had gathered $177.5 million as of May 2016, an earlier filing indicated. Its predecessor gathered at least $148.4 million by late 2014, according to an SEC filing. It isn't clear from the filing whether that amount indicated a final closing for the vehicle, which initially was seeking $125 million.

Digitalis Ventures, New York

Digitalis Ventures raised a $100 million inaugural fund aimed at startups addressing health-care problems. The firm raised most of the capital for the vehicle from a large, undisclosed institution, said Geoffrey W. Smith, founder and managing partner of Digitalis. The firm expects to make about 10 to 12 investments through the fund, about 30% of which is expected to be seed deals in which the firm helps launch a company.

Eclipse Ventures, Palo Alto, Calif.

Eclipse Ventures closed on at least $183.8 million so far for Eclipse Fund II LP and at least $125 million so far for Eclipse Continuity Fund I LP, according to Securities and Exchange Commission filings. It isn't clear if the amounts recorded mark final closings. Eclipse spun out Formation 8's hardware fund when the former venture-capital firm disbanded in 2015. The hardware fund, which closed on $125 million that year, was rebranded Eclipse Ventures Fund I LP before Formation 8 announced it wouldn't raise a third fund, Private Equity Analyst previously reported.

Fifth Wall Ventures, Los Angeles

Fifth Wall Ventures scored at least $159.3 million in funding so far for Fifth Wall Ventures LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The fund, which has a $200 million offering amount, is being managed by Brendan Wallace and Brad Greiwe, according to the filing.

Flagship Pioneering, Cambridge, Mass.

Flagship Ventures, which launches and funds life-sciences startups, raised a $285 million fund to back selected holdings and has rebranded itself as Flagship Pioneering. Flagship's most recent family of early-stage funds totaled $585 million and closed in 2015. The firm plans to deploy the new vehicle, Flagship Ventures Opportunities Fund I LP, in growth-phase portfolio companies.

Greenspring Associates, Owings Mills, Md.

Greenspring Associates raised at least $325 million for Greenspring Opportunities IV LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. It isn't clear from the filing whether the fund has held a final closing. The firm initially recorded a $225 million goal for the vehicle and later increased the amount to $275 million, according to separate SEC filings. Greenspring makes venture capital-focused deals through fund-of-funds, secondary and direct investment strategies. Through its direct investment strategy, the firm targets expansion-stage venture-backed companies. The firm in 2014 gathered at least $152.3 million for its third opportunities fund, which initially had a $100 million offering amount that later was increased to $175 million, according to SEC filings.

Hatteras Venture Partners, Durham, N.C.

Hatteras Venture Partners wrapped up its latest fund above the vehicle's $150 million target. Hatteras Venture Partners V LP is the firm's largest fund aimed at seed- and early-stage investments in the life-sciences sector. Hatteras focuses on deals in the Southeast U.S. Clay Thorp, a general partner at Hatteras, said the final total is between $150 million and $175 million. Limited partners for the fifth fund include Malin Corp., University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill and GlaxoSmithKline PLC. The firm in 2013 wrapped up a $125 million fourth fund.

Innova Memphis, Memphis, Tenn.

Innova Memphis raised at least $31.3 million so far for a new agricultural-technology fund with a $45 million offering amount, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The fund, Innova AG Innovation Fund IV LP, is being raised under the Agriculture Department's Rural Business Investment Program, said Innova Memphis Partner Jan Bouten. Innova Memphis backs early-stage companies in the health-care, technology and agricultural-technology sectors through preseed, seed and early-stage rounds. The firm raised at least $20 million in a first close for Innova Fund III LP in 2014. It isn't clear on what amount the fund ultimately closed.

Moderne Ventures, Chicago

Moderne Ventures picked up at least $24.3 million in funding for its debut fund, Moderne Ventures Fund I LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. Moderne through its venture-capital strategy seeks early-stage investments in technology companies looking for opportunities in real estate, mortgage, finance, insurance and home services.

New Enterprise Associates, Menlo Park, Calif.

New Enterprise Associates is looking to raise $3 billion for New Enterprise Associates 16 LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The firm, which didn't record any commitments to the fund in the filing, closed on $2.8 billion for its 15th fund in 2015, along with an opportunity fund totaling $350 million. New Enterprise Associates is a multistage investor that backs health-care and technology companies.

Northwestern Mutual Future Ventures, Milwaukee

Northwestern Mutual Life Insurance Co. launched a venture-capital arm with $50 million to target startups that aim to help consumers achieve financial security. Northwestern Mutual Future Ventures will make investments of $500,000 to $3 million.

PivotNorth Capital, Menlo Park, Calif.

Micro venture-capital firm PivotNorth Capital gathered $35 million for its third fund, which will invest in seed-stage software startups. PivotNorth Fund III LP was oversubscribed, said Michael Kim, managing partner at Cendana Capital, a major investor in PivotNorth. The firm invests in a dozen companies per fund. PivotNorth raised $35 million for its second fund in 2013, Private Equity Analyst previously reported.

Rembrandt Venture Partners, San Francisco

Rembrandt Venture Partners is seeking $200 million for Rembrandt Partners IV LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. Although it isn't clear how much Rembrandt ultimately raised for its third fund, the firm collected at least $45.1 million for the vehicle, according to a 2013 SEC document. Rembrandt held a final closing on $160.5 million for its second fund in 2010.

Robotics Hub, Pittsburgh

Robotics Hub is raising a $50 million fund, Robotics Hub Fund 1 LP, and has received at least $2.6 million toward its goal, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. Christopher Moehle, managing director of the Robotics Hub, is a graduate of Carnegie Mellon University, from which the concept for the fund originated.

Samsung NEXT, Mountain View, Calif.

Samsung Electronics Co., through its Samsung NEXT venture-capital arm, launched a $150 million investment fund to support early-stage startups that focus on software and services such as virtual reality, artificial intelligence and Internet of Things technologies. Samsung NEXT, formerly known as Samsung Global Innovation Center, is targeting preseed to Series B investments.

SJF Ventures, Durham, N.C.

SJF Ventures, a firm that backs startups with socially positive impacts, closed its fourth fund, SJF Ventures IV LP, with $125 million. The firm invests $3 million to $10 million per deal in growth-stage companies. Its portfolio includes logistics company Optoro Inc., eggs producer Vital Farms and health startup Solera Health Inc. Last year, NEXTracker, a maker of solar-panel technology for which SJF led a $25 million Series B round, was acquired by Flex Ltd.

SOSV, Princeton, N.J.

SOSV, a venture-capital firm that manages numerous accelerators, raised $150 million for its first fund open to outside investors. SOSV III LP has 65 investors including the International Finance Corp., a member of the World Bank; Lemelson Foundation; and Russell Family Foundation. SOSV previously funded its accelerators with its own capital. The firm has accelerators for investing in companies focused on hardware, life sciences, food, the development of intelligent cities and China startups.

Streamlined Ventures, Palo Alto, Calif.

Streamlined Ventures has set out to raise $40 million for its second fund, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. No commitments were recorded for Streamlined Ventures II LP in the filing. Streamlined focuses on seed-stage investments. The firm in 2014 gathered at least $32.4 million for its first fund, which initially was seeking $25 million, according to SEC filings.

Thayer Ventures, Valencia, Calif.

Thayer Ventures raised a third fund of $100 million to invest within the hospitality industry. The firm backs companies primarily in seed and Series A rounds, said Leland Pillsbury, newly appointed managing director at Thayer.

Versant Venture Management, San Francisco

Versant Venture Management reached its $400 million hard cap for its sixth health-care fund. The amount collected for Versant Venture Capital VI LP is an increase from the $305 million the firm raised for its fifth fund, which wrapped up in 2014. Versant with the sixth fund expects to back 20 to 25 biotechnology companies in the U.S., Canada and Europe. Among the pool's limited partners are Teralys Capital and HarbourVest Partners.

EUROPE

Buyouts

Apax Partners, London

Apax Partners closed its latest fund, Apax IX, at its $9 billion hard cap after spending less than a year in the market. The firm launched the vehicle at the beginning of 2016 and held a $7.9 billion first closing in June, Private Equity Analyst sister publication Private Equity News previously reported. About 44% of the fund's capital came from investors in North America, 23% from Asia, 21% from Europe and 12% from other regions. The fund, which will target investments in the consumer, health-care, technology and telecommunications sectors, so far has made two investments. Apax's previous vehicle, Apax VIII, closed on $7.5 billion in 2013.

HgCapital, London

HgCapital held a first closing on its latest fund after collecting 2 billion ($2.5 billion) from investors. The firm started marketing the fund which is targeting 2 billion with a 2.5 billion hard cap in September, said people familiar with the matter. HgCapital told investors on Dec. 16 it had held the first closing that week and that it was due to close the fund on 2.5 billion in early February. The vehicle, which will back Northern European businesses in the technology, media, communications, services and industrial sectors, is fully allocated, one of the people said. The firm will put in a general-partner commitment of more than 2% of the fund size, the people said. In addition to raising its latest flagship buyout fund, HgCapital also is collecting capital for its next Mercury fund, which will make smaller investments in the telecommunications, media and technology space. That fund will hold a first and final close in early February, the person said. The vehicle is expected to raise more than 500 million.

Palatine Private Equity, Manchester, U.K.

Palatine Private Equity is looking to raise up to 100 million ($121.5 million) for its first impact fund, said a person familiar with the matter. The lower midmarket firm is targeting 75 million. The fund is expected to close on 100 million in the first half of 2017, the person said. Palatine will invest up to 10 million of equity from the fund per company and will deploy the vehicle in conjunction with its flagship buyout fund, which closed on 220 million in 2015.

Funds of Funds

HarbourVest Partners, Boston

HarbourVest Partners is targeting $1 billion for its next international fund of funds. The firm is planning to hold a first closing for HarbourVest International Private Equity Partners VIII LP by the end of March, according to an investor memo. The firm's target is less than the $1.54 billion it raised in 2015 for HarbourVest International Private Equity Partners VII LP. The new vehicle will make fund commitments, buy secondhand stakes and pool money with other investors for deals. It will target investments throughout Europe, the Asia-Pacific region and emerging markets, according to an investor presentation. HarbourVest will seek out funds that aim to consolidate businesses across fragmented markets as well as ride on consumer growth and new technologies.

Mezzanine & Other Debt

Alcentra Group, London

People familiar with Alcentra Group in January said the firm expected to wrap up its latest loan fund with  ¬2.5 billion ($2.63 billion) to  ¬3 billion in investor pledges. No closing has been announced as of press time. The credit firm, part of Bank of New York Mellon Corp.'s investment management arm, pushed past its target of about  ¬2 billion, the people said. Clareant European Direct Lending Fund II LP will focus on senior loans in Europe and will dwarf the  ¬850 million fund Alcentra raised for the strategy in 2014.

Permira, London

Permira is aiming to collect  ¬1 billion ($1.04 billion) for its third private-debt fund, said people familiar with the matter. Permira's debt funds lend to European companies that struggle to obtain capital for expansion plans. The firm in 2015 held a final closing on  ¬800 million for Permira Credit Solutions II, which had a total investment capacity of  ¬1.1 billion, including leverage available to the direct lending vehicle.

Venture Capital

Alven Capital, Paris

Alven Capital held a first and final closing for its fifth fund on  ¬250 million ($268.5 million) to back early-stage businesses. About 80% of the capital for Alven Capital V came from European institutional investors, with the rest coming from family offices and entrepreneurs in Europe, North America and Asia. The firm, which has invested in more than 100 companies, primarily in France's technology sector, closed the fund's predecessor with  ¬120 million in 2013.

Serena Capital, Paris

Serena Capital received commitments of  ¬80 million ($84.9 million) for its third investment fund, which will focus on big data and artificial intelligence. The pool, Serena Data Ventures, will invest  ¬500,000 to  ¬3 million in first financing rounds. The firm held a first closing for its second fund in late 2013 and ultimately wrapped up the vehicle at  ¬130 million.

Actis held a series of rolling closes on its fourth energy fund, bringing the amount raised for the vehicle to nearly $2 billion, said a person familiar with the matter. Private Equity Analyst sister publication Private Equity News previously reported the fund was seeking that amount. Actis Energy 4 LP will invest in electricity generation and distribution businesses in Latin America, Africa and Asia. The emerging-markets firm counts among the vehicle's investors the Employees Retirement System of Texas and New York City Police Pension Fund, according to a Companies House filing. The fund's predecessor closed at $1.15 billion in 2013.

L Catterton so far has collected about $303 million for L Catterton Latin America II LP, which has a $500 million hard cap, said a person familiar with the matter. Through its Latin America platform, consumer-focused L Catterton typically invests $40 million to $75 million in midmarket companies operating in the region. The firm also is in talks with investors to raise a  ¬500 million ($531.2 million) buyout fund targeting European companies in the consumer sector, said a person familiar with the matter.

Axiom Asia Private Capital, Singapore

Axiom Asia Private Capital raised $1.03 billion for its fourth fund of funds. Axiom Asia IV LP exceeded a $750 million target. Axiom closed its third fund of funds in 2012 with about $1.15 billion. Limited partners in the new fund include the Michigan Department of Treasury Bureau of Investments and Montana Board of Investments, according to public disclosures. As with its previous funds of funds, Axiom plans to commit the latest vehicle across a portfolio of about 20 to 25 partnerships with an emphasis on funds investing in greater China, according to the firm's managing partners. Axiom favors midmarket funds, typically ones of less than $1 billion in size. In addition to backing funds, Axiom also participates in direct deals and co-investments, typically investing $3 million to $20 million at a time.

MARKETS - BACK SECTION
LME volumes plunge over Chinese new year; Commodities

By Henry Sanderson and Neil Hume 
489 words
31 January 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
22
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

For the London Metal Exchange, the traditional winter holiday lull in activity which affects most other financial markets comes almost a month later.

Traders say that the influence of China in copper, aluminium and nickel markets means their operations almost grind to a halt during the Chinese new year, heralding the year of the rooster.

The slowdown was clearly visible yesterday, with turnover in benchmark 3-month copper futures falling to just 8 per cent of the daily average trading volume of the past year.

It is a further illustration of how the centre of the global commodity markets has slowly shifted east during a decade of economic growth that has transformed China into the largest importer of almost every leading commodity.

Over the past year, Chinese speculators and funds have piled into commodities , through domestic bourses such as Shanghai Futures Exchange, the Dalian Commodities Exchange, and also the LME.

As trading volumes in China have soared, it has created opportunities for arbitrage with global markets due to the country's strict capital controls.

Raw materials have other attractions for Chinese investors. They are a way of betting on its economy and a hedge against a stronger US dollar, a theme at the fore since Donald Trump's win.

"With a certain amount of inevitability the markets have almost completely stalled," said Malcolm Freeman, a director at Kingdom Futures.

One trader said the fall in volumes was partly selfreinforcing, with brokerages reluctant to take big positions or provide liquidity in the absence of Chinese investors in the "Spring Festival". As a result, some London traders were taking a holiday during Chinese new year.

Another factor weighing on activity, according to traders, is the lack of arbitrage opportunities with the Chinese markets shut. On top of that, the lunar new year has fallen just after the "third Wednesday" contract roll, which also typically sees lower volumes on the LME.

Guy Wolf, global head of market analytics at broker Marex Spectron, said it was not unusual to see LME volumes dip below 50 per cent of normal on certain days during the Chinese new year.

In 2015 and 2016, the slowdown was relatively mild, with volume declines of 20-25 per cent in copper. But in both those years there was intense scrutiny around the strength of the Chinese economy which stimulated demand, said Mr Wolf.

"This year, the market is much more positive on Chinese local conditions, and that relatively relaxed state is indicating we may see volume declines which are double that of the past two years," he said.

Volumes on copper traded on the LME fell to 4,527 lots yesterday afternoon. That compares with average daily trading volume of 53,555 lots last year. In aluminium, only 6,325 lots traded, compared with an average of 44,534 lots last year.

COMPANIES
JPMorgan to mine customer data; Banks

By Laura Noonan, Investment Banking Correspondent 
473 words
31 January 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
17
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

As Wall Street strives to maintain the momentum of the " Trump trade ", the world's biggest investment bank is taking a cue from Amazon: using customers' spending histories to sell them products or services they might want in future.

JPMorgan Chase, which has the biggest sales and trading businesses in the world, is launching a customer relationship management and analytics system so its sales people can sell better, a person familiar with the project said.

The system works by linking clients' phone calls to profiles of the customers' previous trades and trading behaviours, using the same technology that call centres have employed for decades.

JPMorgan then overlays an analytics programme to help the salesperson suggest trades that would be most suited to the client. "It's a little bit like how Amazon suggests what you might like to buy next," the person said.

The bank declined to comment, but the person said JPMorgan hopes the new system will create a "more tailored and individual client experience", and will ultimately result in clients buying more products and services.

Clients will be able to request that certain information about their trades is known only by the people they deal directly with and not included in the general system.

The bank hopes to eventually host the system on the cloud, an online storage facility, so its sales people across the globe have the same tools and clients get the same service.

The new system is part of a wider technology and innovation investment by JPMorgan, which made almost $21bn in sales and trading revenues in 2016.

That was 15 per cent more than it made a year earlier, as the fallout from the UK's Brexit vote and then Donald Trump 's surprise election as US president drove a flurry of trading.

The underlying trend is bleaker; banks have all experienced sharp falls in trading revenues since the financial crisis began in 2008, and have also suffered a steep increase in costs, both in terms of holding extra capital and complying with new regulations.

This has made it essential for banks to find ways to become more efficient and exploit new commercial possibilities. Several banks, including HSBC and Barclays, have tried to bolster their investment banks by getting their corporate and investment banks working more closely together, so that corporate clients get earlier and more targeted exposure to investment bank products.

Initiatives such as JPMorgan's, and moving to electronic platforms for some products such as equities, have helped banks become more efficient.

Lenders are investing significant resources in technology as they seek new efficiencies.

But technology has also empowered a new crop of nimble competitors who offer a no-frills trading service cheaply through online portals and apps.

Additional reporting by Ben McLannahan

Markets
Italy Takes Unlikely Turn as Real Estate Hot Spot; After years of strong demand pushed up commercial real-estate prices in the U.K., Germany and France, investors are looking elsewhere for higher returns

By Art Patnaude 
917 words
31 January 2017
12:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Corrections & Amplifications

The name of Barings Real Estate Advisers was misspelled as Barings Real Estate Advisors in an earlier version of this article. (Jan. 31, 2017)

Sales of commercial real estate in Italy hit a nine-year high in 2016, driven by investors looking outside of Europe's biggest property markets for higher returns.

Property transaction volumes in Italy reached  ¬9.1 billion ($9.7 billion) last year, topping the  ¬8 billion in 2015 and the highest level since the  ¬10.4 billion peak in 2007, according to real-estate services firm CBRE Group Inc. The increased demand has helped boost values of the most sought-after types of property in top cities, like stores in Milan's most fashionable shopping districts.

The size of the market in Italy remains small compared with the rest of Europe. While the Italian economy is the fourth biggest on the Continent, it accounted for just 4% of the  ¬251 billion of property traded in Europe last year, CBRE said. Sweden, a much smaller economy, made up around 7% of the total.

Still, the increasing deal activity in Italy reflects a shift by some investors away from Europe's biggest markets. Following the 2008 financial crisis, investors in Europe first set their sights on the U.K., Germany and France, and later turned to countries like Spain and Ireland.

But after years of strong demand pushing up values in those markets, investors have been looking further afield. For instance, U.K. transaction volumes were nearly halved in 2016 from the year earlier, while deals in Germany fell around 27%, according to deal tracker Real Capital Analytics.

Italy attracted a few U.S. private-equity investors and major global players like Houston-based Hines in the early postcrisis years. But lately they have been joined by others. Last year "marked the return of cross-border and domestic institutional investors," said Jos Tromp, head of EMEA research at CBRE.

Barings Real Estate Advisers, based in Charlotte, N.C., recently made its first two acquisitions in Italy. In December, the firm bought an office building in Milan for  ¬44.35 million. In January it bought the Nuovo Borgo shopping mall in the town of Asti, about 70 miles from Milan, for  ¬51.5 million.

The property in Milan fits into Barings's plans to invest in major European office markets, said Valeria Falcone, head of Italy at the firm. The mall is in the wealthier northern part of the country, where retail has performed well even during periods of economic weakness, Ms. Falcone said.

Italy's higher yields than elsewhere in Europe "of course make it more interesting," Ms. Falcone said.

Yields for prime retail property in Milan averaged 3.25% in the fourth quarter of 2016, compared with 2.25% in central London and 2.85% in Paris, CBRE data show. Milan and the surrounding area has been an especially popular destination for investors, Mr. Tromp at CBRE said.

Investing in Italy "is a way to move up the risk curve and deliver returns promised to investors," said Mahbod Nia, chief executive at NorthStar Realty Europe Corp., a U.S. real-estate investment trust. NorthStar, which focuses on the U.K., Germany and France, sold two properties in Italy last year that it had acquired in earlier portfolio deals.

Strong demand has started to push prices higher and initial returns lower for buyers. Prime office yields in Milan were 3.75% in the fourth quarter of last year, down from 5% in the same period two years earlier, CBRE data show.

Peter Papadakos, an analyst with Green Street Advisors, estimated that yields for top quality property in Italy have fallen 0.25 to 0.5 percentage point in the past year. Falling yields are a sign that prices are rising.

"Even with what we've all read about the Italian banking system, there is availability of credit from Italian banks at spreads that are very reasonable," Mr. Papadakos said. "There's nothing that's indicating the market is freezing. There's nothing that's indicating that volume is drying up."

Indeed, yields of some properties have fallen to the point that some investors are expressing concern about the Italian market overheating. They warn that the low yields could be a risk if interest rates at central banks start to rise.

"When you invest in a trophy building in the center [of Milan] at a 3% yield, that's pretty tight," said Manfredi Catella, chief executive at Italian property firm Coima. "Paradoxically, locations where investors perceive risk to be lowest might not be what they assume."

Investors caution that many of the perennial problems with investing in Italy remain. These include poor transparency, low liquidity, political uncertainty and weak economic growth.

"It's not one of those markets where one can autopilot a deal from abroad," said Riccardo Dallolio, a managing director at Miami-based private-equity firm H.I.G. Capital LLC.

H.I.G. in January bought an office building in a Milan suburb and a shopping mall about 55 miles away. The company didn't disclose what it paid. The firm's average deal size is around  ¬40 million to  ¬50 million, Mr. Dallolio said.

Despite challenges, investors expect demand in Italy to continue to rise this year. "There is a lot of capital chasing yield," Mr. Catella said.

Laura Noonan, Investment Banking Correspondent

Wall Street s biggest bank is taking a cue from Amazon in using customers  spending histories to sell them products or services they might want in future.

JPMorgan Chase, which has the biggest sales and trading businesses in the world, is launching a new Customer Relationship Management and analytics system so that its sales people can sell better, a person familiar with the project said.

While CRM has been common place in other industries for decades, banks have been slow adapters. Their systems are notoriously poorly linked up, traders usually have to manually look up clients and may only then get some of the information the bank holds about that clients  dealings and preferences.

JPMorgan s system works by linking clients  phone calls to profiles of the customers  previous trades and trading behaviours, using the same technology that call centres have employed for decades.

JPMorgan then overlays an analytics programme to help the salesperson suggest trades that would be most suited to the client.  It s a little bit like how Amazon suggests what you might like to buy next,  the person said.

One large US institution said they did not yet have a system like JPMorgan s but it s  a good idea . A person at a large European bank said they were planning to introduce something similar later this year which would identify callers and flash client details on to a screen  with details of meetings, holdings and due diligence on the client.  The information would be subject to controls on who could see and deal with the clients in question.

The bank declined to comment, but the person said JPMorgan hopes the new system will create a  more tailored and individual client experience , and will ultimately result in clients buying more products and services.

Clients will be able to request that certain information about their trades is known only by the people they deal directly with and not included in the general system.

The bank hopes to eventually host the system on  the cloud , an online storage facility, so its sales people across the globe have the same tools and clients get the same service.

The new sales and trading system is part of a wider technology and innovation investment by JPMorgan, which made almost $21bn in sales and trading revenues in 2016.

That was 15 per cent more than JPMorgan s sales and trading revenue a year earlier, as the fallout from Brexit and then "https://www.ft.com/donald-trump" s surprise election as US president drove a flurry of trading.

The underlying trend is bleaker; banks have all experienced sharp falls in trading revenues since the financial crisis began in 2008, and have also suffered a steep increase in costs, both in terms of holding extra capital and the cost of complying with a raft of new regulations.

This has made it essential for banks to find ways to become more efficient and exploit new commercial possibilities. Several banks, including HSBC and Barclays, have tried to bolster their investment banks by getting their corporate and investment banks working more closely together, so that corporate clients get earlier and more targeted exposure to investment bank products.

Technology has been both a friend and foe for traditional banks. Initiatives such as JPMorgan s, and moving to electronified platforms for some products such as equities, have helped banks become more efficient. Lenders are investing significant resources in technology   Goldman Sachs employs more engineers than Facebook   as they seek new efficiencies.

But technology has also empowered a new crop of nimble competitors who offer a no frills trading service cheaply through online portals and apps.

Symphony Technology Group acquired venture capital-backed Fishbowl Inc. with plans to accelerate the marketing company's growth.

Fishbowl has found a new backer following a limited process it started about six months ago as it was looking for investors, said company Chief Executive Dev Ganesan.

With Symphony Technology's support, Fishbowl, of Alexandria, Va., will explore potential acquisitions and focus on expanding its international footprint, Mr. Ganesan added.

Fishbowl is a restaurant industry-focused marketing services company that provides data analytics designed to drive revenue growth. It counts Jamba Inc., Applebee's Restaurants LLC and Buffalo Wild Wings Inc. among its clients.

Fishbowl has raised $25 million in three rounds of venture capital funding led by Edison Partners, according to a Fishbowl spokeswoman. Venture backers of the marketing services provider also included Valhalla Partners and mezzanine investor Escalate Capital Partners.

"Our industry is going through a dramatic transformation," Mr. Ganesan said, adding that the marketing technology industry is searching for new ways to reach consumers in more effective ways as the overall market becomes increasingly digital.

Canaccord Genuity Group Inc. provided financial advice to Fishbowl.

Elsewhere in the sector, MasterCard Inc. purchased Applied Predictive Technologies Inc. from Accel-KKR and Goldman Sachs Group Inc.'s merchant banking division in 2015.

Palo Alto, Calif.-based Symphony Technology targets data analytics-based companies and businesses and software-based companies.

Last year, Symphony Technology sold contract research organization Evidera Inc. to Pharmaceutical Product Development LLC, which is backed by Carlyle Group LP and Hellman & Friedman; and sold AlphaImpactRx Inc. to IMS Health Holdings Inc.

Citadel Securities chief leaves after 5 months

Nicole Bullock in New York 
411 words
28 January 2017
00:01
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Kevin Turner, the chief executive of Citadel Securities, is leaving just five months after the former Microsoft executive joined the group.

Peng Zhao, a 10-year veteran of Citadel Securities, will replace Mr Turner.

As the former chief operating officer of Microsoft, Mr Turner was a high-profile hire for Citadel Securities. When his appointment was announced, Ken Griffin, Citadel s founder and chief executive, called him a  world-class business builder .

On Friday, a spokesperson for Citadel Securities said the departure was  amicable , but declined to elaborate or say where Mr Turner might be going.

Citadel Securities is the market making arm of Citadel, which runs a separate hedge fund business. Mr Turner joined last August as other big, established financial institutions including BlackRock, Bridgewater and Schroders also hired top-level computer scientists to help them build and develop next-generation investment systems that can outperform humans.

 I want to thank Kevin Turner for his leadership and the lasting impact he made on our firm,  Mr Griffin said.

Mr Turner said:  I want to thank Ken Griffin for his friendship and support, and for the opportunity to be a part of one of the most respected financial services companies in the world. I wish him and the entire team continued success. 

Mr Zhao was most recently chief scientist at Citadel, overseeing the group s development of quantitative trading and analytics.

Citadel Securities has been building up market positions in equities and fixed income markets once occupied by banks.

Many banks, hit by more stringent regulatory capital rules, have been focusing on a narrower set of business lines. In their place, nonbank market makers have emerged, using computers to automatically price trades at lightning speed. Chicago-based Citadel Securities has become the largest of a new breed of share traders.

It now executes trades of more than 1bn shares every day in the US, as well as a fifth of the country s options transactions. It is also one of the largest traders of interest rate swaps in the US.

Citadel Securities this month agreed to pay $22.6m with US markets regulators to settle charges it misled customers over the prices they were getting for some of their deals.

The Securities and Exchange Commission said two of the algorithms Citadel used to execute orders on behalf of retail investors had not sought to obtain the best price in the marketplace.
